Chapter (27): Preliminary Information about the Contract. The presence of the sold item in the sale session; so only movable property was eligible for sale. When they permitted the sale of land, it was necessary in the 'mancipatio' contract initially to bring a part of the earth of the land as a symbol of its presence in the contract session like movables. Thus contracts among them were subject to forms of primitive material formalism (1).
B - In the pre-Islamic Arab period, there were sales in which formalism dominated the freedom and will of one of the contracting parties, such as the sale by touching, throwing, and casting a stone. It was customary that if the buyer touched the item or threw a stone on it, or the seller threw it to him, the contract became binding.
6/27 - The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales, and Islamic legislation came through him liberating contracts from foolish restrictions and subjecting them merely to offer and acceptance issued by mutual consent and legal capacity, considering this mutual consent as binding without forms and ceremonies.
So much so that Islamic jurisprudence considered what suffices instead of offer and acceptance in indicating the agreement of the two wills, that agreement which is the basis for considering contracts. Thus it permitted mutual transaction (ta'ati) in sale and the like, and considered the contract concluded thereby (2).
(1) See 'Roman Law' by Monier and Giffard. Professor Muhsin al-Barazi says in his book 'Roman Law' 2/154: 'Ceremonies and forms are an essential pillar in Roman law which is characterized by this trait. Even if some contracts there are concluded by mutual consent, such as sale, lease, and partnership, that is only out of necessity following the expansion of economic life and as an exception to the rule.' Ta'ati is taking possession of the sold item after knowing the price: for example, if a person finds goods in a shop with a price written on them or they are of known price, and he takes them and hands the price to the seller, and the seller accepts it willingly without verbal offer and acceptance, the sale becomes binding between them (cf. F 9/30-10).
(2) From 387

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. [The Islamic legislation] [does not differentiate] between base and precious wealth, nor between movable and immovable property in sale, nor between presence and absence of the sold item. Islamic law is not bound by any formalities that have no role in realizing the meaning of the contract between the parties, unless they affect the intended purpose of the contract, such as requiring possession in a gratuitous contract and mortgage, or if a significant interest depends on them, such as requiring witnesses for the validity of a marriage contract (1).
Islamic law validated the contracts of discerning minors and slaves if their guardians and masters permitted them to trade. Similarly, it equalized men and women in contractual freedom and respect for will in their transactions, whether commutative, gratuitous, obligations, and marriage. No legislation before Islamic legislation is known to have preceded it in the field of liberating contracts from the burdens of their formalities.
:
:
:
(1) The wisdom behind requiring witnesses for the validity of marriage is that social interaction and cohabitation between a man and a woman could be based on suspicious and unlawful grounds if marriage were not announced and known. If it were valid secretly like financial contracts, fornication would not be distinguished from marriage. Ibn Taymiyyah (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his fatwas: 'The legal command to have witnesses and publicity in the marriage contract is only to distinguish it from fornication and to protect chaste women from resembling or being confused with prostitutes. Hence festivities such as beating the duff and holding a banquet are prescribed for marriage to ensure publicity' (Ibn Taymiyyah's Fatawa 3/270-271, abridged). More detail and clarification will follow (cf. F 10/30).
:
388
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal philosophy discussion on the nature of contracts. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, contrasts the Islamic approach to contracts with Roman and pre-Islamic Arab practices. In Roman law, contracts were heavily formalistic—for example, the 'mancipatio' for land sale required a symbolic piece of earth to represent the land's presence. Pre-Islamic Arab sales had similar formalisms like the 'sale by touching' (bay' al-mulamasa) and 'sale by throwing' (bay' al-munabadha), which the Prophet ﷺ prohibited. Islamic legislation liberated contracts from such superstitions, basing them solely on offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) made with mutual consent (taradi) and legal capacity (ahliyya). The Hanafi school, in particular, recognized that the essence of a contract is the agreement of wills; thus, even without verbal offer and acceptance, if the parties' mutual intent is clearly demonstrated—as in 'ta'ati' (mutual transaction via conduct)—the contract is valid. For example, taking an item with a known price and handing over the money, with both parties satisfied, constitutes a valid sale. This reflects the Hanafi position prioritizing intention over form. However, the text notes that formalities are required when they serve a necessary purpose: possession in gratuitous contracts (hiba) and mortgage (rahn) ensures the donor/creditor's intent is finalized, and witnesses in marriage (nikah) distinguish it from fornication (sifah). The Hanafi school also validated contracts of discerning minors and slaves when authorized, and established full equality between men and women in contractual capacity—a significant advancement in legal history. This passage underscores the core Islamic legal principle: the substance of the contract (agreement) outweighs its form, except when form protects a higher objective (maqsad).

Key Terms
عقد ('aqd): A contract is a legal bond between two parties based on their mutual agreement (ijab and qabul) and consent, creating obligations.
إيجاب وقبول (ijab wa qabul): Offer and acceptance; the verbal or conduct-based expression of mutual agreement that forms a contract.
تعاطي (ta'ati): A transaction conducted by mutual action (taking and giving) without verbal offer and acceptance.
شكليات (shakliyyat): Formalities; external procedures or rituals required to validate a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an exception to the Hanafi maxim 'al-asl fi al-uqud al-rida' as stated in the passage?
A) Sale of a house requires a written contract.
B) Marriage requires witnesses and publicity.
C) All contracts require verbal offer and acceptance.
D) Gifts do not require possession.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-asl fi al-uqud al-rida' establishes that mutual consent is the essence of contracts, with formalities required only when they serve a clear legal objective.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Islamic Contract Law: Breaking Free from Formalism – A Comparative Analysis
Comparative legal history of contract formalism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic law, especially the Hanafi school, liberated contracts from the formalism of Roman and pre-Islamic Arab systems, and identify the conditions under which formalities remain required.

Core Idea
Islam replaced ritualistic contract formalities with mutual consent as the sole binding element.

This passage sets Islamic contract law against the backdrop of Roman and Jahili practices, where sales were bound by physical rituals (mancipatio, mulamasa). The Prophet ﷺ swept these away, establishing that a contract is formed by consent alone. In this lesson, we will trace that transformation and see how the Hanafi school took it further by allowing conduct (ta'ati) to replace verbal formulas, while still requiring form when it protects a vital interest (marriage, gifts).

Guided Path
1
Contrast Roman and Jahili formalism
The text describes Roman 'mancipatio' requiring a symbolic lump of earth, and Jahili sales by touch/throw. Identify the common flaw: the contract is binding by a physical act, not by free will.
This highlights the pre-Islamic emphasis on ritual over reason.
2
Identify the Prophetic reform
The Prophet ﷺ forbade those sales, redirecting the basis to 'offer and acceptance with mutual consent.' Note the key phrase: 'إطلاق العقود من القيود السخيفة' (liberating contracts from foolish restrictions).
This is the foundational shift from form to substance.
3
Understand the Hanafi development: ta'ati
The text explains that even offer/acceptance can be dispensed with if conduct (ta'ati) clearly shows agreement. The example: taking goods with known price and paying. This is a further step in removing formalism.
Hanafis rely on 'urf to interpret conduct as consent.
4
Recognize the retained formalities
The passage states that formalities are only required 'إذا كان لها مساس بالغاية المقصودة من العقد' (if they affect the intended purpose). Two examples: possession in gratuitous contracts, witnesses in marriage.
These are not arbitrary; they serve identifiable maqasid.
5
Evaluate the broader impact
The text notes that Islamic law validated contracts of minors/slaves with permission, and equalized men and women. This demonstrates a general principle of expanding contractual freedom.
This supports the ictihad-based flexibility of the Hanafi school.

Islamic Contract Law's Liberality Regarding Formalities
Summarizing Islamic law's flexible approach to contract formalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi/Islamic principle regarding contract formalities and identify its exceptions with their justifications.

Core Idea
Islamic contract law rejects empty formalities — only those serving a critical purpose are required.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm encapsulates a foundational principle of Islamic contract law: the focus on substance over form. Understanding this principle is essential for any mufti or student of fiqh when evaluating modern contracts. The text also highlights key exceptions and the wisdom behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Read the passage and extract the rule that Islamic law does not adhere to formalities that have no role in realizing the meaning of the contract.
2
Note the exceptions
Find the two exceptions mentioned: requiring possession (qabd) in gratuitous contracts and pledge, and requiring witnesses (shuhud) for the validity of marriage.
The exceptions are justified by the principle's own logic: they affect the intended purpose of the contract.
3
Understand the rationale for marriage witnesses
Examine the footnote: witnesses distinguish marriage from illicit intercourse, protect women, and ensure publicity.
The footnote quotes Ibn Taymiyyah and explains why secrecy would lead to confusion with prostitution.
4
Recognize the equal treatment of parties
Note that the passage says Islamic law equalizes men and women, and validates contracts of discerning minors and slaves with permission.
This shows the principle's breadth: it applies across all persons and transactions.
5
Reflect on modern relevance
Consider how this principle applies to modern contracts such as online agreements, where formalities like writing may be unnecessary.
A mufti can use this principle to rule on novel transactions.

The sixth chapter: Theory of contracts and what pertains to them in fiqh. (It will later explain) valuable and valueless properties, and it did not differentiate in sale between movable and immovable, nor between presence and absence of the sold item. And Islamic legislation did not adhere to any of the formalities that have no role in realizing the meaning of the contract between its two parties, unless they affect the intended purpose of the contract, such as requiring possession in a gratuitous contract and pledge, or if a significant interest depends on them, such as requiring witnesses for the validity of a marriage contract. And Islamic legislation validated the contracts of discerning minors and slaves if their guardians and masters permit them to trade. Likewise, it equalized men and women in freedom of contracts and respect for will in their dispositions of exchange, donation, obligations, and marriage. No legislation prior to Islamic legislation is known in the field of liberating contracts from the burdens of their formalities. (Footnote: The wisdom in requiring witnesses for the validity of marriage: the meeting and cohabitation between a man and a woman could be on a suspicious, strange, and unlawful basis if marriage were not announced and known. If it were valid secretly like financial contracts, then illicit intercourse would not be distinguished from marriage. Ibn Taymiyyah said in his fatwas: The legal requirement of witnesses and announcement in marriage is only to distinguish it from illicit intercourse and to protect chaste women from resembling or being confused with prostitutes. Hence, celebrating with the duff and wedding feast is prescribed in marriage to ensure publicity. (Ibn Taymiyyah's Fatawa 3/270-271 abridged.) More detail and clarification will follow (see index 10/30).) 388.

Commentary
This passage states a key principle of Islamic contract law: it is not bound by formalities that do not directly affect the essence or purpose of the contract. The Hanafi school generally follows this approach, as seen in the flexibility of buying and selling contracts. Exceptions are made when formality (like possession in gifts and pledges, or witnesses in marriage) serves a crucial purpose. The footnote explains the wisdom behind requiring witnesses for marriage: to prevent it from being confused with illicit sexual relationships, emphasizing the importance of public announcement.

Key Terms
شكليات (shakiliyyat): Formalities that have no role in realizing the meaning of the contract
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery of the subject matter, required for validity in gratuitous contracts and pledge.
شهود (shuhud): Witnesses, required for the validity of the marriage contract.

Sabab in Hanafi Contract Theory
Defines sabab with examples and distinguishes it from rukn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sabab (cause) in Hanafi legal theory, distinguish it from rukn (pillar), and identify examples of sabab in contracts and torts.

Core Idea
Sabab is an external cause that triggers a legal effect; rukn is an intrinsic pillar of the act itself.

Legal rulings do not arise randomly; they are tied to specific causes identified by the Lawgiver. This lesson introduces the concept of sabab (cause), a foundational tool for understanding how contracts, torts, and other transactions generate their legal effects. You will learn to distinguish a cause from a pillar, a common point of confusion.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the definition of sabab: 'Every event to which the Lawgiver has attached another matter, in terms of existence and non-existence, and it is external to its essence.' Understand that sabab exists outside the legal effect and its presence triggers the effect while its absence prevents it.
Focus on 'external' – cause is not part of the effect's essence.
2
Study the examples
Work through the given examples: destruction causing liability, killing causing retaliation, kinship causing inheritance, co-ownership causing pre-emption. Note that each cause is a distinct event separate from its legal consequence.
Contrast with rukn: for instance, offer and acceptance are pillars of a contract, not causes.
3
Compare sabab and rukn
Sabab resembles rukn in that both are necessary for the effect – without cause, no effect; without pillar, no act. But sabab is external, while rukn is internal (part of the essence). Use the author's test: 'Is the cause part of the definition of the effect?' If not, it's a sabab.
Destruction is not part of the definition of compensation – it's external.
4
Apply the linguistic sign
The author provides a sign: the effect is often attributed to the cause, e.g., 'obligations of the contract' – here the contract is the cause. Practice identifying such attributions in fiqh texts.
This helps in recognizing cause-effect relationships in legal language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل حادث ربط به الشرع أمراً آخر وجوداً وعدماً، وهو خارج عن ماهيته أي ان وجود السبب يستلزم وجود المسبب، كما أن عدم السبب يستلزم عدم المسبب .
مثال ذلك في الأمور المدنية والجزائية : أنَّ اتلاف مال الغير سبب لضمانه وتعويضه ؛ وأنَّ القتل العمد العدواني سبب للقصاص؛ وأن القرابة سبب للإرث؛ وأن الشركة في العقار سبب لاستحقاق الشفعة في حصة الشريك الآخر إذا باعها. وكل عقد ينعقد أو تصرف يباشر هو سبب شرعي لحكمه وآثاره الحقوقية التي رتبها الشرع عليه.
ويلحظ أن السبب يشبه الركن من حيث ارتباط مُسَبِّبه (بصيغة المفعول) به وجوداً وعدماً.
ولكنه يفترق عن الركن من حيث إن سبب الشيء أمر خارجي عنه وليس جزءاً ذاتياً من ماهيته؛ فإنَّ إتلاف مال الغير مثلاً ليس جزءاً من مفهوم الضمان والتعويض، وكذا القرابة ليست جزءاً من حقيقة الإرث، بل هما أمران منفصلان عن ماهية مسبباتهما. وهكذا كل سبب بالنسبة إلى مسببه وهو حكمه الذي ربط به.
ومن علامة السبب أن يضاف إليه غالباً حكمه المسبب عنه ؛ كقولنا : التزامات العقد، وضمان الإتلاف وعقوبة الجريمة وصلاة الصبح، وصيام رمضان إلخ .. فالعقد، والإتلاف والجريمة ودخول وقت الصبح، وشهر رمضان هي أسباب لما أضيف إليها.
Every event to which the Lawgiver has attached another matter, in terms of existence and non-existence, and it is external to its essence, meaning that the existence of the cause necessitates the existence of the effect, just as the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect. An example of that in civil and penal matters: the destruction of another's property is a cause for its guarantee and compensation; intentional unjust killing is a cause for retaliation; kinship is a cause for inheritance; and co-ownership in real estate is a cause for the entitlement of pre-emption in the share of the co-owner if he sells it. Every contract that is concluded or transaction that is undertaken is a legal cause for its ruling and legal effects that the Lawgiver has attached to it. It is noted that the cause resembles the pillar (rukn) in terms of the connection of its effect to it in existence and non-existence. But it differs from the pillar in that the cause of something is external to it and not an essential part of its essence; for example, the destruction of another's property is not part of the concept of guarantee and compensation, nor is kinship part of the reality of inheritance; rather they are two matters separate from the essence of their effects. Thus every cause in relation to its effect, which is the ruling attached to it. And from the sign of the cause is that its resulting ruling is often attributed to it, as when we say: obligations of the contract, guarantee of destruction, punishment of the crime, the dawn prayer, fasting of Ramadan, etc. So the contract, destruction, crime, entry of dawn time, and the month of Ramadan are causes for what is attributed to them.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sabab' (cause) in Islamic legal theory. The author begins by giving the technical definition: a cause is an external event that the Lawgiver has linked to another matter (the effect) such that the existence of the cause necessitates the existence of the effect, and the absence of the cause necessitates the absence of the effect. He then provides examples from civil and penal law: destruction of property causes liability, intentional killing causes retaliation, kinship causes inheritance, and co-ownership causes pre-emption rights. He also notes that every contract or transaction is a cause for its legal effects. The author then compares cause to 'rukn' (pillar), noting that both are linked to their effects in existence and non-existence, but they differ in that a cause is external to the effect while a pillar is an intrinsic part of it. Finally, he gives a linguistic sign: the effect is often attributed to the cause, e.g., 'obligations of the contract' implies the contract is the cause. This passage is foundational for understanding how legal effects are generated in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): An external event to which the Lawgiver has attached a legal effect, such that the existence of the cause necessitates the existence of the effect and its non-existence necessitates the non-existence of the effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The act of praying
B) The destruction of another's property
C) The intention (niyyah) in worship
D) The contract's offer and acceptance

Key Takeaway
Sabab is an external event that triggers a legal ruling; it is not part of the ruling's essence, unlike a rukn.

The Two Senses of Illah (Ratio Legis) in Hanafi Usul
Defines the two meanings of 'illah' with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two definitions of illah (apparent suitable cause vs. legislative wisdom) and explain why the first definition is used for qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Illah is a specific apparent suitable cause for rulings, and also the wisdom behind them; only the first is reliable for qiyas.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the term 'illah', which is central to legal reasoning. It clarifies that illah has two distinct meanings in usul: a technical cause for analogical reasoning, and the broader purpose or wisdom. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying qiyas and for deriving rulings in new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Extract the first definition (apparent suitable cause) and the second (legislative wisdom) from the text.
Note the Arabic phrase 'السبب الظاهر المناسب' for the first definition.
2
Analyze the examples
For each example (intoxication, destruction of property, transgression against life, anger), determine which definition applies and why.
All examples given clearly illustrate the first definition.
3
Distinguish from sabab
Understand that illah is a specific type of sabab with added conditions: being apparent and suitable.
The text states: 'العلة نوع خاص من السبب'.
4
Connect to qiyas
Explain why Hanafi usul requires the illah for qiyas to be apparent and suitable rather than merely the hidden wisdom.
The second definition (wisdom) is too speculative for reliable analogy.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new ruling, propose whether the illah is the apparent cause or the wisdom, justifying your choice.
Practice with examples like riba or gambling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٢٨ ج - العلة : - أما العلة فإنها نوع خاص من السبب، فهي: السبب الظاهر المناسب الذي يبني عليه الشارع الحكم. فالإسكار مثلاً علة في تحريم الخمر والعدوان على مال الغير بالإتلاف علة في تضمين المتلف. والعدوان على النفس المعصومة علة في إيجاب القصاص. واضطراب الفكر عند الغضب علة في نهي النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عن أن يقضي القاضي وهو غضبان. وقد تسمى العلة بهذا المعنى سبباً أيضاً. - وقد تطلق العلة على معنى الحكمة التشريعية في الحكم المشروع أي على المصالح والمفاسد التي تتعلق بها الأوامر والنواهي الشرعية فتكون بمعنى الغاية فالقمع والزجر وصيانة الأنفس هي علل العقوبات بهذا المعنى . ر :) الموافقات (٢٦٥١ .
4/28 c - The Illah: As for the illah, it is a specific type of cause, namely: the apparent suitable cause upon which the Lawgiver bases the ruling. For example, intoxication is an illah for the prohibition of wine, and transgression against another's property by destruction is an illah for imposing liability on the destroyer. And transgression against an inviolable life is an illah for prescribing retaliation. And emotional disturbance due to anger is an illah for the Prophet's (peace be upon him) prohibition of a judge giving a ruling while angry. And the illah in this sense may also be called a cause (sabab). And the illah is also used to mean the legislative wisdom behind a legislated ruling, i.e., the interests and harms that the legal commands and prohibitions pertain to, thus meaning the purpose; so suppression, deterrence, and protection of lives are the illahs (wisdoms) of punishments in this sense. Ref: Al-Muwafaqat (2:651).
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'illah' (ratio legis) in two senses commonly used in Hanafi usul. First, illah is a specific type of sabab (cause): it is the apparent and suitable attribute on which the Lawgiver bases a ruling. Examples: intoxication is the illah for prohibiting wine; destruction of property is the illah for liability; transgression against life is the illah for retaliation; anger is the illah for prohibiting a judge from ruling. This first sense is sometimes also called sabab. Second, illah can refer to the legislative wisdom (hikmah) behind a ruling, i.e., the interests (masalih) or harms (mafasid) that the ruling aims to achieve, such as the purposes of punishment being suppression, deterrence, and protection of lives. The author references al-Muwafaqat of al-Shatibi. This dual usage is important: in qiyas (analogical reasoning), Hanafi jurists typically require illah to be a manifest and suitable attribute, not merely a hidden wisdom.

Key Terms
علة (illah): 1) The apparent suitable cause upon which the Lawgiver bases a ruling. 2) The legislative wisdom behind a ruling, i.e., the purpose or interest.
سبب (sabab): A cause; in usul, a broader category that includes illah as a specific type.
حكمة (hikmah): The legislative wisdom or purpose behind a ruling, such as the interests (masalih) or harms (mafasid) that the ruling aims to achieve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'illah' in the sense of the apparent suitable cause given in the passage?
A) Kinship as a cause for inheritance
B) Intoxication as a cause for prohibiting wine
C) The contract as a cause for obligations
D) The dawn time as a cause for prayer

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, illah has two meanings: the apparent suitable cause (for qiyas) and the legislative wisdom; confusing them leads to flawed legal reasoning.

The Linguistic Origin of Shart: From Signs to Legal Conditions
Linguistic derivation of 'shart'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the linguistic evolution of the term 'shart' from its root meaning of 'distinctive sign' to its application in contractual stipulations.

Core Idea
The word 'shart' originates from a sign that distinguishes — from the signs of the Hour to the stipulations in a contract.

The term 'shart' (condition) is fundamental in Islamic legal theory. Before delving into its technical meaning, this lesson explores its linguistic roots. Understanding that 'shart' originally meant a distinguishing sign helps us appreciate why jurists chose this word for conditions — they act as signs that differentiate one contract from another. This linguistic grounding is essential for grasping the legal concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root meaning of 'shart'
According to the passage, the root of 'shart' in Arabic indicates a distinctive sign (علامة دالة مميزة). Recognize that this is the core semantic component.
This is the foundation for all later uses.
2
Examine the derivations given in the text
The passage gives three examples: (1) Ashraq al-Sa'ah (signs of the Hour) as events indicating proximity; (2) Shurtah (police) as they are distinguished by uniforms; (3) Shart in contracts as a stipulation that distinguishes a contract from others by additional rulings.
Each derivation preserves the idea of a distinguishing marker.
3
Connect to the legal usage
Note how the linguistic sense of a distinguishing mark naturally extended to contractual conditions: when parties stipulate a condition, it adds a unique characteristic to their agreement, setting it apart. This continuity explains why the same word is used.
This connection between language and law is a key feature of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط : الشرط بفتح فسكون؛ وجمعه شروط تدل مادته بمختلف صيغها وحركاتها في أصل اللغة على معنى العلامة الدالة المميزة. ومنه قيل : أشراط الساعة، جمع شَرَط (بفتحتين) للحوادث الدالة على قربها . ومنه الشرطي واحد الشرطة (بالضم) لأفراد السلطة المكلفة بضبط النظام والأمن الداخلي، لأنهم يمتازون بعلامات في ألبستهم تميزهم عن سواهم . والشريطة بمعنى الشرط وجمعها شرائط (ر ) المصباح ومفردات الراغب). ومنه أطلق الشرط لغة على ما يشترطه الإنسان في عقوده والتزاماته. على نفسه أو غيره، لأنه كعلامة تميز العقد عن أمثاله بأحكام إضافية تخصه اتفق عليها الطرفان. وقد عم هذا المعنى وشاع في اللغة واستمر وعليه يقع أشباه قولهم في المثل العربي المتقدم: الشرط أملك، عليك أم لك» - أما في اصطلاح الفقهاء والأصوليين فالشرط أو الشريطة هو كل أمر ربط به غيره عدماً لا وجوداً، وهو خارج عن ماهيته. أي أن عدم الشرط يستلزم عدم الأمر المشروط له، أما وجود الشرط فلا يستلزم وجود المشروط. مثال ذلك أهلية العاقد، فإنها شريطة في كل عقد، ففاقد هذه
The condition: 'al-shart' with a fatha on the shin and sukun on the ra; its plural is 'shurut'. Its root in various forms and vowels in the original language indicates the meaning of a distinctive sign. From this is derived 'ashrat al-sa'ah' (signs of the Hour), plural of 'sharat' (with two fathas), for events indicating its proximity. And from this is 'al-shurti' (policeman), one of the 'shurtah' (police), for the individuals of the authority charged with maintaining order and internal security, because they are distinguished by signs on their clothing that set them apart. And 'al-sharitah' means condition, its plural 'shara'it' (ref. al-Misbah and Mufradat al-Raghib). From this, 'shart' was used in language for what a person stipulates in his contracts and obligations, upon himself or others, because it is like a sign that distinguishes the contract from its counterparts by additional rulings specific to it agreed upon by both parties. This meaning became widespread and continued in the language, and it is upon this that sayings like the early Arab proverb 'The condition is more entitled, whether it is for you or against you' are based. As for the terminology of jurists and usul scholars, 'al-shart' or 'al-sharitah' is every matter to which something else is attached in terms of non-existence only, not existence, and it is external to its essence. That is, the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned matter, while the existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. An example of that is the capacity of the contracting party, for it is a condition in every contract, so the one lacking this...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage defines 'shart' (condition) from both linguistic and terminological perspectives. Linguistically, the root indicates a distinctive sign, as seen in 'Ashraq al-Sa'ah' (signs of the Hour), police officers (shurtah) marked by uniforms, and stipulations in contracts. The customary usage refers to contractual stipulations. The terminological definition is key: a condition is an external requirement whose absence necessitates the absence of the conditioned thing, but its presence does not necessitate the presence of the conditioned. For example, the capacity (ahliyyah) of a contracting party is a condition; if it is absent, the contract is invalid, but its presence alone does not ensure validity (other factors are needed). This distinguishes shart from sabab (cause), where existence necessitates existence. The example is cut off, but the definition is complete. This is a standard Hanafi definition used across all chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): Linguistically: a distinguishing sign. Terminologically: an external matter to which something else is attached in terms of non-existence only (its absence necessitates absence of the conditioned, but its presence does not necessitate presence).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pillars of prayer
B) The police force (shurtah)
C) The obligation of zakat
D) The prohibition of interest

Key Takeaway
The linguistic root of 'shart' as a distinctive sign provides the foundation for its legal usage, emphasizing its external and marking function.

The Terminological Definition of Shart: An External Necessary Condition
Terminological definition of shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the terminological definition of 'shart' and distinguish it from 'sabab' using the formula 'absence necessitates absence, presence does not necessitate presence'.

Core Idea
A shart is an external requirement: its absence voids the conditioned, but its presence alone is not enough.

Now we move from language to law. The terminological definition of 'shart' in Hanafi usul is precise: it is an external matter to which something else is attached only in terms of non-existence. This means that if the condition is missing, the conditioned is also missing; but if the condition is present, the conditioned may or may not be present. This lesson dissects this definition and clarifies the critical distinction between a condition (shart) and a cause (sabab).

Guided Path
1
State the terminological definition
The passage defines shart as 'كل أمر ربط به غيره عدماً لا وجوداً، وهو خارج عن ماهيته'. Memorize this formula: the condition is external, and its relationship to the conditioned is only in terms of non-existence.
This is the core of the Hanafi concept.
2
Break down the formula
The formula has two parts: (1) The non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned. (2) The existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Understand the logical implication: condition is necessary but not sufficient.
Contrast with sabab, where existence necessitates existence (e.g., sunrise necessitates prayer time).
3
Apply with the example of capacity
The passage begins an example: 'أهلية العاقد' (capacity of the contracting party) is a shart in every contract. If a person lacks capacity (e.g., insane), the contract is void (condition absent → conditioned absent). But if capacity is present, the contract may still be invalid due to other missing conditions (condition present does not guarantee presence of conditioned).
This example illustrates the definition in practice.

الشرط : الشرط بفتح فسكون؛ وجمعه شروط تدل مادته بمختلف صيغها وحركاتها في أصل اللغة على معنى العلامة الدالة المميزة. ومنه قيل : أشراط الساعة، جمع شَرَط (بفتحتين) للحوادث الدالة على قربها . ومنه الشرطي واحد الشرطة (بالضم) لأفراد السلطة المكلفة بضبط النظام والأمن الداخلي، لأنهم يمتازون بعلامات في ألبستهم تميزهم عن سواهم . والشريطة بمعنى الشرط وجمعها شرائط (ر ) المصباح ومفردات الراغب). ومنه أطلق الشرط لغة على ما يشترطه الإنسان في عقوده والتزاماته. على نفسه أو غيره، لأنه كعلامة تميز العقد عن أمثاله بأحكام إضافية تخصه اتفق عليها الطرفان. وقد عم هذا المعنى وشاع في اللغة واستمر وعليه يقع أشباه قولهم في المثل العربي المتقدم: الشرط أملك، عليك أم لك» - أما في اصطلاح الفقهاء والأصوليين فالشرط أو الشريطة هو كل أمر ربط به غيره عدماً لا وجوداً، وهو خارج عن ماهيته. أي أن عدم الشرط يستلزم عدم الأمر المشروط له، أما وجود الشرط فلا يستلزم وجود المشروط. مثال ذلك أهلية العاقد، فإنها شريطة في كل عقد، ففاقد هذه
The condition: 'al-shart' with a fatha on the shin and sukun on the ra; its plural is 'shurut'. Its root in various forms and vowels in the original language indicates the meaning of a distinctive sign. From this is derived 'ashrat al-sa'ah' (signs of the Hour), plural of 'sharat' (with two fathas), for events indicating its proximity. And from this is 'al-shurti' (policeman), one of the 'shurtah' (police), for the individuals of the authority charged with maintaining order and internal security, because they are distinguished by signs on their clothing that set them apart. And 'al-sharitah' means condition, its plural 'shara'it' (ref. al-Misbah and Mufradat al-Raghib). From this, 'shart' was used in language for what a person stipulates in his contracts and obligations, upon himself or others, because it is like a sign that distinguishes the contract from its counterparts by additional rulings specific to it agreed upon by both parties. This meaning became widespread and continued in the language, and it is upon this that sayings like the early Arab proverb 'The condition is more entitled, whether it is for you or against you' are based. As for the terminology of jurists and usul scholars, 'al-shart' or 'al-sharitah' is every matter to which something else is attached in terms of non-existence only, not existence, and it is external to its essence. That is, the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned matter, while the existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. An example of that is the capacity of the contracting party, for it is a condition in every contract, so the one lacking this...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage defines 'shart' (condition) from both linguistic and terminological perspectives. Linguistically, the root indicates a distinctive sign, as seen in 'Ashraq al-Sa'ah' (signs of the Hour), police officers (shurtah) marked by uniforms, and stipulations in contracts. The customary usage refers to contractual stipulations. The terminological definition is key: a condition is an external requirement whose absence necessitates the absence of the conditioned thing, but its presence does not necessitate the presence of the conditioned. For example, the capacity (ahliyyah) of a contracting party is a condition; if it is absent, the contract is invalid, but its presence alone does not ensure validity (other factors are needed). This distinguishes shart from sabab (cause), where existence necessitates existence. The example is cut off, but the definition is complete. This is a standard Hanafi definition used across all chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): Linguistically: a distinguishing sign. Terminologically: an external matter to which something else is attached in terms of non-existence only (its absence necessitates absence of the conditioned, but its presence does not necessitate presence).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Existence of shart necessitates existence of mashrut
B) Absence of shart necessitates absence of mashrut
C) Shart is an integral part of mashrut
D) Shart and mashrut are synonymous

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of shart as an external matter whose absence voids the conditioned but whose presence does not guarantee it is a cornerstone of legal reasoning.

Definition of Shart and Its Distinction from Sabab
Defining 'shart' and distinguishing it from 'sabab'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'shart' as a legal condition in Hanafi fiqh, distinguish it from 'sabab' (cause), and apply the distinction to analyze contract validity.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) only influences non-existence; its absence invalidates, but its presence does not guarantee the conditioned.

This passage introduces one of the most fundamental distinctions in Hanafi legal theory: the difference between a condition (shart) and a cause (sabab). Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing any legal ruling, especially in contract law. The source defines shart linguistically and technically, and contrasts it with sabab through clear examples from sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic origin of shart
Trace the root meaning of 'sign' (ashrat al-sa'ah, shurta) and how it applies to contractual stipulations as distinguishing marks.
The passage notes that linguistic usage has persisted into legal usage.
2
Extract the technical definition
From: 'al-shart is every matter upon which something else is dependent in terms of non-existence, not existence, and it is external to its essence.'
Key phrase: 'adaman la wujudan' (non-existence, not existence).
3
Note the external quality of shart
Conditions are not part of the essence of the act (e.g., offer and acceptance for sale) but are external complements.
Examples: capacity of contracting party, suitability of property, deliverability.
4
Distinguish shart from sabab
Sabab's connection is in both existence and non-existence; shart's connection is only in non-existence. Sabab necessarily produces its effect when conditions are met and obstacles absent.
This is the core distinction: shart does not cause existence.
5
Apply the distinction to examples
Analyze the three examples (capacity, owned property, deliverability) to see how each is a condition whose absence invalidates the sale.
Also note the reference to Majalla article 209 and maqasid al-shari'ah.

الفصل (۲۸) ألفاظ اصطلاحية في نظرية العقد

الأهلية كالمجنون مثلاً لا ينعقد عقده .
وكذا قابلية المال المبيع للتمليك، فإنها شريطة لانعقاد البيع، فإذا لم يكن المال المبيع قابلاً للتمليك لا ينعقد البيع، كما في المال الموقوف، فإن بيعه باطل لعدم قابليته للتمليك.
وكذا كون المبيع مقدور التسليم، فلو باع حيواناً شارداً منه لا يقدر على تسليمه فالبيع باطل (م/۲۰۹) .
ففقدان أحد الشروط قد اقتضى عدم انعقاد البيع شرعاً، لكن تحقق هذه الشروط في المال لا يوجب وجود البيع.
- وكل من هذه الشروط هو أمر خارجي عن معنى البيع فأهلية العاقد وقابلية المال ليسا أجزاءً ذاتية في عملية العقد وماهيته، لأن حقيقة العقد إيجاب وقبول ؛ وإنما المال محل له ، والعاقد فاعل.
وبهذه الصفة الخارجية يتشابه الشرط والسبب ولكنهما يفترقان من حيث إن الارتباط بين الشرط ومشروطه إنما هو في العدم، أما الارتباط بين السبب ومسببه فهو في الوجود وفي العدم.
ولذلك كان من المقرّر أنَّ السبب متى وجد وكان مستوفي الشرائط منتفي الموانع لا بد أن ينتج الحكم المسبب عنه؛ بخلاف الشرط فإنَّ وجوده لا يوجب وجود المشروط.
- والشروط بوجه عام إنما هي مكملات للأمور المشروطة لها في نظر الشارع كتكميل الصفة للموصوف، بحيث إن عدمها يخل بالمقاصد الشرعية من الأحكام. (ر )) الموافقات للشاطبي ٢٦٢/١ - ٢٦٤).
فالقدرة على تسليم المبيع مكملة لغاية البيع، لأنه سبب للملكية وغاية الملكية الوصول إلى الانتفاع فعدم إمكان تسليم المبيع يخل بهذه الغاية المشروع لأجلها، فلذا كانت هذه القدرة شرطاً في البيع.
وهكذا نظر التشريع في كل الشرائط التي يشترطها بالنسبة إلى مشروطاتها.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Section 28/5-d: The Condition. The condition (shart) with a fatha on the shīn and sukūn on the rā; its plural is shurūt. Its root, in various forms and vowel movements, in the original language indicates the meaning of a distinguishing sign. From this comes 'ashrāṭ al-sāʿah' (signs of the Hour), plural of sharat (with two fatha) for the events indicating its nearness. From this also comes 'shurtī' (policeman), singular of 'shurṭa' (with ḍamma), for members of the authority tasked with maintaining order and internal security, because they are distinguished by signs in their clothing that differentiate them from others. And 'sharīṭa' meaning condition, plural 'sharāʾiṭ' (see al-Miṣbāḥ and Mufradāt al-Rāghib). From this, the term 'shart' is used linguistically for what a person stipulates in his contracts and obligations, upon himself or others, because it is like a sign that distinguishes the contract from its counterparts by additional provisions specific to it agreed upon by both parties. This meaning became general and widespread in the language and persisted, and upon it fall phrases like the advanced Arab proverb: 'The condition is most deserving, whether for you or against you.' As for the terminology of the jurists and uṣūliyyūn, al-sharṭ or al-sharīṭa is every matter upon which something else is dependent in terms of non-existence, not existence, and it is external to its essence. That is, the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned thing, while the existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Example: the capacity of the contracting party, for it is a condition in every contract; the one lacking this capacity, like a madman, for example, his contract is not concluded. Likewise, the suitability of the sold property for ownership is a condition for the validity of the sale; if the sold property is not suitable for ownership, the sale is not concluded, as in the case of endowed property (waqf), for its sale is invalid due to its unsuitability for ownership. Likewise, the sold item must be deliverable; if someone sells an animal that is stray and he cannot deliver it, the sale is invalid (Majalla article 209). So the absence of one of the conditions necessitates the non-conclusion of the sale legally, but the fulfillment of these conditions in the property does not necessitate the existence of the sale. Each of these conditions is an external matter to the meaning of sale: the capacity of the contracting party and the suitability of the property are not intrinsic parts of the contract process and its essence, because the reality of the contract is offer and acceptance; rather, the property is its subject matter, and the contracting party is the agent. By this external quality, the condition and the cause (sabab) resemble each other, but they differ in that the connection between the condition and its conditioned is only in non-existence, while the connection between the cause and its effect is in both existence and non-existence. Therefore, it is established that when the cause exists and fulfills its conditions and obstacles are absent, it necessarily produces the resultant ruling; unlike the condition, whose existence does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Conditions in general are complements to the conditioned matters in the view of the Lawgiver, like the completion of an attribute for the described thing, such that their absence undermines the legal purposes (maqāṣid) of the rulings. (See al-Muwāfaqāt by al-Shāṭibī, 1/262-264). Thus, the ability to deliver the sold item is a complement to the purpose of sale, because it is a cause for ownership, and the purpose of ownership is access to benefit; the impossibility of delivering the sold item undermines this purpose for which it was legislated, so this ability is a condition in the sale. And thus the legislation considers all the conditions it stipulates with respect to their conditioned matters.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and conceptual clarification. It begins by defining 'shart' (condition) linguistically, tracing its root meaning of a distinguishing sign. It then proceeds to the technical definition in Hanafi jurisprudence: a condition is an external factor whose absence entails the non-existence of the conditioned thing, but whose presence does not guarantee it. This is a key distinction from 'sabab' (cause), which necessarily produces its effect when conditions are met and obstacles absent. The author illustrates with classic Hanafi examples: capacity of the contracting party (ahliyya) is a condition for contract validity; a contract by a madman is void. Similarly, the sold property must be owned by the seller (not endowed property) and must be deliverable. These conditions are external to the essence of the contract (offer and acceptance). The passage also references the Majalla (article 209) and al-Shāṭibī's al-Muwāfaqāt to emphasize that conditions are complements serving the maqāṣid (higher objectives) of the law. This is a critical foundational concept for understanding contract theory in the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
شَرْط (shart): An external matter upon which something else is dependent in terms of non-existence; its absence necessitates the absence of the conditioned, but its presence does not guarantee it.
سَبَب (sabab): A cause whose complete fulfillment (with conditions and absence of obstacles) necessarily produces its legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly distinguishes a condition (shart) from a cause (sabab)?
A) Both condition and cause are internal to the essence of the act.
B) The condition's connection is only in non-existence; the cause's connection is in both existence and non-existence.
C) The condition necessarily produces existence; the cause only ensures non-existence.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between shart and sabab is fundamental: a condition only affects non-existence, while a cause affects both existence and non-existence.

Types of Conditions in Contracts: Legal and Stipulated
Defining and distinguishing legal and stipulated conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a legal condition (shart shar'i) and a stipulated condition (shart ja'li) and apply this distinction to classify conditions in contracts.

Core Idea
Conditions imposed by the Lawgiver differ fundamentally from those created by the contracting parties.

This lesson explores the two basic types of conditions in Islamic contract theory. Understanding the source of a condition—whether from the Lawgiver or from the parties—determines its legal effect and how it interacts with the contract. We will also see how conditions differ from causes (asbab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditions
The jurists divide conditions into shart shar'i (legal condition) and shart ja'li (stipulated condition). Recognize that the source of the condition is the key: one comes from the Lawgiver, the other from the individual's will.
2
Understand the defining feature of each
A legal condition is imposed by the Shari'ah and its fulfillment is necessary for the validity or effect of an act. A stipulated condition is created by the person and suspends a contract or obligation on a future event.
3
Apply the distinction to examples
Apply to examples from the text: capacity of the contracting party (shart shar'i), ability to deliver the sold item (shart shar'i), suspending a guarantee on travel (shart ja'li), suspending divorce on an action (shart ja'li).
4
Distinguish conditions from causes (sabab)
A cause necessarily produces its effect when conditions are met and impediments are absent; a condition does not necessitate the conditioned, it only blocks it when absent. The link between condition and conditioned is only in non-existence, whereas for cause it is in both existence and non-existence.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٦/٢٨ - والفقهاء يقسمون الشرط باعتبار مصدر شرطيته إلى نوعين :
- شرط يفرضه الشرع، فيجعل تحققه لازماً لتحقق أمر آخر ربط به عدماً، بحيث إذا لم يتحقق الشرط لم يتحقق ذلك الأمر، وهذا ما تقدم بيانه وأمثلته في الفقرة السابقة.
- وشرط ينشئه الإنسان بتصرفه وإرادته فيجعل بعض عقوده أو التزاماته معلقة عليه ومرتبطة به، بحيث إنه إذا لم يتحقق ذلك الأمر لا تتحقق تلك العقود والالتزامات.
وذلك كما لو علق الإنسان كفالته بأمر يلائمها فقال لشخص دائن : إن سافر مدينك فلان اليوم، أو إذا لم يعد من سفره اليوم، فأنا كفيل بدينك الذي لك عليه فإن سفر المدين أو عدم عودته من سفره يصبح لثبوت الكفالة على القائل، فلا يعتبر كفيلاً ملتزماً بأداء الدين ما لم يتحقق ذلك الشرط الذي شرطه للكفالة شرطا

Chapter (28) Technical terms in contract theory: Capacity, as in the case of a madman, his contract is not concluded. Also the suitability of the sold property for ownership is a condition for the conclusion of sale; if the sold property is not suitable for ownership, the sale is not concluded, as in the case of waqf property, because its sale is invalid due to its unsuitability for ownership. Also the sold item must be capable of delivery; if someone sells an animal that is out of his reach and he cannot deliver it, the sale is invalid (Article 209). So the absence of one of the conditions entails that the sale is not concluded legally, but the existence of these conditions in the property does not necessitate the existence of the sale. Each of these conditions is external to the meaning of sale; the capacity of the contracting party and the suitability of the property are not essential parts of the process and essence of the contract, because the reality of the contract is offer and acceptance; rather the property is its subject, and the contracting party is the agent. By this external characteristic, the condition (shart) and the cause (sabab) resemble each other, but they differ in that the connection between the condition and its conditioned is only in non-existence, whereas the connection between the cause and its effect is in both existence and non-existence. Therefore, it is established that when the cause exists and fulfills its conditions and is free from impediments, it necessarily produces the effect; unlike the condition, because its existence does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Conditions in general are complements to the conditioned matters in the view of the Lawgiver, like the completion of an attribute for the described thing, so that their absence disrupts the legal purposes of the rulings. (Refer to Al-Muwafaqat by Al-Shatibi, 1/262-264). The ability to deliver the sold item completes the purpose of sale, because sale is a cause for ownership, and the purpose of ownership is to attain benefit; so the impossibility of delivering the sold item disrupts this purpose for which it was legislated; therefore this ability is a condition in sale. And thus legislation views all conditions that it stipulates for their conditioned matters. [PAGE_370] Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh 6/28 - The jurists divide the condition according to the source of its conditionality into two types: A condition imposed by the Lawgiver, which makes its fulfillment necessary for the fulfillment of another matter connected to it in non-existence, such that if the condition is not fulfilled, that matter is not fulfilled; this is what has been explained and exemplified in the previous paragraph. And a condition created by a person through his action and will, making some of his contracts or obligations suspended upon it and connected to it, so that if that matter is not fulfilled, those contracts and obligations are not fulfilled. For example, if a person suspends his guarantee on a suitable matter and says to a creditor: If your debtor travels today, or if he does not return from his trip today, then I am a guarantor for your debt against him; then the travel of the debtor or his failure to return becomes a condition for the establishment of the guarantee on the speaker, so he is not considered a guarantor obligated to pay the debt until that condition which he stipulated for the guarantee is fulfilled. Similarly, if a man suspends the divorce of his wife on an action or omission, saying to her: If you do such and such, or if you do not do such and such today, you are divorced; then her action or its non-occurrence becomes a condition for the occurrence of divorce, so this suspended divorce does not occur until that condition is fulfilled (1). So the first type, which originates from the Lawgiver, is called the legal condition (shart shar'i); and it is the subject of our discussion here. The second type, which originates from the person's will, is called the stipulated condition (shart ja'li). It is called 'ja'li' because the matter that became a condition for the obligation was not (1) It is noted in this context that Article 90 of our Personal Status Law issued in 1373 AH = 1953 AD restricted the validity of suspending divorce on a condition and invalidated any suspension of divorce if its purpose is merely encouragement to do something, prevention from doing something, or confirmation of news. By this restriction, which is in agreement with legal proofs and ijtihads, many family tragedies arising from suspending divorce in an illegitimate manner, foolishly and ignorantly by many men, have been eliminated. (Ref: F 3/18).
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a legal philosophy discussion on the concept of 'shart' (condition) in Islamic contract theory. The author first explains that conditions for a valid contract, such as capacity of the contracting parties, suitability of the subject matter for ownership, and ability to deliver, are external to the essence of the contract (which is offer and acceptance). He distinguishes between 'shart' (condition) and 'sabab' (cause): the connection between condition and conditioned is only in non-existence (i.e., if the condition is absent, the conditioned is absent), whereas the connection between cause and effect is in both existence and non-existence. Conditions are complements to the conditioned matters; their absence disrupts the legal purposes. Then the author classifies conditions into two types: (1) Shart shar'i (legal condition) – imposed by the Lawgiver, such as the capacity of the contracting parties; (2) Shart ja'li (stipulated condition) – created by the parties' own will, such as suspending a guarantee or divorce on a future event. The focus of this discussion is the first type. The passage also notes a modern legal restriction on conditional divorce.

Key Terms
الشرط الشرعي (al-shart al-shar'i): A condition imposed by the Lawgiver that is necessary for the validity of a legal act
الشرط الجعلي (al-shart al-ja'li): A condition created by a person through his will, upon which a contract or obligation is suspended
السبب (al-sabab): A cause that necessarily produces its legal effect when all conditions are met and impediments absent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between a legal condition (shart shar'i) and a stipulated condition (shart ja'li) according to the passage?
A) A legal condition is external to the contract, while a stipulated condition is internal.
B) A legal condition is imposed by the Lawgiver, while a stipulated condition is created by the individual's own will.
C) A legal condition must be written, while a stipulated condition can be oral.
D) A legal condition applies only to sales, while a stipulated condition applies to all contracts.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental distinction between legal and stipulated conditions lies in the source: the Lawgiver vs. the contracting parties.

The Legal Preventer (al-Mani') – Definition and Examples
Defining the concept of preventer in legal theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mani' in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from shart, and apply it to the examples given.

Core Idea
A preventer exists, so the ruling disappears; a condition is absent, so the effect disappears.

In Hanafi legal theory, understanding what blocks a ruling is as important as understanding what triggers it. This lesson introduces al-mani', the legal preventer—a factor whose very presence nullifies a ruling that would otherwise apply. We will dissect its definition, contrast it directly with shart (condition), and walk through concrete examples from sales and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic definition
Begin with the literal meaning of al-mani' as 'that which prevents or hinders' to ground the technical concept.
The word 'mani' is the active participle of mana'a.
2
Identify the technical definition
The usuli definition: 'whatever necessitates, by its existence, the non-existence of something else.' Note that existence of the preventer is the cause of the negation.
This is the opposite of shart, where absence of the condition causes negation.
3
Contrast with shart
Both shart and mani' affect the existence of a ruling, but in opposite ways: shart: absence → nafy; mani': existence → nafy.
Memorize: الشرط يلزم من عدمه عدم، والمانع يلزم من وجوده عدم
4
Parse each example and identify the preventer
For each example (defect in sale, debt on estate, killer heir), locate the preventer and the ruling it blocks. Note the rationale (ḥikmah) given by the author.
The rationale reveals the underlying legal purpose (maqṣid).
5
Apply the definition to a new scenario
Try to generate your own example of a preventer from another fiqh chapter, such as prayer or ḥudūd, and verify it fits the definition.
This trains your ability to transfer the concept across chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷/۲۸ - هـ - المانع :
والمانع هو في اللغة اسم فاعل من المنع، ومعناه: كل ما يمنع سواه ويكون حائلاً دونه .
أما في اصطلاح الفقهاء الأصوليين فالمانع هو: كل ما يستلزم وجوده انتفاء غيره .
وبذلك يكون المانع عكس الشرط، فإنَّ الشرط كما رأينا آنفاً يلزم من عدمه عدم غيره أما المانع فيلزم من وجوده عدم غيره. - وذلك كالعيب القديم في المبيع، والدين على المورث المتوفى وقتل الوارث مورّثه، ومرور الزمان على الحق، أي تقادمه:
فظهور عيب قديم في المبيع يجهله المشتري هو مانع شرعي من لزوم البيع في حق المشتري دفعاً للضرر عنه ؛ فلا يصبح عقد البيع ملزماً له، بل يثبت له الخيار في فسخ البيع ورد المبيع المعيب لأن ظهور هذا العيب مخل بالتراضي الذي هو أساس العقود، فيكون إلزام المشتري بالمبيع معيباً يلحق به ضرراً لا يقتضيه عقده ولم يرض به. ووجود الدين على المورّث المتوفى مانع شرعي من الإرث فيما يعادل الدين من تركته .
وقتل الوارث لمورثه مانع شرعي يحرم به القاتل من الإرث رغم قيام
Section 7/28 - H - The Preventer (al-Mani'): Linguistically, al-mani' is the active participle from al-man', meaning: everything that prevents something else and acts as a barrier before it. In the terminology of the usuli jurists, al-mani' is: everything whose existence necessitates the non-existence of something else. Thus, the preventer is the opposite of the condition; the condition, as we saw earlier, necessitates from its non-existence the non-existence of something else, while the preventer necessitates from its existence the non-existence of something else. Examples include: an old defect in the sold item, a debt upon the deceased testator, the killer heir killing his testator, and the passage of time over a right, i.e., prescription. The appearance of an old defect in the sold item unknown to the buyer is a legal preventer (mani' shar'i) from the bindingness of the sale in favor of the buyer, to repel harm from him; thus the sales contract does not become binding upon him, but he has the option (khiyar) to rescind the sale and return the defective merchandise, because the appearance of this defect disrupts the mutual consent that is the basis of contracts, so forcing the buyer to accept the defective item would cause him harm that his contract did not require and to which he did not consent. The existence of a debt upon the deceased testator is a legal preventer from inheritance to the extent of the debt from his estate. The killer heir killing his testator is a legal preventer that deprives the killer of inheritance despite the existence of
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of al-mani' (preventer). The author explains the linguistic meaning, then the technical definition used by usul al-fiqh scholars: 'whatever necessitates, by its existence, the non-existence of something else.' This is the opposite of a condition (shart), where non-existence of the condition leads to non-existence of the effect. Here, existence of the preventer leads to non-existence of the effect. Examples from various areas of fiqh are given: (1) A hidden defect in a sold item prevents the sale from being binding upon the buyer, giving him the option to return it. (2) Debt on the deceased prevents inheritance until the debt is paid. (3) A killer heir is prevented from inheriting from his victim. The text also mentions prescription (taqadum) as a preventer. The purpose is to show that certain factors can block legal effects that would otherwise occur. The commentary follows the author's order: linguistic definition, technical definition, contrast with condition, then examples with reasoning.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani'): ما يستلزم وجوده انتفاء غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) أن يعلّق الزوج الطلاق على شرط
B) ظهور عيب قديم في المبيع يجهله المشتري
C) أن يتزوج الرجل امرأة بصداق مؤجل
D) أن يسافر المدين فيضمنه الكفيل

Key Takeaway
Al-mani' is a factor whose presence nullifies a legal effect, opposite to shart whose absence nullifies it.

Essential Elements of the Contract (Muqawwimat al-'Aqd)
Defining the four essential elements of a contract and distinguishing them from conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four essential elements (muqawwimat) of a contract and distinguish them from legal conditions (shurut) in Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
A contract exists only when its four muqawwimat are present; conditions only affect validity, not existence.

Before diving into the rulings of specific contracts, one must grasp what makes a contract a contract in the first place. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to contract theory clarifies the conceptual foundation: every contract has four indispensable elements (muqawwimat) without which it cannot be imagined to exist. These are distinct from the conditions (shurut) imposed by the Lawgiver, which are requirements for validity but not for existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing whether a contract is non-existent (ma'dum) or merely invalid (batil/fasid).

Guided Path
1
List the Four Muqawwimat
From the passage, extract the four essential elements: the two contracting parties (al-'aqidan), the subject matter (mahall al-'aqd), the object or purpose (mawdu' al-'aqd), and the pillars (arkan) which are offer and acceptance.
Note that the term 'arkan' here is used broadly; Hanafis technically consider only offer and acceptance as the rukn.
2
Understand the Logical Necessity
Recognize that these four elements are not merely important but logically necessary—their absence means the contract cannot be conceived to exist at all, as opposed to conditions which are separable in thought.
The author stresses that one cannot imagine a contract without a subject matter or parties.
3
Distinguish from Shurut
Legal conditions (shurut) are complementary requirements imposed by the Lawgiver that are conceptually independent of the contract's existence. They include capacity, suitability of subject matter, etc. The contract can be imagined without them, but the Lawgiver made them prerequisites for validity.
The passage uses the example of legal capacity: it is a condition, not an essential element.
4
Apply the Distinction
Given a contract scenario, determine whether a missing attribute is a muqawwimat (making the contract non-existent) or a shart (making it invalid but existent).
This has practical consequences for fatwa: a missing muqawwimat leads to a ruling of 'no contract at all'.
5
Examine the Linguistic Basis
The term 'qiwam' (from which muqawwimat derives) means the pillar by which something stands—reinforcing the idea that these elements are the foundation without which the contract collapses.
The author cites Al-Misbah al-Munir to ground the terminology.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
According to this terminology, we will proceed in our future discussions.
9/28 - Remaining from the general information is the discussion of the classification of contracts. We have preferred to postpone it to the end so that it may be preceded by information on which its proper understanding depends. Now, after the general preliminary information presented, we move to the fundamental discussions in the theory of contract.
Indeed, the discussions of the general theory of contract are divided into three groups: 1- Formation of the contract and related matters; 2- Effects of the contract and related matters; 3- Dissolution of the contract. We will treat these discussions below in this order. We advise the reader to reconsider the detailed outline of the theory of contract that we presented before Chapter 27.

Chapter Twenty-Nine: The Substance of the Contract (1)
Essential Elements of the Contract: The Two Contracting Parties – The Subject Matter of the Contract – The Object of the Contract – The Pillars of the Contract.
/1 - Indeed, the contract, as we have seen, is one of the legal causes, and rulings are attached to it. This is the meaning of our statement: the contract is a source of obligation. And it is, as its definition shows, a joint voluntary act based on mutual consent, linking two sides of persons through its legal rulings, which are the rights and obligations that the contract creates in a specific subject.
The substance of the contract is only constituted by four essential elements that must exist in every contract. They are:
- The two contracting parties, called the two sides of the contract.
(1) The word 'qiwam' linguistically, with fath or kasr, is the pillar by which a thing is established and organized (al-Misbah al-Munir). We have approved its use here in the sense of the fundamental elements without which the existence of the contract cannot be conceived; whether they are a pillar in the technical sense, i.e., an intrinsic part of the process and meaning of the contract, like offer and acceptance, or they are other rational necessities like the two contracting parties and the subject matter.
These essential elements are different from the legal conditions in the contract, because conditions are only complementary requirements imposed by the Lawgiver in those fundamental elements of the contract, such as specific capacity in the contracting party and specific suitability in the subject matter of the contract. Legal conditions are matters in principle whose existence could be imagined without the contract, had the Lawgiver not stipulated their existence and made the contract dependent upon them for legislative purposes he observes. This is unlike those elements that we call essential elements, like the contracting party and the subject matter of the contract, for the existence of the contract cannot be conceived without them.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is part of the introductory framework of contract theory (nazariyyat al-'aqd) in Hanafi fiqh as presented by Mustafa al-Zarqa. The author first postpones the classification of contracts to a later point, emphasizing the need for prior foundational knowledge. He then outlines the three major divisions of contract theory: formation, effects, and dissolution. The core of this section is the identification of four essential elements (muqawwimat) without which no contract can be conceptually imagined: (1) the two contracting parties (al-'aqidan), (2) the subject matter (mahall al-'aqd), (3) the object (mawdu' al-'aqd), and (4) the pillars (arkan), which include offer and acceptance. The author clarifies that these muqawwimat differ from shurut (conditions) imposed by the Lawgiver; conditions are complementary requirements (e.g., legal capacity or specific suitability of the subject matter) that, while necessary for validity, are conceptually separable from the essence of the contract. The muqawwimat, by contrast, are logically inherent to any contract. This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi legal theory: the existence of a contract is tied to its muqawwimat, while its validity depends on fulfilling the shurut. The footnote explains the linguistic meaning of 'qiwam' and justifies its use as a technical term encompassing both intrinsic parts (like offer and acceptance) and other rational necessities (like parties and subject matter).

Key Terms
العاقدان (al-'aqidan): The two parties who enter into a contract, i.e., the offeror and the offeree.
محل العقد (mahall al-'aqd): The subject matter of the contract, i.e., the thing being transacted, such as a commodity in a sale or the work in a hire.
موضوع العقد (mawdu' al-'aqd): The object or purpose of the contract, i.e., the legal effect intended, such as transfer of ownership or permission of use.
أركان العقد (arkan al-'aqd): The constitutive pillars of the contract, primarily the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).
مقومات (muqawwimat): Essential elements of a contract without which its existence cannot be conceived; includes parties, subject matter, object, and pillars.
شرائط شرعية (shurut shar'iyyah): Legal conditions imposed by the Lawgiver (e.g., capacity, suitability of subject matter) that are necessary for the validity but not the existence of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Legal capacity of the contracting party
B) The subject matter of the contract
C) The presence of witnesses
D) A written document

Key Takeaway
A contract exists only when all four muqawwimat are present; conditions (shurut) affect validity, not existence.

The Four Essential Components of a Contract (Muqawwimat al-Aqd)
Defines the four essential components of a contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four muqawwimat of a contract and distinguish them from shara'it (legal conditions) in Hanafi contract theory.

Core Idea
A contract cannot exist without its four essential components; conditions are added by the Lawgiver but not intrinsic.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa lays the foundation for understanding contracts in Islamic law. He defines the four components that constitute the essence of any contract and separates them from the legal conditions that the Lawgiver may impose. This distinction is crucial for any mufti analyzing the validity of contractual arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four muqawwimat
List the four essential components as given: contracting parties (al-'aqidan), subject matter (mahall al-'aqd), object (mawdu' al-'aqd), and pillars (arkan).
These are separate from shara'it.
2
Understand each component
Parties can be single or multiple, principals or agents. Mahall is the specific thing on which the contract operates. Mawdu' is the constant purpose of the contract type. Arkan are the constitutive elements like offer and acceptance.
Hanafi fiqh defines arkan as offer and acceptance only.
3
Distinguish muqawwimat from shara'it
Muqawwimat are necessary by nature; the contract cannot be conceived without them. Shara'it are additional requirements from the Lawgiver; the contract could in principle exist without them but for the Shari'ah stipulation.
Examples of shara'it: capacity, suitability of subject matter.
4
Apply to a concrete example
For a sale contract: parties (buyer, seller), mahall (the goods), mawdu' (transfer of ownership), arkan (offer and acceptance).
If any of these is missing, the contract is void.

وهو كما ظهر من تعريفه عمل إرادي مشترك يقوم على التراضي، ويربط جانبين من الأشخاص بأحكامه الشرعية وهي الحقوق والالتزامات التي يُنشئها العقد في موضوع معين.

(1) قوام الشيء لغة ، بالفتح والكسر ، هو عماده الذي يقوم به وينتظم المصباح المنير . وقد استحسنا استعماله هنا بمعنى المقومات الأساسية التي لا يمكن أن يتصور وجود العقد دونها؛ سواء كانت ركناً بالمعنى الاصطلاحي، أي جزءاً ذاتياً في عملية العقد ومعناه كالإيجاب والقبول، أو كانت من اللوازم العقلية الأخرى كالعاقدين والمحل .
أوصاف يتصور
وهذه المقومات هي غير الشرائط الشرعية في العقد لأن الشرائط إنما هي تكميلية أوجبها الشارع في تلك المقومات الأساسية للعقد، كأهلية خاصة في العاقد، وقابلية خاصة في محل العقد. فالشرائط الشرعية أمور يمكن في الأصل أن وجود العقد دونها لولا أن الشارع اشترط وجودها وجعل العقد متوقفاً عليها لمقاصد تشريعية يلحظها بخلاف تلك العناصر التي سميناها مقوّمات كالعاقد والمحل المتعاقد عليه فإنها لا يمكن تصور وجود العقد دونها .

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
- ومحل العقد، ويسمى : المعقود عليه .
- وموضوع العقد
والأركان، وهي العناصر الذاتية الإنشائية التي يتكوّن منها العقد .
أولاً : العاقدان
٢/٢ - فأما العاقدان فقد يكون كل طرف منهما شخصاً منفرداً أو متعدداً، كما لو تخارج فريق من الورثة مع أحدهم، أي تعاقدوا معه على أن يدفعوا إليه مبلغاً معيناً ويأخذوا حصته الإرثية من التركة فيقتسموها بنسبة ما دفع كل منهم . وقد يكون العاقدان أصيلين أو نائبين عن غيرهما في العقد كالوكيلين والوصيين، وقد يكون أحدهما أصيلاً عن نفسه والآخر وكيلاً عن غيره . ثانياً : محل العقد

ولمحل العقد شرائط شرعية يتوقف على توافرها انعقاده سنراها في شرائط الانعقاد .

وهذا الموضوع واحد ثابت غير مختلف في كل أفراد النوع الواحد من العقود وإنما يختلف باختلاف نوع العقد.

The twenty-ninth chapter: The essence of the contract (1)
Components of the contract: the two contracting parties - the subject matter of the contract - the object of the contract - the pillars of the contract.
/1 - Indeed, the contract, as we have seen, is one of the legal causes upon which rulings follow, and this is the meaning of our saying: the contract is a source of obligation.
And it is, as appeared from its definition, a joint volitional act based on mutual consent, and it links two sides of persons by its legal rulings, which are the rights and obligations that the contract creates in a specific subject matter.
Thus the essence of the contract is only constituted by four essential components that must exist in every contract, and they are:
-
The two contracting parties, who are called the two sides of the contract.
(1) The essence of a thing, linguistically, with fath and kasr, is its pillar by which it stands and is organized [from al-Misbah al-Munir]. And we have preferred to use it here in the sense of the fundamental components without which the existence of the contract cannot be conceived; whether they are a pillar (rukn) in the terminological sense, i.e., an intrinsic part in the process of the contract and its meaning, like offer and acceptance, or they are among other intellectual necessities, like the contracting parties and the subject matter.
These components are other than the legal conditions in the contract because the conditions are merely complementary, which the Lawgiver made obligatory in those fundamental components of the contract, such as specific capacity in the contracting party and specific suitability in the subject matter. Thus the legal conditions are things that, in principle, the contract could exist without them were it not that the Lawgiver stipulated their existence and made the contract dependent on them for legislative purposes He considers, unlike those elements which we have called components, such as the contracting party and the contracted subject matter, for the existence of the contract without them cannot be conceived.
