399
Chapter Six: The theory of contracts and related matters in fiqh
- And the subject matter (mahall) of the contract, which is called: the thing contracted upon.
- And the object (mawdu') of the contract
And the pillars (arkan), which are the intrinsic constitutive elements from which the contract is composed.
First: The two contracting parties
2/2 - As for the two contracting parties, each side may be a single person or multiple persons, as when a group of heirs make a mutual relinquishment (takharij) with one of them, i.e., they contract with him to pay him a certain amount and take his inheritance share from the estate, then they divide it among themselves in proportion to what each paid. And the two contracting parties may be principals or agents for others in the contract, like two agents (wakils) and two executors (wasis), and one of them may be a principal for himself and the other an agent for another.
Second: The subject matter (mahall) of the contract
3/29 - As for the subject matter (mahall) of the contract, or the thing contracted upon, it is that in which the effect and ruling of the contract are established; such as the sold property in a sale contract, the gifted property in a gift contract, the mortgaged property in a mortgage contract, the debt guaranteed in a guarantee contract, the shared enjoyment in a marriage contract, and so on...
The subject matter of the contract has legal conditions which its validity depends on their fulfillment, which we will see in the conditions of validity.
Third: The object (mawdu') of the contract
4/29 - As for the object (mawdu') of the contract, what is meant by it is its specific purpose, i.e., the original objective for which the contract was legislated.
And this object is one, constant, and does not differ among all individuals of the same type of contract; rather it differs according to the type of contract.
:
400
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is from the introductory chapter on the theory of contracts. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the four essential components (muqawwimat) of any contract in Islamic jurisprudence. He begins by clarifying that a contract is a legal cause (sabab) that produces effects, and it is a joint volitional act based on mutual consent. Then he introduces the four components: (1) the two contracting parties (al-'aqidan), (2) the subject matter (mahall al-'aqd), (3) the object (mawdu' al-'aqd), and (4) the pillars (arkan). He emphasizes that these are fundamental: without them the contract cannot be conceived. He distinguishes them from legal conditions (shara'it), which are complementary requirements imposed by the Lawgiver on these components (e.g., capacity for parties, suitability of the subject matter). Conditions could theoretically be absent but for the Shari'ah stipulating them for legislative reasons; whereas components are indispensable by nature. The passage then elaborates on each component: the parties can be single or multiple, principals or agents; the subject matter is the thing on which the contract's effect is realized (e.g., sold item, gifted property); the object is the specific purpose for which the contract type is legislated (e.g., ownership transfer in sale, marriage for companionship); and the pillars are the constitutive elements like offer and acceptance (mentioned in the footnote). The author uses the term 'qiwam' or 'qawam' for essence, taken from Arabic lexicons meaning the pillar that holds something together. This discussion is foundational for understanding contract structure in Hanafi fiqh, as it sets the framework for conditions that will follow.

Key Terms
قوام العقد (qiwam al-'aqd): The essential components (muqawwimat) without which the existence of the contract cannot be conceived, whether they are pillars (arkan) in the terminological sense or other intellectual necessities.
مقومات العقد (muqawwimat al-'aqd): The four fundamental elements: contracting parties, subject matter, object, and pillars.
محل العقد (mahall al-'aqd): The specific thing upon which the contract operates and its effect is established, e.g., the sold goods in a sale.
موضوع العقد (mawdu' al-'aqd): The ultimate purpose or objective for which the contract type was legislated; this is constant for each contract type.
أركان العقد (arkan al-'aqd): The intrinsic constitutive elements of the contract, such as offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).
شرائط العقد (shara'it al-'aqd): Legal conditions that are complementary and imposed by the Lawgiver on the components, e.g., capacity or suitability of the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a key distinction between the 'muqawwimat' (components) of a contract and the 'shara'it' (legal conditions)?
A) Components are optional; conditions are mandatory.
B) Components are necessary for existence by nature; conditions are imposed by the Lawgiver and could potentially be absent without them.
C) Components only apply to sale contracts; conditions apply to all contracts.
D) Components are related to the parties only; conditions relate to the subject matter only.

Key Takeaway
A contract is defined by its four essential components: parties, subject matter, object, and pillars; conditions are external requirements for validity.

Elements of a Contract: Parties, Subject, and Object
Defines the three constitutive elements of a contract and distinguishes object from motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three constitutive elements of a contract (arkan) and distinguish the object (mawdu') from the personal motive (ba'ith) using Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
A contract has three pillars: parties, subject matter, and object; motive is not a pillar.

Contracts are the backbone of Islamic commercial law. To properly analyze any contract, you must first identify its essential building blocks. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the three constitutive elements (arkan) of a contract: the two contracting parties (al-aaqidan), the subject matter (al-mahall), and the object (al-mawdu'). Understanding these elements is crucial for a mufti when determining the validity of a transaction. The passage also clarifies a common confusion by distinguishing the object (the legal purpose of the contract type) from the personal motive (al-baith), which is generally irrelevant unless it involves an explicit prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three pillars
From the passage, list the three constitutive elements: contracting parties, subject matter, and object. Note that these are the arkan of a contract in Hanafi theory.
Remember: pillars are essential; without any one, the contract is invalid.
2
Define the subject matter (mahall)
The mahall is where the contract's effect takes place. For each contract type, identify what the subject matter is (e.g., sold item in sale, usufruct in lease). The passage provides examples.
The subject matter must meet conditions like existence, ownership, deliverability, etc., for validity.
3
Define the object (mawdu')
The object is the specific purpose (ghaya naw'iyya) of the contract type. It is constant for all contracts of the same type. For example, in sale, it is transferring ownership for a consideration.
Do not confuse object with subject matter: subject matter is the thing, object is the legal purpose.
4
Distinguish object from motive
The passage contrasts the object (constant per contract type) with the personal motive (ba'ith), which varies per party. The Hanafi position is that motive generally does not affect validity, though it may be considered in some cases.
This distinction is critical for fatwa: a contract is not invalid merely because of a bad motive, unless it involves a clear prohibition.
5
Apply to a specific contract
Take a sale contract: identify the parties, subject matter (the item), and object (transfer of ownership). Then ask: what might be a possible motive of the seller? Recognize that the motive does not alter the contract type.
This exercise solidifies the theoretical framework.

الفصل (۲۹) قوام العقد
فالموضوع في كل عقد بيع إنما هو نقل ملكية المبيع إلى المشتري بعوض ؛ وفي كل هبة إنما هو تمليك العين الموهوبة مجاناً بلا عوض؛ وفي كل عقد إجارة إنما هو تمليك منافع المأجور بعوض؛ وفي كل عقد إعارة إنما هو تمليك المنافع بلا عوض ؛ وهكذا.
ولذا قلنا: إن موضوع العقد هو الغاية النوعية فيه(۱)
وبذلك تتميز الغاية النوعية عن الباعث الذي هو غاية شخصية للعاقد. فإن الباعث مثلاً لبعض الناس على بيع شيء من ماله قد يكون هو احتياجه إلى الثمن أو كراهيته للمبيع، أو رغبته في الربح؛ وقد يكون الباعث لغيره على البيع هو رغبته في تهريب المال من وجه دائن أو وارث أو غير ذلك .
فالباعث قد يختلف باختلاف كل عاقد، أما الموضوع فإنما يختلف باختلاف نوع العقد كما بيناه (۲) .
المتعة
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أنه في بعض العقود قد يكون محل العقد مشتبهاً ما بين المحل والموضوع ،والعاقد كما في عقد النكاح. فإن محله في الحقيقة هو المشتركة بين الزوجين بغية التناسل أما موضوعه فهو ملك هذه المتعة، أي حق الاستمتاع ولذا يعرفه الفقهاء بأنه عقد موضوع لملك المتعة (ر: الدرر، أول النكاح). وبما أن غرض هذه المتعة التي هي محل العقد إنما هو الاستيلاد لذلك يقولون: إن محل النكاح هو المرأة بهذا الاعتبار، ولكن محله الحقيقي إنما هو المتعة الجنسية المشتركة لا المرأة وإلا كان في النكاح محل العقد والعاقد شيئاً واحداً.
(۲) إن لمشروعية الغرض الباعث على التصرف أو عدم مشروعيته اعتباراً مؤثراً في كثير من أحوال العقود وأحكامها في نظر فقهائنا . وهذا ما استقر عليه الاجتهاد القضائي الحديث في الفقه الفرنسي أيضاً. (ر) نظرية العقد للأستاذ السنهوري ف/٥٤٤-٥٤٨.
أن الأستاذ الدكتور شفيق شحاتة في رسالته عن النظرية العامة للالتزامات في غير الشريعة الإسلامية» (ف/ ٤٨) يقول :
والواقع أن الدافع أو الباعث لم يعتبر أبداً عند الفقهاء، حتى إنهم قالوا: إن البيع يصح، ولو أن الغرض الذي سيستعمل فيه المبيع غير مشروع .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
- The place of the contract, called: the subject matter.
- And the object of the contract.
And the pillars, which are the essential constituent elements of which the contract is composed.
First: The two contracting parties
2/2 - As for the two contracting parties, each party may be a single person or multiple persons, as in the case of a group of heirs making a settlement with one of them, i.e., contracting with him that they pay him a certain amount and take his inherited share of the estate, then dividing it according to what each paid. And the two contracting parties may be principals or agents acting for others, like two agents or two executors; or one may be a principal for himself and the other an agent for someone else.
Second: The subject matter of the contract
3/29 - As for the subject matter of the contract or the thing contracted upon, it is that in which the effect and ruling of the contract are established; such as the sold property in a sale, the gifted property in a gift, the mortgaged property in a mortgage, the guaranteed debt in a guarantee, the shared sexual enjoyment in marriage, and so on.
The subject matter of a contract has legal conditions whose fulfillment is necessary for its validity; we will see them in the conditions of validity.
Third: The object of the contract
4/29 - As for the object of the contract, what is meant is its specific purpose, i.e., the primary objective for which the contract was legislated.
And this object is one and constant, not differing among all individuals of the same type of contract; rather, it differs according to the type of contract.
Chapter (29): The Structure of the Contract
Thus, the object in every sale contract is transferring ownership of the sold item to the buyer for a consideration; in every gift, it's making the gifted item owned gratuitously without consideration; in every lease contract, it's making the usufruct of the leased property owned for a consideration; in every loan for use, it's making the usufruct owned without consideration; and so on.
Hence we said: The object of the contract is its specific purpose (1).
And by this, the specific purpose is distinguished from the motive, which is a personal purpose of the contracting party. For instance, the motive of some people for selling something they own may be their need for the price, or their dislike of the sold item, or their desire for profit; and the motive of others for selling may be their desire to transfer assets away from a creditor, heir, or the like.
Thus, the motive may differ from one contracting party to another, whereas the object differs by the type of contract as we explained (2).
The enjoyment
(1) It is noticed here that in some contracts, the subject matter may be ambiguous between the subject matter and the object and the contracting party, as in the marriage contract. For its subject matter in reality is the shared sexual enjoyment between the spouses for the purpose of procreation, while its object is the ownership of this enjoyment, i.e., the right to enjoy. Therefore, the jurists define it as "a contract whose object is ownership of enjoyment" (see al-Durar, beginning of Nikah). And since the purpose of this enjoyment which is the subject matter is procreation, they say that the subject matter of marriage is the woman in this sense, but its real subject matter is the shared sexual enjoyment, not the woman; otherwise, in marriage the subject matter and the contracting party would be the same thing.
(2) The legality or illegality of the motive behind a transaction is a significant consideration in many cases of contracts and their rulings in the view of our jurists. This has also been settled by modern judicial jurisprudence in French law. (See: Theory of Contract by Professor al-Sanhuri, pp. 544-548).
Professor Dr. Shafiq Shahata says in his thesis on 'The General Theory of Obligations in non-Islamic Shari'a' (p. 48):
“The fact is that the motive or incentive was never considered by the jurists, so much so that they said: A sale is valid even if the purpose for which the sold item will be used is unlawful.”
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of contracts in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically the constitutive elements (arkan) of a contract. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, a contemporary Hanafi scholar, explains that a contract is composed of three essential pillars: the two contracting parties (al-aaqidan), the subject matter (al-mahall or al-ma'qud alayh), and the object (al-mawdu'). The text first defines each element, then distinguishes the object (ghaya naw'iyya) from the motive (ba'ith).

For the contracting parties, they may be individuals or groups, principals or agents. This flexibility is important in Hanafi fiqh, where contracts like kharij (settlement) among heirs are valid.

The subject matter (mahall al-aqd) is where the effect of the contract takes place—e.g., the sold item in a sale, the gifted property in a gift. The author notes that the subject matter has legal conditions for validity, which will be discussed later. In Hanafi law, for instance, the subject matter must exist, be owned, be deliverable, and be known to the parties.

The object (mawdu') is the specific purpose or ultimate goal for which the contract was legislated. It is constant for each type of contract: in sale, it is transferring ownership for a price; in gift, gratuitous transfer; in lease, transferring usufruct for a price; in borrowing, gratuitous use. This is a key distinction: the object is type-specific, while the motive is personal and varies among individuals.

The footnote on the marriage contract is particularly insightful: the subject matter of marriage is the shared sexual enjoyment (al-mut'a al-mushtaraka), while the object is the ownership of that enjoyment. This avoids equating the subject matter with the woman herself, which would conflate the subject matter with one of the contracting parties.

The second footnote discusses the role of motive in contract validity. While Hanafi jurists generally do not invalidate a contract due to an unlawful motive (as confirmed by the quote from Shafiq Shahata), they do consider it in some contexts, such as determining intent. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that contracts are based on apparent expressions, not hidden intentions, except where fraud or evasion is proven.

Key Terms
محل العقد (mahall al-aqd): The subject matter of the contract, the thing in which the effect and ruling of the contract are established.
موضوع العقد (mawdu' al-aqd): The object of the contract, the specific purpose (ghaya naw'iyya) for which the contract type was legislated.
الباعث (al-baith): The personal motive or incentive of a contracting party, which may vary among individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale contract, what is the object (mawdu') according to the passage?
A) The sold item itself.
B) The price paid.
C) Transferring ownership of the sold item to the buyer for a consideration.
D) The personal motive of the seller.

Key Takeaway
The three constitutive pillars of a contract are the parties, subject matter, and object; the object must be distinguished from the personal motive, which generally does not affect validity.

Subject Matter vs. Motive in Hanafi Contract Theory
Distinguishing subject matter from motive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of subject matter (mawḍū‘) and distinguish it from personal motive (bā‘ith), and apply the distinction to classify contract types.

Core Idea
The subject matter defines the contract type; the motive is the party's personal reason.

Contracts are classified by their generic purpose—the subject matter—which remains constant for each type. But each party may have a personal motive driving the contract. In this lesson, we learn to separate these concepts and see how Hanafi fiqh uses this distinction to determine the fundamental nature of a contract, and why mixing them leads to confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter of a contract
Read the passage and note that for each contract type (sale, gift, hire, loan), the subject matter is the generic purpose: transfer of ownership, gratuitous transfer, transfer of usufruct, etc.
The subject matter (mawḍū‘) is synonymous with al-ghāyah al-naw‘iyyah (generic purpose).
2
Contrast subject matter with motive
The passage explains that motive (bā‘ith) is personal and varies, e.g., need for money, dislike of item, profit, hiding assets. It is not part of the contract’s definition.
Motive can be legitimate or illegitimate, but that does not change the contract type.
3
Apply the distinction to a mixed case: marriage
The passage notes that in marriage, the object (maḥall) is the woman, but the subject matter is ownership of mut‘ah (sexual enjoyment). The motive (procreation) is personal.
This clarifies that the woman is not the subject matter; the subject matter is the right to enjoy.
4
Understand the consequence of the distinction
Knowing the subject matter allows a mufti to correctly classify a contract. The motive then becomes relevant for rulings like rescission, not for basic validity.
The distinction is foundational for applying the maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' correctly.

الفصل (۲۹) قوام العقد
فالموضوع في كل عقد بيع إنما هو نقل ملكية المبيع إلى المشتري بعوض ؛ وفي كل هبة إنما هو تمليك العين الموهوبة مجاناً بلا عوض؛ وفي كل عقد إجارة إنما هو تمليك منافع المأجور بعوض؛ وفي كل عقد إعارة إنما هو تمليك المنافع بلا عوض ؛ وهكذا.
ولذا قلنا: إن موضوع العقد هو الغاية النوعية فيه(۱)
وبذلك تتميز الغاية النوعية عن الباعث الذي هو غاية شخصية للعاقد. فإن الباعث مثلاً لبعض الناس على بيع شيء من ماله قد يكون هو احتياجه إلى الثمن أو كراهيته للمبيع، أو رغبته في الربح؛ وقد يكون الباعث لغيره على البيع هو رغبته في تهريب المال من وجه دائن أو وارث أو غير ذلك .
فالباعث قد يختلف باختلاف كل عاقد، أما الموضوع فإنما يختلف باختلاف نوع العقد كما بيناه (۲) .
المتعة
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أنه في بعض العقود قد يكون محل العقد مشتبهاً ما بين المحل والموضوع ،والعاقد كما في عقد النكاح. فإن محله في الحقيقة هو المشتركة بين الزوجين بغية التناسل أما موضوعه فهو ملك هذه المتعة، أي حق الاستمتاع ولذا يعرفه الفقهاء بأنه عقد موضوع لملك المتعة (ر: الدرر، أول النكاح). وبما أن غرض هذه المتعة التي هي محل العقد إنما هو الاستيلاد لذلك يقولون: إن محل النكاح هو المرأة بهذا الاعتبار، ولكن محله الحقيقي إنما هو المتعة الجنسية المشتركة لا المرأة وإلا كان في النكاح محل العقد والعاقد شيئاً واحداً. (۲) إن لمشروعية الغرض الباعث على التصرف أو عدم مشروعيته اعتباراً مؤثراً في كثير من أحوال العقود وأحكامها في نظر فقهائنا . وهذا ما استقر عليه الاجتهاد القضائي الحديث في الفقه الفرنسي أيضاً. (ر) نظرية العقد للأستاذ السنهوري ف/٥٤٤ .(٥٤٨)
أن الأستاذ الدكتور شفيق شحاتة في رسالته عن النظرية العامة للالتزامات في غير الشريعة الإسلامية» (ف/ ٤٨) يقول :
والواقع أن الدافع أو الباعث لم يعتبر أبداً عند الفقهاء، حتى إنهم قالوا: إن البيع يصح، ولو أن الغرض الذي سيستعمل فيه المبيع غير مشروع .
٤٠١
الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
ولا يخفى أن هذا الحكم غير سديد على إطلاقه حتى بالنسبة إلى الاجتهاد الحنفي الذي اتخذه الأستاذ المشار إليه أساساً للبحث في رسالته هذه.
فقد نص فقهاء الحنفية على مسائل يتجلى فيها أنهم يقررون تأثيراً هاماً لمشروعية الباعث وعدمها في مواطن من أحكام العقود : فمن ذلك ما صرحوا به في الإجارة أنَّ الإنسان لو استأجر عاملاً لهدم بنائه، ثم بدا له العدول عن الهدم، فإنّ له فسخ الإجارة بهذا العذر. أما إذا أراد فسخ الاستئجار لأنه وجد عاملاً أرخص أجراً، أو أراد أن يهدم البناء بنفسه فلا يعتبر فسخه. وكذلك نصوا في المزارعة - وهي نوع شركة زراعية تكون فيها الأرض من جانب والعمل من آخر - أن من عليه البذر يحق له فسخ المزارعة قبل إلقاء البذر في الأرض. لأن فيها إتلاف ماله وهو البذر فله الرجوع عنها، كما سنرى في بحث العقود غير اللازمة (ف ٧/٤٠ ثانياً) . لكن إذا أراد فسخ المزارعة لأنه وجد مزارعاً يرضى بنصيب أقل، أو أراد أن يعمل بنفسه فلا يحق له الفسخ (ر: رد المحتار ١٧٧/٥). فهذه الفروع الفقهية كما ترى من أوضح الصور على اعتبار الباعث وتأثير مشروعيته وعدمها في أحكام العقود بنظر فقهائنا.
حتى
إِنَّ عدم مشروعية الباعث على العقد قد توجب بطلان العقد في بعض المذاهب الاجتهادية، ولو كان العقد في ذاته مشروعاً، وهي قضية خلافية مشهورة في علم أصول الفقه الإسلامي، وذلك كمسألة الزواج بالمرأة المطلقة ثلاثاً إذا كان الباعث على الزواج ليس رغبة في الحياة الزوجية معها بل لأجل تحليلها للزوج الأول، وكمسألة بيع السلاح للعصاة والبغاة وبيع العصير لأجل اتخاذه خمراً، إلى غير ذلك. من المسائل التي اختلف فيها الاجتهاد. وقد أوضح هذا المبدأ واختلاف الفقهاء الأصوليين في حدوده ومدى تأثيره الإمام الشاطبي في مواطن عِدة من كتابه الموافقات»: (ر) الموافقات المسألة السابعة من النوع الثاني في الشروط ٢٧٤/١ - ۲۸۰ والمسألة الثالثة من بحث مقاصد المكلف، ۳۳۳/۲ - ۳۳۷) .
ويتصل هذا المبدأ بالقاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «الأمور بمقاصدها» النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام
وهي مستمدة مستمدة من قول
إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امری ما نوی ر ف ٢/١٠ الحاشية . نعم إن لفقهاء المذاهب أنظاراً مختلفة في حدود نظرية (الباعث) ومدى تأثيره. فبيع المدين أمواله بقصد تهريبها من وجه الدائنين صحيح نافذ في الاجتهاد الحنفي إذا كان غير محجور عليه قضاء بالإفلاس، لأن الدين متعلق بذمته، أما أعيان ماله فهي
Chapter (29): The Substance of the Contract. The subject matter in every contract of sale is only the transfer of ownership of the sold item to the buyer in exchange for a consideration; in every gift it is only the gratuitous transfer of ownership of the gifted item without consideration; in every contract of hire it is only the transfer of the usufruct of the hired item in exchange for consideration; in every contract of loan (for use) it is only the transfer of usufruct without consideration; and so on. Therefore we said: The subject matter of a contract is its generic purpose (1). By this the generic purpose is distinguished from the motive (bā‘ith), which is a personal purpose of the contracting party. For example, the motive for some people in selling something from their property may be their need for the price, or their dislike for the sold item, or their desire for profit; and the motive for others in selling may be their desire to hide the property from a creditor, an heir, or something else. So the motive may vary with each contracting party, whereas the subject matter varies only with the type of contract as we have explained (2). Mut‘ah (enjoyment). (1) Note at this point that in some contracts the object of the contract may be ambiguous between the object and the subject matter, and the contracting party, as in the marriage contract. Its object in reality is the joint enjoyment between the spouses for the purpose of procreation, but its subject matter is the ownership of this enjoyment, i.e., the right to enjoy. Therefore jurists define it as a contract whose subject matter is the ownership of enjoyment (see al-Durar, beginning of Nikah). And since the purpose of this enjoyment, which is the object of the contract, is procreation, they say: The object of the marriage contract is the woman in this regard, but its real object is the mutual sexual enjoyment, not the woman, otherwise in marriage the object of the contract and the contracting party would be one and the same. (2) The legitimacy or illegitimacy of the purpose motivating the transaction has an influential consideration in many circumstances and rulings of contracts in the view of our jurists. This is also what modern judicial jurisprudence in French law has settled upon. (See: Theory of Contract by Professor al-Sanhuri p/544-548). Professor Dr. Shafiq Shihatah in his thesis on the general theory of obligations in non-Islamic law (p/48) says: 'The reality is that the motive has never been considered by the jurists, even to the extent that they said: The sale is valid even if the purpose for which the sold item will be used is illegitimate.' 401 Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It is not hidden that this ruling is not sound in its absoluteness, even with respect to Hanafi jurisprudence which the above-mentioned professor took as a basis for research in his thesis. The Hanafi jurists have explicitly stated issues in which it is evident that they establish an important effect for the legitimacy or illegitimacy of the motive in places of contractual rulings. Among that is what they stated about hire: If a person hires a worker to demolish his building, then he decides to abandon the demolition, he has the right to rescind the hire contract due to this excuse. But if he wants to rescind the hire because he found a worker with a cheaper wage, or he wants to demolish the building himself, his rescission is not considered. Likewise they stated about muzāra‘ah – which is a type of agricultural partnership where the land is from one side and the work from another – that the one who is responsible for the seed has the right to rescind the muzāra‘ah before casting the seed into the ground, because it involves destroying his property, namely the seed, so he has the right to withdraw from it, as we will see in the discussion of non-binding contracts (p/7/40, second point). But if he wants to rescind the muzāra‘ah because he found a farmer who accepts a smaller share, or he wants to work himself, he does not have the right to rescind (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/177). These juristic branches, as you see, are among the clearest examples of considering the motive and the effect of its legitimacy or illegitimacy in contractual rulings in the view of our jurists. Even the illegitimacy of the motive for the contract may necessitate the nullity of the contract in some juristic schools, even if the contract itself is legitimate, and this is a well-known controversial issue in the science of Islamic legal theory. Examples include the marriage to a woman divorced three times when the motive for marriage is not desire for marital life with her but rather to make her lawful for the first husband, and the sale of weapons to rebels and aggressors, and the sale of grape juice for the purpose of making wine, and so on – issues over which juristic opinion has differed. Imam al-Shāṭibī clarified this principle and the difference among theoreticians regarding its limits and extent of influence in several places in his book al-Muwāfaqāt (see: al-Muwāfaqāt, issue 7 of the second type on conditions 1/274-280, and issue 3 of the discussion of the purposes of the obligated person 2/333-337). This principle is connected to the legal maxim that says: ‘Matters are judged by their objectives,’ derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: ‘Actions are but by intentions, and every man shall have only that which he intended.’ (see footnote 2/10). Yes, indeed the jurists of the schools have different views on the limits of the theory of motive and the extent of its effect. For example, the sale of a debtor's property with the intention of hiding it from creditors is valid and effective in Hanafi jurisprudence if he has not been judicially placed under interdiction for bankruptcy, because the debt is attached to his liability (dhimmah), while his specific assets (a‘yān mālihi) remain free.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-‘Āmm is a discussion of legal philosophy regarding the role of motive (bā‘ith) in contracts within Hanafi fiqh. The author, Dr. Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, begins by clearly distinguishing between the 'subject matter' (mawḍū‘) of a contract—its generic purpose that defines the type of contract (e.g., transfer of ownership in sale, transfer of usufruct in hire)—and the 'motive' (bā‘ith), which is the personal reason driving a particular contracting party. He then directly addresses a common misconception: that classical jurists, including Hanafis, never consider the motive. Al-Zarqā refutes this by citing specific Hanafi rulings in ijarah (hire) and muzara‘ah (sharecropping) where the legitimacy of the motive affects the right to rescind. For example, hiring a worker to demolish a building can be rescinded if the owner changes his mind about demolishing, but not if he simply finds cheaper labor—showing that the motive (changing the purpose) matters. Similarly, in muzara‘ah, the party providing seed can withdraw before sowing because it involves potential loss of his property, but not if his motive is merely to get a better deal. These examples demonstrate that Hanafi fiqh does give weight to the motive, especially when it relates to a change in the underlying purpose. The passage then connects this to the broader maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are judged by their objectives), derived from the hadith on intentions. The author notes the ikhtilāf among schools regarding extreme cases where an illegitimate motive might void a contract (e.g., marriage to make a thrice-divorced woman lawful, sale of weapons to rebels). While Hanafis generally uphold the validity of such contracts if the external form is sound, they still consider motive in nuanced ways, as seen in the debtor sale example: a debtor's sale to hide assets is valid unless he is legally declared bankrupt. This lesson refines the understanding that while Hanafis do not nullify contracts purely due to bad motive in all cases, motive is not irrelevant; it is a factor in specific rulings, especially regarding rights of rescission and the binding nature of contracts.

Key Terms
موضوع العقد (mawḍū‘ al-‘aqd): The generic purpose that defines the type of contract (e.g., transfer of ownership in sale).
الباعث (al-bā‘ith): The personal motive of the contracting party, which may vary from person to person.
المتعة (al-mut‘ah): The sexual enjoyment that is the subject matter of the marriage contract.
الغاية النوعية (al-ghāyah al-naw‘iyyah): The generic purpose that characterizes a contract type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the subject matter (mawḍū‘) from the motive (bā‘ith)?
A) The subject matter is fixed per contract type; the motive varies per individual.
B) The subject matter is always a physical object; the motive is an intention.
C) The subject matter is the consideration; the motive is the price.
D) The subject matter is the same as the object (maḥall); the motive is the purpose.

Key Takeaway
A contract's identity is determined by its subject matter (generic purpose), not by the parties' personal motives.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Approach to Motive in Contracts: Refuting a Misconception
Critical refinement on motive's role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between outward contract validity and motive-based rescission rights, and evaluate the claim that motive is never considered in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh gives the motive a nuanced role: it affects rescission but not basic validity.

A common claim is that classical Islamic fiqh, including Hanafi, never considers the motive of a contracting party. Dr. al-Zarqā refutes this by presenting concrete examples from ijarah and muzara‘ah where the motive determines the right to rescind. In this lesson, we examine these examples, link them to the maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā', and understand the cross-madhhab debate on when an illegitimate motive may void a contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception to be refined
The passage begins by criticizing Professor Shafiq Shihata's claim that motive is never considered. Recognize that this claim is too absolute.
The criticism is based on explicit Hanafi rulings.
2
Analyze the ijarah examples
Two scenarios: (a) hirer changes mind about demolition → rescission allowed (motive = change of purpose). (b) hirer finds cheaper worker → rescission not allowed (motive = financial gain).
The difference lies in whether the motive relates to the intrinsic purpose of the contract.
3
Analyze the muzara‘ah examples
Two scenarios: (a) seed provider withdraws before sowing → rescission allowed (motive = protecting property). (b) seed provider finds better partner → rescission not allowed (motive = better profit).
Again, only motives linked to the contract's core purpose justify rescission.
4
Link to the maxim al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā
The maxim is derived from the hadith of intentions. It is often cited to argue that motive affects validity, but Hanafis apply it more narrowly to specific rulings like rescission, not to invalidate contracts with bad motives.
The maxim operates alongside other principles like 'al-yaqīn lā yazūl bi al-shakk'.
5
Examine the cross-madhhab dispute
The passage mentions cases like tahlil marriage or selling weapons to rebels, where some schools void the contract entirely due to illegitimate motive. Hanafis generally uphold the contract's outward form while considering the motive sinful or affecting other rulings.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a Hanafi mufti will not declare such contracts void, but may advise against them or impose restrictions.

الفصل (۲۹) قوام العقد
فالموضوع في كل عقد بيع إنما هو نقل ملكية المبيع إلى المشتري بعوض ؛ وفي كل هبة إنما هو تمليك العين الموهوبة مجاناً بلا عوض؛ وفي كل عقد إجارة إنما هو تمليك منافع المأجور بعوض؛ وفي كل عقد إعارة إنما هو تمليك المنافع بلا عوض ؛ وهكذا.
ولذا قلنا: إن موضوع العقد هو الغاية النوعية فيه(۱)
وبذلك تتميز الغاية النوعية عن الباعث الذي هو غاية شخصية للعاقد. فإن الباعث مثلاً لبعض الناس على بيع شيء من ماله قد يكون هو احتياجه إلى الثمن أو كراهيته للمبيع، أو رغبته في الربح؛ وقد يكون الباعث لغيره على البيع هو رغبته في تهريب المال من وجه دائن أو وارث أو غير ذلك .
فالباعث قد يختلف باختلاف كل عاقد، أما الموضوع فإنما يختلف باختلاف نوع العقد كما بيناه (۲) .
المتعة
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أنه في بعض العقود قد يكون محل العقد مشتبهاً ما بين المحل والموضوع ،والعاقد كما في عقد النكاح. فإن محله في الحقيقة هو المشتركة بين الزوجين بغية التناسل أما موضوعه فهو ملك هذه المتعة، أي حق الاستمتاع ولذا يعرفه الفقهاء بأنه عقد موضوع لملك المتعة (ر: الدرر، أول النكاح). وبما أن غرض هذه المتعة التي هي محل العقد إنما هو الاستيلاد لذلك يقولون: إن محل النكاح هو المرأة بهذا الاعتبار، ولكن محله الحقيقي إنما هو المتعة الجنسية المشتركة لا المرأة وإلا كان في النكاح محل العقد والعاقد شيئاً واحداً. (۲) إن لمشروعية الغرض الباعث على التصرف أو عدم مشروعيته اعتباراً مؤثراً في كثير من أحوال العقود وأحكامها في نظر فقهائنا . وهذا ما استقر عليه الاجتهاد القضائي الحديث في الفقه الفرنسي أيضاً. (ر) نظرية العقد للأستاذ السنهوري ف/٥٤٤ .(٥٤٨)
أن الأستاذ الدكتور شفيق شحاتة في رسالته عن النظرية العامة للالتزامات في غير الشريعة الإسلامية» (ف/ ٤٨) يقول :
والواقع أن الدافع أو الباعث لم يعتبر أبداً عند الفقهاء، حتى إنهم قالوا: إن البيع يصح، ولو أن الغرض الذي سيستعمل فيه المبيع غير مشروع .

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

فقد نص فقهاء الحنفية على مسائل يتجلى فيها أنهم يقررون تأثيراً هاماً لمشروعية الباعث وعدمها في مواطن من أحكام العقود : فمن ذلك ما صرحوا به في الإجارة أنَّ الإنسان لو استأجر عاملاً لهدم بنائه، ثم بدا له العدول عن الهدم، فإنّ له فسخ الإجارة بهذا العذر. أما إذا أراد فسخ الاستئجار لأنه وجد عاملاً أرخص أجراً، أو أراد أن يهدم البناء بنفسه فلا يعتبر فسخه. وكذلك نصوا في المزارعة - وهي نوع شركة زراعية تكون فيها الأرض من جانب والعمل من آخر - أن من عليه البذر يحق له فسخ المزارعة قبل إلقاء البذر في الأرض. لأن فيها إتلاف ماله وهو البذر فله الرجوع عنها، كما سنرى في بحث العقود غير اللازمة (ف ٧/٤٠ ثانياً) . لكن إذا أراد فسخ المزارعة لأنه وجد مزارعاً يرضى بنصيب أقل، أو أراد أن يعمل بنفسه فلا يحق له الفسخ (ر: رد المحتار ١٧٧/٥). فهذه الفروع الفقهية كما ترى من أوضح الصور على اعتبار الباعث وتأثير مشروعيته وعدمها في أحكام العقود بنظر فقهائنا.
حتى
إِنَّ عدم مشروعية الباعث على العقد قد توجب بطلان العقد في بعض المذاهب الاجتهادية، ولو كان العقد في ذاته مشروعاً، وهي قضية خلافية مشهورة في علم أصول الفقه الإسلامي، وذلك كمسألة الزواج بالمرأة المطلقة ثلاثاً إذا كان الباعث على الزواج ليس رغبة في الحياة الزوجية معها بل لأجل تحليلها للزوج الأول، وكمسألة بيع السلاح للعصاة والبغاة وبيع العصير لأجل اتخاذه خمراً، إلى غير ذلك. من المسائل التي اختلف فيها الاجتهاد. وقد أوضح هذا المبدأ واختلاف الفقهاء الأصوليين في حدوده ومدى تأثيره الإمام الشاطبي في مواطن عِدة من كتابه الموافقات»: (ر) الموافقات المسألة السابعة من النوع الثاني في الشروط ٢٧٤/١ - ۲۸۰ والمسألة الثالثة من بحث مقاصد المكلف، ۳۳۳/۲ - ۳۳۷) .

وهي مستمدة مستمدة من قول
إنما الأعمال بالنيات وإنما لكل امری ما نوی ر ف ٢/١٠ الحاشية . نعم إن لفقهاء المذاهب أنظاراً مختلفة في حدود نظرية (الباعث) ومدى تأثيره. فبيع المدين أمواله بقصد تهريبها من وجه الدائنين صحيح نافذ في الاجتهاد الحنفي إذا كان غير محجور عليه قضاء بالإفلاس، لأن الدين متعلق بذمته، أما أعيان ماله فهي
Chapter (29): The Substance of the Contract. The subject matter in every contract of sale is only the transfer of ownership of the sold item to the buyer in exchange for a consideration; in every gift it is only the gratuitous transfer of ownership of the gifted item without consideration; in every contract of hire it is only the transfer of the usufruct of the hired item in exchange for consideration; in every contract of loan (for use) it is only the transfer of usufruct without consideration; and so on. Therefore we said: The subject matter of a contract is its generic purpose (1). By this the generic purpose is distinguished from the motive (bā‘ith), which is a personal purpose of the contracting party. For example, the motive for some people in selling something from their property may be their need for the price, or their dislike for the sold item, or their desire for profit; and the motive for others in selling may be their desire to hide the property from a creditor, an heir, or something else. So the motive may vary with each contracting party, whereas the subject matter varies only with the type of contract as we have explained (2). Mut‘ah (enjoyment). (1) Note at this point that in some contracts the object of the contract may be ambiguous between the object and the subject matter, and the contracting party, as in the marriage contract. Its object in reality is the joint enjoyment between the spouses for the purpose of procreation, but its subject matter is the ownership of this enjoyment, i.e., the right to enjoy. Therefore jurists define it as a contract whose subject matter is the ownership of enjoyment (see al-Durar, beginning of Nikah). And since the purpose of this enjoyment, which is the object of the contract, is procreation, they say: The object of the marriage contract is the woman in this regard, but its real object is the mutual sexual enjoyment, not the woman, otherwise in marriage the object of the contract and the contracting party would be one and the same. (2) The legitimacy or illegitimacy of the purpose motivating the transaction has an influential consideration in many circumstances and rulings of contracts in the view of our jurists. This is also what modern judicial jurisprudence in French law has settled upon. (See: Theory of Contract by Professor al-Sanhuri p/544-548). Professor Dr. Shafiq Shihatah in his thesis on the general theory of obligations in non-Islamic law (p/48) says: 'The reality is that the motive has never been considered by the jurists, even to the extent that they said: The sale is valid even if the purpose for which the sold item will be used is illegitimate.' 401 Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It is not hidden that this ruling is not sound in its absoluteness, even with respect to Hanafi jurisprudence which the above-mentioned professor took as a basis for research in his thesis. The Hanafi jurists have explicitly stated issues in which it is evident that they establish an important effect for the legitimacy or illegitimacy of the motive in places of contractual rulings. Among that is what they stated about hire: If a person hires a worker to demolish his building, then he decides to abandon the demolition, he has the right to rescind the hire contract due to this excuse. But if he wants to rescind the hire because he found a worker with a cheaper wage, or he wants to demolish the building himself, his rescission is not considered. Likewise they stated about muzāra‘ah – which is a type of agricultural partnership where the land is from one side and the work from another – that the one who is responsible for the seed has the right to rescind the muzāra‘ah before casting the seed into the ground, because it involves destroying his property, namely the seed, so he has the right to withdraw from it, as we will see in the discussion of non-binding contracts (p/7/40, second point). But if he wants to rescind the muzāra‘ah because he found a farmer who accepts a smaller share, or he wants to work himself, he does not have the right to rescind (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/177). These juristic branches, as you see, are among the clearest examples of considering the motive and the effect of its legitimacy or illegitimacy in contractual rulings in the view of our jurists. Even the illegitimacy of the motive for the contract may necessitate the nullity of the contract in some juristic schools, even if the contract itself is legitimate, and this is a well-known controversial issue in the science of Islamic legal theory. Examples include the marriage to a woman divorced three times when the motive for marriage is not desire for marital life with her but rather to make her lawful for the first husband, and the sale of weapons to rebels and aggressors, and the sale of grape juice for the purpose of making wine, and so on – issues over which juristic opinion has differed. Imam al-Shāṭibī clarified this principle and the difference among theoreticians regarding its limits and extent of influence in several places in his book al-Muwāfaqāt (see: al-Muwāfaqāt, issue 7 of the second type on conditions 1/274-280, and issue 3 of the discussion of the purposes of the obligated person 2/333-337). This principle is connected to the legal maxim that says: ‘Matters are judged by their objectives,’ derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: ‘Actions are but by intentions, and every man shall have only that which he intended.’ (see footnote 2/10). Yes, indeed the jurists of the schools have different views on the limits of the theory of motive and the extent of its effect. For example, the sale of a debtor's property with the intention of hiding it from creditors is valid and effective in Hanafi jurisprudence if he has not been judicially placed under interdiction for bankruptcy, because the debt is attached to his liability (dhimmah), while his specific assets (a‘yān mālihi) remain free.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhī al-‘Āmm is a discussion of legal philosophy regarding the role of motive (bā‘ith) in contracts within Hanafi fiqh. The author, Dr. Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, begins by clearly distinguishing between the 'subject matter' (mawḍū‘) of a contract—its generic purpose that defines the type of contract (e.g., transfer of ownership in sale, transfer of usufruct in hire)—and the 'motive' (bā‘ith), which is the personal reason driving a particular contracting party. He then directly addresses a common misconception: that classical jurists, including Hanafis, never consider the motive. Al-Zarqā refutes this by citing specific Hanafi rulings in ijarah (hire) and muzara‘ah (sharecropping) where the legitimacy of the motive affects the right to rescind. For example, hiring a worker to demolish a building can be rescinded if the owner changes his mind about demolishing, but not if he simply finds cheaper labor—showing that the motive (changing the purpose) matters. Similarly, in muzara‘ah, the party providing seed can withdraw before sowing because it involves potential loss of his property, but not if his motive is merely to get a better deal. These examples demonstrate that Hanafi fiqh does give weight to the motive, especially when it relates to a change in the underlying purpose. The passage then connects this to the broader maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā' (matters are judged by their objectives), derived from the hadith on intentions. The author notes the ikhtilāf among schools regarding extreme cases where an illegitimate motive might void a contract (e.g., marriage to make a thrice-divorced woman lawful, sale of weapons to rebels). While Hanafis generally uphold the validity of such contracts if the external form is sound, they still consider motive in nuanced ways, as seen in the debtor sale example: a debtor's sale to hide assets is valid unless he is legally declared bankrupt. This lesson refines the understanding that while Hanafis do not nullify contracts purely due to bad motive in all cases, motive is not irrelevant; it is a factor in specific rulings, especially regarding rights of rescission and the binding nature of contracts.

Key Terms
موضوع العقد (mawḍū‘ al-‘aqd): The generic purpose that defines the type of contract (e.g., transfer of ownership in sale).
الباعث (al-bā‘ith): The personal motive of the contracting party, which may vary from person to person.
الغاية النوعية (al-ghāyah al-naw‘iyyah): The generic purpose that characterizes a contract type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the Hanafi school allows rescission in the ijarah scenario of hiring a worker for demolition but not when a cheaper worker is found?
A) The first scenario involves an illegal motive; the second does not.
B) The first scenario involves a change in the contract's purpose; the second involves a purely financial motive.
C) The first scenario is a binding contract; the second is non-binding.
D) The first scenario involves a loss of property; the second does not.

Key Takeaway
Motive is not ignored in Hanafi fiqh; it affects rescission rights in non-binding contracts and is weighed against other principles in determining contractual effects.

The Role of Motive (al-Bā'ith) in Hanafi Contract Law
Analyzing the role of motive in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on motive to evaluate the validity of contract rescission in ijarah and muzara'ah, and compare it with the broader role of motive in other madhhabs.

Core Idea
Motive affects rescission in Hanafi contracts but not the foundational validity.

This lesson examines how Hanafi jurists incorporate the motive (bā'ith) of the contracting parties into specific rulings, particularly rescission in ijarah and muzara'ah. You will see that while a valid motive (like avoiding waste) permits rescission, an economic motive (like seeking a cheaper alternative) does not. This nuanced approach is tied to the major maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' and contrasts with other madhhabs that may void contracts due to illegitimate motives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
Identify the two Hanafi examples from the passage: ijarah (hire) and muzara'ah (sharecropping).
2
Distinguish valid and invalid motives for rescission
For each example, note the valid motive (e.g., change of purpose, avoiding waste) that allows rescission, and the invalid motive (e.g., finding a cheaper worker or farmer) that does not.
The valid motive relates to the contract's purpose or prevention of harm.
3
Derive the underlying principle
Conclude that in Hanafi fiqh, motive matters for rescission when it is connected to the subject matter or legitimate interest, but not for mere economic advantage.
This aligns with the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha'.
4
Compare with other madhhabs
Understand the broader dispute: some schools may void a contract entirely if the motive is illegitimate (e.g., tahlil marriage, selling weapons to rebels).
Hanafis generally uphold the contract's validity if the contract itself is sound.
5
Apply the exception: debtor's sale
Analyze the Hanafi ruling that a debtor's sale to evade creditors is valid if he is not interdicted, because the debt is on his liability, not his assets.
This is a key exception where bad motive does not affect validity.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Chapter (29): The Substance of the Contract. 29/5 - This, and it becomes clear from the foregoing that the subject matter of the contract in terms of ultimate outcome is what jurists call the legal effect (hukm al-aqd), i.e., the consequence that results from it. So the subject matter, the typical purpose, and the effect are very close concepts, and their names differ according to the perspective. The subject matter of a sale contract, which is transfer of ownership for consideration, when looked at from the perspective of the two contracting parties, is a goal they seek to achieve through this type of contract that leads to it. And when looked at from the perspective of the Lawgiver, it is the ruling and effect that the legislation establishes as a result of the contract after its occurrence (see: F 3/27). Fourth: Pillars of the Contract. 29/6 - As for the pillars of the contract, i.e., the parts it consists of, they are two things: offer and acceptance. They are, as became clear from previous occasions, the constitutive factors in building the contract. Offer and acceptance are the legal act that binds the two contractors, making their wills a binding law for them within the limits of their rights. Offer and acceptance are called the contract formula, i.e., the corresponding expressions indicating the agreement of the two parties (see F 3/27). This formula involves important fiqh discussions that we will devote to the following chapter. That is a great hardship. Free, and if every buyer were required to know what debts his seller has, it would be a great difficulty and the wisdom of legislation would be repelled. However, later Hanafis issued fatwas regarding waqf that the waqf of a debtor is not effective to the extent of his debt unless the creditor creditors permit it, to prevent the debtor from resorting to waqf of his property with the motive of evading the authority of the creditors. (see: F 6/15 footnote and F 5/38 and 5/65, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/403, 395-396).

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the role of motive (bā'ith) in contracts, followed by a definition of the contract's subject matter and pillars, as presented by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa in his introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by critiquing an absolute statement that motive is irrelevant, even within Hanafi ijtihad. He then provides two clear Hanafi examples where motive affects the ruling: in ijarah (hire), a person may rescind if he changes his mind about demolition (a valid motive), but not if he simply finds a cheaper worker or wants to work himself (invalid motives). Similarly, in muzara'ah (sharecropping), the seed owner may rescind before planting to avoid wasting his property, but not for economic reasons. These demonstrate that Hanafi jurists consider motive in contract rulings. The author then notes a controversial principle: in some madhhabs, an illegitimate motive can void an otherwise valid contract, citing examples like tahlil marriage and selling weapons to rebels. He references Imam al-Shatibi's discussion in al-Muwafaqat and connects the principle to the major maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are determined by their objectives), derived from the hadith of intentions. He then presents a Hanafi ruling: a debtor's sale of property to evade creditors is valid if he is not interdicted, because the debt is on his liability, not his assets. The passage then shifts to defining the subject matter of a contract (mawdu' al-aqd) as its legal effect (hukm), using the example of sale (transfer of ownership). It explains that this is the goal for the parties and the ruling from the Lawgiver's perspective. Finally, it states that the pillars of a contract are offer and acceptance, which together form the contract formula (sigha). A footnote addresses later Hanafi fatwas on a debtor's waqf, which is not enforceable without creditor consent to prevent evasion. The discussion emphasizes that despite different views, Hanafi fiqh gives motive a role within specific limits, and that contract structure is built on offer and acceptance as essential elements.

Key Terms
الباعث (al-bā'ith): The motive or reason behind entering a contract, which may affect its rulings in specific contexts.
مقاصد (maqāṣid): Objectives or purposes; here related to the maxim 'al-umūr bi maqāṣidihā'.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh considers motive in contract rescission when it relates to the contract's purpose, but generally does not void a contract due to bad motive alone.

The Structure of Contracts: Subject Matter and Pillars in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining contract components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the subject matter of a contract as its legal effect and identify the pillars as offer and acceptance, distinguishing Hanafi views from other schools.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the subject matter is the legal effect, and the only pillars are offer and acceptance.

This lesson clarifies the key components of a contract according to Hanafi jurists. The subject matter (mawdu') is equated with the legal effect (hukm) and the goal (ghayah) of the parties. The pillars (arkan) are limited to offer and acceptance, which together form the contract formula (sigha). Understanding this structure is essential for analyzing contract formation and validity.

Guided Path
1
Define the subject matter of a contract
Explain that in Hanafi fiqh, the subject matter (mawḍūʿ al-ʿaqd) is the same as the legal effect (ḥukm al-ʿaqd) and the typical purpose (ghāyah). Use the example of sale: transfer of ownership.
It is viewed from two perspectives: the parties' goal and the Lawgiver's ruling.
2
Identify the pillars of a contract
State that the pillars (arkān) are only two: offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl).
These are the constitutive factors that form the contract.
3
Understand the contract formula
Note that offer and acceptance together are called the sigha (formula), which represents the mutual agreement.
4
Contrast with other schools
While Hanafis limit pillars to offer and acceptance, other madhhabs may include the subject matter, price, or other elements as pillars.
This affects contract formation conditions.
5
Apply to a modern contract
Analyze a simple sale: 'I sell this book for $10' (offer) and 'I accept' (acceptance). The subject matter is the transfer of ownership.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
ولا يخفى أن هذا الحكم غير سديد على إطلاقه حتى بالنسبة إلى الاجتهاد الحنفي الذي اتخذه الأستاذ المشار إليه أساساً للبحث في رسالته هذه.
فقد نص فقهاء الحنفية على مسائل يتجلى فيها أنهم يقررون تأثيراً هاماً لمشروعية الباعث وعدمها في مواطن من أحكام العقود : فمن ذلك ما صرحوا به في الإجارة أنَّ الإنسان لو استأجر عاملاً لهدم بنائه، ثم بدا له العدول عن الهدم، فإنّ له فسخ الإجارة بهذا العذر. أما إذا أراد فسخ الاستئجار لأنه وجد عاملاً أرخص أجراً، أو أراد أن يهدم البناء بنفسه فلا يعتبر فسخه. وكذلك نصوا في المزارعة - وهي نوع شركة زراعية تكون فيها الأرض من جانب والعمل من آخر - أن من عليه البذر يحق له فسخ المزارعة قبل إلقاء البذر في الأرض. لأن فيها إتلاف ماله وهو البذر فله الرجوع عنها، كما سنرى في بحث العقود غير اللازمة (ف ٧/٤٠ ثانياً) . لكن إذا أراد فسخ المزارعة لأنه وجد مزارعاً يرضى بنصيب أقل، أو أراد أن يعمل بنفسه فلا يحق له الفسخ (ر: رد المحتار ١٧٧/٥). فهذه الفروع الفقهية كما ترى من أوضح الصور على اعتبار الباعث وتأثير مشروعيته وعدمها في أحكام العقود بنظر فقهائنا.
حتى
إِنَّ عدم مشروعية الباعث على العقد قد توجب بطلان العقد في بعض المذاهب الاجتهادية، ولو كان العقد في ذاته مشروعاً، وهي قضية خلافية مشهورة في علم أصول الفقه الإسلامي، وذلك كمسألة الزواج بالمرأة المطلقة ثلاثاً إذا كان الباعث على الزواج ليس رغبة في الحياة الزوجية معها بل لأجل تحليلها للزوج الأول، وكمسألة بيع السلاح للعصاة والبغاة وبيع العصير لأجل اتخاذه خمراً، إلى غير ذلك. من المسائل التي اختلف فيها الاجتهاد. وقد أوضح هذا المبدأ واختلاف الفقهاء الأصوليين في حدوده ومدى تأثيره الإمام الشاطبي في مواطن عِدة من كتابه الموافقات»: (ر) الموافقات المسألة السابعة من النوع الثاني في الشروط ٢٧٤/١ - ۲۸۰ والمسألة الثالثة من بحث مقاصد المكلف، ۳۳۳/۲ - ۳۳۷) .

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It is not hidden that this ruling is not sound in its absolute sense even with respect to Hanafi ijtihad which the mentioned professor took as the basis for research in his dissertation. For Hanafi jurists have stated in issues where it is manifest that they affirm an important effect of the legality or illegality of the motive in instances of contract rulings: among that is what they explicitly stated in ijarah that if a person hires a worker to demolish his building, then he decides to refrain from demolition, he may rescind the ijarah due to this excuse. But if he wants to rescind the hire because he found a cheaper worker, or he wants to demolish the building himself, his rescission is not considered. Likewise they stated in muzara'ah - which is a type of agricultural partnership where the land is from one side and the work from the other - that the one responsible for the seed has the right to rescind the muzara'ah before casting the seed into the ground, because it involves wasting his property which is the seed, so he may withdraw from it, as we will see in the discussion of non-binding contracts (F 7/40 secondarily). But if he wants to rescind the muzara'ah because he found a farmer who accepts a smaller share, or he wants to work himself, he does not have the right to rescind (see: Radd al-Muhtar 5/177). So these fiqh branches, as you see, are among the clearest examples of considering the motive and the effect of its legality or illegality in contract rulings according to our jurists. Even that the illegality of the motive for a contract may render the contract void in some madhhabs of ijtihad, even if the contract in itself is legitimate, and it is a well-known disputed issue in usul al-fiqh. That is like the issue of marrying a woman thrice-divorced if the motive for marriage is not desire for marital life with her but rather to make her permissible for the first husband, and like the issue of selling weapons to rebels and transgressors and selling grape juice to be made into wine, and so forth, issues on which ijtihad differed. Imam al-Shatibi explained this principle and the difference among usuli jurists regarding its limits and extent of its effect in several places in his book al-Muwafaqat (see: al-Muwafaqat, the seventh issue of the second type on conditions 1/274-280, and the third issue from the discussion of the objectives of the legally responsible person 2/333-337). This principle is connected to the legal maxim: 'Matters are determined by their objectives,' which is derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Indeed actions are by intentions, and every person gets what he intended' (F 2/10 footnote). Yes, the jurists of the madhhabs have different views on the limits of the theory of motive and its effect. So the sale of a debtor's property with the intention of evading creditors is valid and effective in Hanafi ijtihad if he is not legally interdicted due to bankruptcy, because the debt is attached to his liability, while his specific assets are... unites? Chapter (29): The Substance of the Contract. 29/5 - This, and it becomes clear from the foregoing that the subject matter of the contract in terms of ultimate outcome is what jurists call the legal effect (hukm al-aqd), i.e., the consequence that results from it. So the subject matter, the typical purpose, and the effect are very close concepts, and their names differ according to the perspective. The subject matter of a sale contract, which is transfer of ownership for consideration, when looked at from the perspective of the two contracting parties, is a goal they seek to achieve through this type of contract that leads to it. And when looked at from the perspective of the Lawgiver, it is the ruling and effect that the legislation establishes as a result of the contract after its occurrence (see: F 3/27). Fourth: Pillars of the Contract. 29/6 - As for the pillars of the contract, i.e., the parts it consists of, they are two things: offer and acceptance. They are, as became clear from previous occasions, the constitutive factors in building the contract. Offer and acceptance are the legal act that binds the two contractors, making their wills a binding law for them within the limits of their rights. Offer and acceptance are called the contract formula, i.e., the corresponding expressions indicating the agreement of the two parties (see F 3/27). This formula involves important fiqh discussions that we will devote to the following chapter. That is a great hardship. Free, and if every buyer were required to know what debts his seller has, it would be a great difficulty and the wisdom of legislation would be repelled. However, later Hanafis issued fatwas regarding waqf that the waqf of a debtor is not effective to the extent of his debt unless the creditor creditors permit it, to prevent the debtor from resorting to waqf of his property with the motive of evading the authority of the creditors. (see: F 6/15 footnote and F 5/38 and 5/65, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/403, 395-396).
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the role of motive (bā'ith) in contracts, followed by a definition of the contract's subject matter and pillars, as presented by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa in his introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by critiquing an absolute statement that motive is irrelevant, even within Hanafi ijtihad. He then provides two clear Hanafi examples where motive affects the ruling: in ijarah (hire), a person may rescind if he changes his mind about demolition (a valid motive), but not if he simply finds a cheaper worker or wants to work himself (invalid motives). Similarly, in muzara'ah (sharecropping), the seed owner may rescind before planting to avoid wasting his property, but not for economic reasons. These demonstrate that Hanafi jurists consider motive in contract rulings. The author then notes a controversial principle: in some madhhabs, an illegitimate motive can void an otherwise valid contract, citing examples like tahlil marriage and selling weapons to rebels. He references Imam al-Shatibi's discussion in al-Muwafaqat and connects the principle to the major maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are determined by their objectives), derived from the hadith of intentions. He then presents a Hanafi ruling: a debtor's sale of property to evade creditors is valid if he is not interdicted, because the debt is on his liability, not his assets. The passage then shifts to defining the subject matter of a contract (mawdu' al-aqd) as its legal effect (hukm), using the example of sale (transfer of ownership). It explains that this is the goal for the parties and the ruling from the Lawgiver's perspective. Finally, it states that the pillars of a contract are offer and acceptance, which together form the contract formula (sigha). A footnote addresses later Hanafi fatwas on a debtor's waqf, which is not enforceable without creditor consent to prevent evasion. The discussion emphasizes that despite different views, Hanafi fiqh gives motive a role within specific limits, and that contract structure is built on offer and acceptance as essential elements.

Key Terms
موضوع العقد (mawḍūʿ al-ʿaqd): The subject matter of the contract, equated with its legal effect (ḥukm al-ʿaqd) and the goal of the parties.
أركان العقد (arkān al-ʿaqd): Pillars of the contract: offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl), which constitute the contract formula.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ījāb wa al-qabūl): Offer and acceptance: the mutual expressions that form the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the pillars of a contract in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Offer, acceptance, and subject matter
B) Offer and acceptance only
C) Subject matter and price
D) Offer, acceptance, and consideration

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract's pillars are only offer and acceptance, and the subject matter is the legal effect that results from the contract.

Essence and Pillars of the Contract: The Three Perspectives and the Formula
Defines the conceptual triad and the pillars of a contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the subject, objective, and ruling of a contract, and identify offer and acceptance as its two pillars.

Core Idea
A contract's legal effect is subject, objective, or ruling depending on viewpoint; its pillars are offer and acceptance.

In this lesson, we explore the conceptual framework of contracts. The author clarifies that the subject, objective, and ruling are three angles on the same reality—the contract's legal outcome. Then he defines the pillars: offer and acceptance, which together form the binding formula.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three perspectives
Recognize that the subject (mawdu'), the specific objective (ghayah), and the ruling (hukm) are different views of the same legal effect of a contract.
They are not separate entities; naming depends on the angle of consideration.
2
Apply perspectives to a concrete contract
For a sales contract, the subject is transfer of ownership for a price; for the two parties it is their objective; for the Lawgiver it is the established ruling.
This clarifies that these concepts converge in practice.
3
Define the pillars
The pillars (arkan) of a contract are the parts from which it is composed. According to this passage, they are two things: offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).
Offer and acceptance are the constructive factors in building the contract.
4
Understand the formula
Offer and acceptance together constitute the formula (sighah)—the corresponding expressions indicating mutual agreement.
The formula is the verbal (or otherwise) expression that binds the parties.
5
Relate to the mufti's work
A mufti uses this framework to analyze contract validity: first check for proper offer and acceptance, then identify the subject and its ruling.
This is foundational for real-world fatwa practice.

Chapter (29) Essence of the Contract. 29/5 - This, and it becomes clear from the preceding that the subject of the contract in terms of its final outcome is what the jurists call the ruling of the contract, i.e., the effect that results from it. So the subject, the specific objective, and the ruling are very close concepts, and the naming differs only according to the perspective. So the subject of a sales contract, which is transferring ownership for a consideration, if viewed from the perspective of the two contracting parties, is their objective that they seek to achieve through this type of contract that leads to it. And if viewed from the perspective of the Legislator, it is the ruling and effect that the legislation establishes as a result of the contract after its occurrence (see: vol. 3, p. 27). Fourth: The Pillars of the Contract. 29/6 - As for the pillars of the contract, i.e., the parts from which it is composed, they are two things: offer and acceptance. They are, as clarified from the previous contexts, the constructive factors in building the contract. For offer and acceptance are the legal action that binds the contracting parties, whereby their two wills become a binding law for them within the limits of their rights. Offer and acceptance are called the formula of the contract, i.e., the corresponding expressions that indicate the agreement of the two parties. (see: vol. 3, p. 27). This formula is associated with important jurisprudential discussions that we will dedicate the following chapter to.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of the contract. The author first clarifies that the subject (mawdu'), the specific objective (ghayah), and the ruling (hukm) of a contract are three perspectives on the same reality: the legal effect intended by the parties and sanctioned by the Lawgiver. Then it states the pillars (arkan) of a contract: offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), which together form the formula (sighah) that binds the parties. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion in the next chapter.

Key Terms
العقد (al-ʿaqd): The contract; the agreement of two wills producing legal effects.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ījāb wa al-qabūl): Offer and acceptance; the two pillars of a contract that form its formula.
الصيغة (al-ṣīghah): The verbal formula expressing the contract.

Key Takeaway

Three Essential Matters of the Contract Formula
Lists three essential conditions for the verbal formula of a contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three essential matters that must be present in the verbal formula of a contract according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A contract's verbal formula must have clarity, correspondence, and determination—else it is invalid.

In Hanafi contract law, the essence of a contract is mutual consent. However, because consent is internal, the law requires a clear verbal expression. This passage introduces three indispensable conditions for the offer and acceptance to be legally effective. Understanding these conditions is foundational for any mufti or jurist dealing with contracts.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the principle: contracts rest on mutual consent
Understand that the verbal formula is merely an expression of internal will; the substance is agreement. This sets the context for why explicit conditions are needed.
2
Identify the first condition: clarity of meaning (jalā' al-maʿnā)
The words used must unambiguously indicate the type of contract, e.g., 'I sell' for a sale. Ambiguity can invalidate the contract.
Hanafi: determined by ʿurf (custom) of the contracting parties.
3
Identify the second condition: correspondence of offer and acceptance (tawāfuq)
Offer and acceptance must match in subject matter, time, and conditions. Minor wording differences are overlooked if the meaning aligns.
Hanafi: deviations in wording are overlooked if meaning matches.
4
Identify the third condition: determination of wills (jazm al-irādatayn)
Each party must express a firm and unconditional intention. Conditional or suspended offers do not create a binding contract until the condition is fulfilled.
Hanafi: conditional offers are not binding.
5
Apply the three conditions to evaluate a contract's validity
Use these criteria to determine if a given verbal formula is legally sufficient. For example, if a seller says 'Maybe I sell' it lacks determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في معنى العقد اتفاق الإرادتين، أي التراضي وليست الألفاظ إلا ترجمة عنهما .
ولما كانت الإرادة من الأمور الباطنة وكانت للعقود نتائج ذات بال في الأموال والأعمال أوجب الفقه الإسلامي ظهور الإرادتين بشكل واضح بين لا شك فيه ؛ وذلك بأن تتوافر في الصيغة اللفظية المعبرة عنهما، وهي الإيجاب والقبول ثلاثة أمور أساسية :
-
جلاء المعنى.
وتوافق الإيجاب والقبول.
- وجزم الإرادتين .
وإليك إيضاح هذه النواحي :
The origin of the meaning of contract is the agreement of two wills, i.e., mutual consent, and words are but a translation of them. Since will is internal and contracts have significant consequences in wealth and actions, Islamic jurisprudence required the manifestation of the two wills in a clear manner without doubt; that is, that the verbal formula expressing them, which is offer and acceptance, must contain three essential matters: (1) clarity of meaning, (2) correspondence of offer and acceptance, and (3) determination of the two wills. And here is an explanation of these aspects:
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage establishes the fundamental rule that the essence of a contract is mutual consent (tarāḍī), and words are merely the external expression. Because consent is internal, Islamic law requires the verbal formula to clearly manifest the parties' intentions. Three conditions are mandatory: (1) clarity of meaning (jalā' al-ma'nā) — the words must unambiguously indicate the type of contract; (2) correspondence (tawāfuq) — offer and acceptance must match; (3) determination (jazm al-irādatayn) — each party must be decisive, not conditional or tentative. These rules ensure legal certainty and prevent disputes.

Key Terms
جلاء المعنى (jalā' al-maʿnā): Clarity of meaning: the words used must clearly indicate the intended type of contract.
توافق الإيجاب والقبول (tawāfuq al-ījāb wa al-qabūl): Correspondence between offer and acceptance: they must match in subject matter, time, and conditions.
جزم الإرادتين (jazm al-irādatayn): Determination of the two wills: both parties must express a firm and unconditional intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many essential matters must the verbal formula of a contract contain?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
A contract's verbal formula is valid only if it meets three conditions: clarity of meaning, correspondence of offer and acceptance, and determination of the two wills.
