Acceptance with Beneficial Variation (القبول المخالف إلى خير)
Explaining that a counter-offer entirely beneficial to the offeror constitutes acceptance, not rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a counter-offer purely beneficial to the offeror does not prevent contract formation and identify when such a variation requires separate consent.

Core Idea
A beneficial variation in acceptance is not a rejection; it is acceptance with a condition subject to the offeror's consent.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in contract formation: when acceptance differs from the offer in a way that is entirely beneficial to the offeror, the contract is still concluded on the original terms, and the beneficial variation is binding only if the offeror consents. This rule reflects the Hanafi emphasis on substantive intentions over formal expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the offer and the variation
Determine the original offer (price and terms) and how the acceptance differs. Is the difference in price, payment method, or other conditions?
The variation must be clearly compared to the offer.
2
Assess whether the variation is entirely beneficial to the offeror
Consider the offeror's perspective. Is the variation beneficial from all angles (من كل وجه)? If it is a reduction in price or an increase in price without adverse terms, it likely qualifies.
If the variation imposes a non-benefit (e.g., change from cash to credit), it is not entirely beneficial.
3
Apply the ruling: contract formed on original offer
If the variation is entirely beneficial, the contract is concluded on the original terms. The acceptor's response is considered acceptance with a conditional benefit.
The change does not become a counter-offer.
4
Determine binding effect of the variation
The variation (reduction or increase) is not automatically binding; it depends on the offeror's consent. If the offeror does not consent, the contract stands on the original terms.
The rule: الحط من العوض والزيادة فيه لمصلحة الطرف الآخر يتوقف لزومهما على رضاه.
5
Distinguish non-beneficial variation
If the variation is not entirely beneficial (e.g., different payment terms), the acceptance is treated as a new offer (إيجاب جديد) requiring separate acceptance.
Example: seller offers 20 cash, buyer accepts 40 credit – not entirely beneficial, so it is a new offer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
انعقاد العقد على أساسه إلى قبول من الطرف الآخر الذي كان هو الموجب، فيصبح القابل موجباً، والموجب قابلاً.
على أنه إذا كانت مخالفة القبول للإيجاب، إنما هي مخالفة إلى خير من كل وجه بالنسبة إلى الشخص الموجب لا تكون مانعة من الانعقاد لأنها في الحقيقة ليست مخالفة وإنما هي عندئذ زيادة في الموافقة، وليست العبرة للأشكال الصورية بل للمقاصد الجوهرية .
فلو أوجب المشتري بعشرة دنانير فقبل البائع بأقل منها، أو أوجب البائع بعشرة فقبل المشتري بأكثر منها كان ذلك في الحقيقة قبولاً بالمبلغ الذي تضمنه الإيجاب مع التزام حط منه أو زيادة فيه لمصلحة الطرف الموجب. فينعقد البيع بالثمن الذي تضمنه الإيجاب، ويتوقف لزوم الحط أو الزيادة على رضى الطرف الموجب لأنَّ القاعدة: أنَّ الحط من العوض والزيادة فيه لمصلحة الطرف الآخر يتوقف لزومهما على رضاه (ر)) رد المحتار ١٩/٤).
أما إذا لم تكن المخالفة إلى خير وأفضل من كل وجه، بل من وجه دون وجه لا ينعقد العقد إلا بموافقة الطرف الآخر، كما لو كانت المخالفة من كل وجه، وذلك كما لو أوجب البائع بعشرين ديناراً نقداً فقبل المشتري بأربعين نسيئة إلى شهر مثلاً، فلا ينعقد العقد، بل يعتبر القبول المخالف إيجاباً يحتاج إلى قبول ذلك لأنه وإن كان الثمن المقبول أزيد مقداراً، قد تكون حاجة البائع أو رغبته في النقد العاجل، أي في الناحية التي خولفت فيفوت غرضه .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The formation of the contract is based on acceptance from the other party who was the offeror, so the acceptor becomes an offeror and the offeror becomes an acceptor. However, if the acceptance differs from the offer in a way that is entirely beneficial to the offeror, it does not prevent formation because in reality it is not a difference but rather an increase in agreement, and consideration is not given to outward forms but to substantive intentions. So if the buyer offered ten dinars and the seller accepted less than that, or the seller offered ten and the buyer accepted more than that, that is in reality an acceptance of the amount contained in the offer with a commitment to reduce or increase it for the benefit of the offeror. The sale is concluded with the price specified in the offer, and the binding nature of the reduction or increase depends on the consent of the offeror because the rule is: the reduction of the consideration or its increase for the benefit of the other party depends for its binding effect on his consent (see Rad al-Muhtar 4/19). But if the variation is not entirely beneficial from all angles, but rather from one angle and not another, the contract is not concluded without the consent of the other party, just as if the variation is from all angles. For example, if the seller offered twenty dinars cash and the buyer accepted forty on credit for one month, then the contract is not concluded; rather, the differing acceptance is considered an offer that requires acceptance, because even though the accepted price is greater in amount, the seller's need or desire may be for prompt cash, i.e., in the aspect that was contradicted, so his purpose is frustrated.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage discusses a key Hanafi rule in contract formation: when acceptance deviates from the offer in a way that is purely beneficial to the offeror, it is not considered a counter-offer but rather acceptance with a beneficial modification. The contract is formed on the original offer's terms, and the beneficial variation (reduction or increase in price) is subject to the offeror's consent. Hanafi jurists base this on the maxim that substance overrides form. The passage provides two scenarios: (1) buyer offers 10, seller accepts less → contract on 10, reduction effective only if buyer agrees; (2) seller offers 10, buyer accepts more → contract on 10, increase effective only if seller agrees. If the variation is not entirely beneficial (e.g., different payment terms), the differing acceptance is treated as a new offer requiring acceptance.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): The initial offer made by one party to a contract.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the other party, which may include a beneficial variation as discussed.
مخالفة إلى خير (mukhalafa ila khayr): A variation in acceptance that is entirely beneficial to the offeror from all perspectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller offers to sell his car for 10,000 dinars cash, and the buyer accepts by saying 'I accept, but I will pay 12,000 dinars over two months,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The contract is invalid because the acceptance differs from the offer.
B) The contract is concluded at 10,000 dinars cash, and the additional 2,000 dinars is binding only if the seller consents.
C) The contract is concluded at 12,000 dinars on credit because the buyer offered more.
D) The acceptance is considered a new offer requiring the seller's acceptance.

Key Takeaway
A counter-offer that is entirely beneficial to the offeror constitutes acceptance forming the contract on original terms, with the benefit contingent on the offeror's consent.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

6 min
Decisiveness of Offer and Acceptance (Jazm al-Iradatayn)
Explaining the requirement of decisiveness in offer and acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of jazm al-iradatayn to determine whether a given contract expression is valid, and distinguish valid suspensive conditions from invalid promises.

Core Idea
An offer or acceptance expressed in the future tense creates no contract — only a definite, decisive utterance binds.

In Hanafi contract law, the expression of offer and acceptance must be completely decisive, reflecting a present intention to be bound. Any hint of hesitation or postponement voids the contract. This lesson unpacks the rule of jazm al-iradatayn, its rationale, and its relationship with suspensive conditions, future dating, and options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement
Jazm al-iradatayn means the offer and acceptance must be definitive, with no hesitation or delay. If there is, the intention to commit is absent.
2
Analyze the example of future tense
The text gives the example: 'I will sell' or 'I will buy' – these do not form a contract because the future tense indicates a promise, not a present commitment.
Even if the other party accepts immediately, still no contract.
3
Distinguish valid ta'liq, idafa, and khiyar
Suspending the contract on a future condition, adding its effect to the future, or granting an option does not contradict decisiveness because the contract itself is concluded definitively; these are built-in terms.
These are allowed only in contracts that admit them.
4
Apply the modern legislative exception
In some jurisdictions, like Syria, the civil code has made certain promises to sell binding if they meet specific conditions, overriding the classical rule. A mufti must be aware of this.
The classical rule remains the baseline in the absence of contrary legislation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً : جزم الإرادتين :
والمراد به أن تكون صيغة الإيجاب والقبول مفيدة للبت في العقد بصورة لا تردد معها ولا تسويف وإلا كانت نية الارتباط منتفية لأن التردد في حكم الرفض .
ومن الواضح أنه إذا انتفت دلالة الصيغة على وقوع الارتباط والتعاقد، فلا عقد ولا التزام. وعلى هذا يقرر الفقهاء أن الوعد بالبيع لا ينعقد به البيع ولا يلزم صاحبه قضاء(۱)
ومن صورة الوعد أن تقترن عبارة الإيجاب أو القبول بأداة تأخير وتسويف، كحرف السين أو سوف. فلو قال البائع لمن يستام الشيء: سأبيعكه بكذا، أو قال المستام سوف أشتريه فإنَّ البيع لا ينعقد بذلك ولو قبل الطرف الآخر قبولاً باتا (۲) (ر: المجلة / ۱۷۱) .
(1) يلحظ هنا أن المواد / ۲۲۰ - ۲۲۷ من قانون الملكية العقارية السابق لدينا ذي الرقم /٣٣٣٩/ قد قضت بأن الوعد ببيع العقار ملزم للواعد إذ حصل بشرائط ومراسيم عينها القانون المذكور وهو موجب للتسجيل الجبري في العقار على اسم الشخص الموعود إذا ادعى هذا على المالك الناكل عن وعده. فالحكم الفقهي في أن الوعد بالعقد غير ملزم أصبح في بلادنا معدلاً بهذا القانون في حق العقار. والواقع أن الوعد ببيع العقار كما رسمه القانون المذكور هو في الحقيقة تعهد من المالك بالبيع متى طلب راغب الشراء خلال مدة معينة وهذا التعهد يتفق عليه الطرفان، وليس مجرد وعد. ثم جاء القانون المدني السوري الذي صَدّر خلال الطبعة الثالثة من هذا المدخل، فعمم هذا الحكم على كل وعد بعقد فاعتبره ملزماً للواعد إذا استوفى شرائطه التي قررها القانون المذكور مهما كان نوع العقد الموعود. ومن الواضح أن عقد الزواج لا يدخل تحت هذا التعميم، لأنه خاضع لقانون الأحوال الشخصية الذي لا يجعل الوعد بالزواج ملزماً.
(۲) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن العقد قد يقع معلقاً وجوده على وجود شرط، كما لو علق الكفيل كفالته على سفر المدين بأن قال : إذا سافر فقد كفلته . وقد يعقد العقد مضافاً بدء حكمه إلى المستقبل، كما لو آجر إنسان داره سنة تبتدىء من أول الشهر القادم. وقد يعقد العقد بخيار لأحد العاقدين كما لو تبايعا على أن يكون المشتري مخيراً إلى ثلاثة أيام فيكون له خلالها حق في نقض البيع ورد المبيع، وهو المسمى: خيار الشرط (ر: ف ۷/۳ تاسعاً). فالتعليق أو الإضافة في العقود التي تقبلهما، وكذا خيار الشرط في العقود التي يجري فيها، لا ينافي الجزم الواجب وجوده في صيغة العقد، كما لا يخفى
Third: Decisiveness of the Two Wills. By this is meant that the form of offer and acceptance must indicate a definitive conclusion of the contract in a manner with no hesitation or delay; otherwise, the intention of commitment is absent because hesitation is like rejection. It is clear that if the expression does not indicate the occurrence of commitment and contracting, there is no contract and no obligation. On this basis, jurists establish that a promise to sell does not effect the sale, nor is the promisor bound judicially. One form of promise is when the expression of offer or acceptance is accompanied by a tool of delay or procrastination, like the letter 'sin' or 'sawfa'. So if the seller says to the prospective buyer: 'I will sell it to you for such-and-such,' or the prospective buyer says 'I will buy it,' then the sale is not concluded by that, even if the other party gives an immediate acceptance. (See Majalla, Article 171).
(1) Note here that Articles 220-227 of our previous Real Property Law No. 3339 ruled that a promise to sell real estate is binding on the promisor when it occurs with conditions and formalities specified by that law, and it is subject to compulsory registration in the name of the promisee if the latter claims against the owner who reneges on his promise. Thus, the fiqh ruling that a promise to contract is not binding has been modified in our country by this law concerning real estate. In fact, the promise to sell real estate as outlined by that law is actually a commitment by the owner to sell whenever the buyer requests during a specified period, and this commitment is agreed upon by both parties, not a mere promise. Then came the Syrian Civil Code, which was issued during the third edition of this introduction, and generalized this ruling to every promise to contract, making it binding on the promisor if it fulfills its conditions as stipulated by that law, regardless of the type of promised contract. It is clear that marriage contract is not included in this generalization because it is subject to the Personal Status Law, which does not make a promise to marry binding.
(2) It should be noted here that a contract may occur suspended on the existence of a condition, as when a guarantor suspends his guarantee on the debtor's travel by saying: 'If he travels, I have stood surety for him.' Or a contract may be added with the start of its effect to the future, as when one rents out his house for a year starting from the first of next month. Or a contract may be concluded with an option for one of the contracting parties, as when they buy and sell on the condition that the buyer has an option for three days, during which he has the right to cancel the sale and return the item; this is called 'khiyar al-shart' (option of condition). So suspension or addition in contracts that admit them, and likewise khiyar al-shart in contracts in which it is valid, do not contradict the decisiveness that must exist in the contract form, as is obvious.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi requirement that the expression of offer and acceptance must be definitive (jazm) and free from hesitation or postponement. If the expression indicates a future intention (e.g., 'I will sell') or a conditional promise, no contract is formed. Hanafi jurists distinguish this from valid suspensive conditions (ta'liq), addition to the future (idafa), and options (khiyar) that are part of the contract structure but do not indicate indecision. The footnote (1) discusses modern Syrian legislation that modifies the classical rule for promises to sell real estate, making them binding under certain conditions. Footnote (2) clarifies that suspensive conditions, future addition, and options do not violate decisiveness because they are built into the contract definitively.

Key Terms
جزم الإرادتين (jazm al-iradatayn): The requirement that the offer and acceptance expressions be definitive and decisive, without any indication of hesitation or delay.
الوعد (al-wa'd): A promise to enter into a contract in the future, which does not create a binding contract in classical Hanafi fiqh.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the contract's existence on a future condition, which is permissible in certain contracts and does not contradict decisiveness.
الإضافة (al-idafa): Adding the start of the contract's effect to a future date, allowed in contracts like ijarah (lease).
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option granted to one or both parties to cancel the contract within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view explained in this passage, which of the following expressions would NOT form a valid contract?
A) The seller says, 'I sell you this car for 10,000 dinars,' and the buyer says, 'I accept.'
B) The seller says, 'I will sell you this car for 10,000 dinars,' and the buyer says, 'I accept.'
C) The seller says, 'I sell you this car for 10,000 dinars on condition that you pay within a month,' and the buyer says, 'I accept.'
D) The seller says, 'I sell you this car for 10,000 dinars,' and the buyer says, 'I accept, with the option to return it within three days.'

Key Takeaway
The decisive expression of offer and acceptance is the bedrock of a valid contract; any future or hesitant language reduces it to a mere promise.

The Requirement of Decisive Intent in Contract Formation
Explains the requirement of decisive intent in contract form and the distinction between promise and contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a binding contract (عقد) and a non-binding promise (وعد) in Hanafi fiqh, and state the requirement of decisive intent (الجزم) in the form (صيغة العقد).

Core Idea
A promise to sell is not a contract — decisive intent at the moment of expression is essential.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi by Mustafa al-Zarqa lays out the fundamental Hanafi principle that a valid contract requires a form that unequivocally indicates mutual commitment. A mere promise, even if accepted, does not create legal obligations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when evaluating whether a transaction is binding or merely promissory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal principle
The passage states that if the form does not indicate mutual commitment and contracting, no contract exists. This is the starting rule for all contract analysis.
2
Distinguish promise from contract
The author explicitly contrasts a promise (wa'd) with a binding contract. Note the examples: future tense markers (sin, sawfa) in offer or acceptance indicate a promise, not a contract, even if the other party accepts definitively.
Reference: Majalla 171.
3
Analyze the requirement of decisive intent (jazm)
The preferred form is past tense (e.g., 'I sold') because it clearly indicates present decisive intent. However, present tense is acceptable if intended for the present moment. The key is that the expression must be jāzim (decisive) – a clear, present commitment.
The author notes that even imperative phrases like 'Take it' can suffice if they imply an offer and acceptance.
4
Apply the principle to scenarios
Given a statement like 'I will sell you this car next week,' determine that no contract is formed at present. It is a promise. In contrast, 'I sell you this car now' (in present tense) forms a contract if accepted.
5
Note the exception from modern law
The passage mentions that Syrian civil law has made promises to sell real estate binding under specific conditions. This is a positive law modification and does not change the underlying fiqhi principle, but a mufti must be aware of it when advising in jurisdictions where such laws apply.
The author excludes marriage contracts from this modification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٠) صيغة العقد وما إليها
ومن الواضح أنه إذا انتفت دلالة الصيغة على وقوع الارتباط والتعاقد، فلا عقد ولا التزام. وعلى هذا يقرر الفقهاء أن الوعد بالبيع لا ينعقد به البيع ولا يلزم صاحبه قضاء(١)
ومن صورة الوعد أن تقترن عبارة الإيجاب أو القبول بأداة تأخير وتسويف، كحرف السين أو سوف. فلو قال البائع لمن يستام الشيء: سأبيعكه بكذا، أو قال المستام سوف أشتريه فإنَّ البيع لا ينعقد بذلك ولو قبل الطرف الآخر قبولاً باتا (٢) (ر: المجلة / ١٧١) .

(١) يلحظ هنا أن المواد / ٢٢٠ - ٢٢٧ من قانون الملكية العقارية السابق لدينا ذي الرقم /٣٣٣٩/ قد قضت بأن الوعد ببيع العقار ملزم للواعد إذ حصل بشرائط ومراسيم عينها القانون المذكور وهو موجب للتسجيل الجبري في العقار على اسم الشخص الموعود إذا ادعى هذا على المالك الناكل عن وعده.
فالحكم الفقهي في أن الوعد بالعقد غير ملزم أصبح في بلادنا معدلاً بهذا القانون في حق العقار.
والواقع [؟] ببيع العقار كما رسمه القانون المذكور هو في الحقيقة تعهد من المالك بالبيع متى طلب راغب الشراء خلال مدة معينة وهذا التعهد يتفق عليه الطرفان، وليس مجرد وعد.
ثم جاء القانون المدني السوري الذي صَدّر خلال الطبعة الثالثة من هذا المدخل، فعمم هذا الحكم على كل وعد بعقد فاعتبره ملزماً للواعد إذا استوفى شرائطه التي قررها القانون المذكور مهما كان نوع العقد الموعود.

(٢) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن العقد قد يقع معلقاً وجوده على وجود شرط، كما لو علق الكفيل كفالته على سفر المدين بأن قال : إذا سافر فقد كفلته . وقد يعقد العقد مضافاً بدء حكمه إلى المستقبل، كما لو آجر إنسان داره سنة تبتدىء من أول الشهر القادم. وقد يعقد العقد بخيار لأحد العاقدين كما لو تبايعا على أن يكون المشتري مخيراً إلى ثلاثة أيام فيكون له خلالها حق في نقض البيع ورد المبيع، وهو المسمى: خيار الشرط (ر: ف ٧/٣ تاسعاً).

ففي عقد البيع لو أوجب المشتري بقوله : هذا عليَّ بألف فقال البائع قبلت أو أوجب البائع بقوله : هو لك بألف إن وافقك أو أعجبك أو إن أردت فقال المشتري : وافقني أو أعجبني أو أردت ينعقد البيع بكل هذه العبارات (ر: «فتح القدير للكمال ابن الهمام ٤٥٨/٥ - ٤٥٩ . حتى إنَّ الفقهاء قد اعتبروا صيغة الأمر وغيرها من العبارات كافية إذا كانت تنبىء عن إيجاب أو قبول مقدر يقتضيه المعنى ويستلزمه كما لو قال : اشتريت منك هذا بكذا فقال المالك : (خذه أو يبارك الله لك

فإرادة العاقدين في العقد المعلق أو المضاف أو المخير فيه مبتوتة ليست كإرادة من: يعد بالبيع وعداً أو يقول : سوف أبيع ولكن العقد المعلق مجزوم به من قبل العاقد لا حالاً، بل عند وجود الشرط الخارجي المعلق عليه ؛ والعقد المضاف مجزوم به للحال، إلا أن ابتداء حكمه مؤخر إلى زمن معين؛ والعقد مع الخيار مبتوت أيضاً في أصل انعقاده، وليس الخيار إلا حقاً ممنوحاً لأحد العاقدين في فسخ العقد القائم. فكل ذلك لا ينافي الجزم بالمعنى المقصود هنا في صيغة العقد.
٤١٠
Chapter (30): The Form of the Contract and What Pertains to It
It is clear that if the indication of the form points to the occurrence of connection and contracting is absent, then there is no contract and no obligation. On this basis, the jurists establish that a promise to sell does not effect a sale and is not binding upon the promisor in a court of law. (1)
One form of promise is that the expression of offer or acceptance is accompanied by a tool of delay and procrastination, like the letter 'sin' or 'sawfa'. So if the seller says to the prospective buyer: 'I will sell it to you for such-and-such,' or if the buyer says: 'I will buy it,' then the sale is not effected by that, even if the other party accepts definitively. (2) (Ref: Majalla 171).

(1) Note here that articles 220-227 of the former Land Property Law no. 3339 in our country stipulated that a promise to sell real estate is binding upon the promisor if it occurs with the conditions and procedures specified by the said law, and it necessitates compulsory registration of the property in the name of the person who was promised, if he claims against the owner who reneges on his promise.
Thus the fiqhi ruling that a promise to contract is non-binding was amended in our country by this law regarding real estate.
In reality, [the promise] to sell real estate as outlined by the said law is in fact an undertaking by the owner to sell whenever the person desiring purchase requests it within a specified period, and this undertaking is agreed upon by both parties, and it is not a mere promise.
Then came the Syrian Civil Code, which was published during the third edition of this introduction, and it generalized this ruling to every promise to contract, considering it binding upon the promisor if it fulfills its conditions stipulated by the said law, regardless of the type of promised contract.
It is clear that the marriage contract does not fall under this generalization, because it is subject to the Personal Status Law, which does not make the promise to marry binding.

(2) It is noted in this regard that a contract may be suspended in its existence upon the existence of a condition, as when the guarantor conditions his guarantee on the debtor's travel by saying: 'If he travels, then I have guaranteed him.' And a contract may be added (future) in the beginning of its effect to the future, as if a person leases his house for a year starting from the first of next month. And a contract may be made with an option for one of the parties, as if they trade with the condition that the buyer has the option for three days, during which he has the right to rescind the sale and return the sold item; this is called 'option of condition' (khiyar al-shart) (ref: section 7/3 ninth).
So suspension or addition in contracts that admit them, and likewise the option of condition in contracts in which it occurs, does not contradict the decisiveness required in the form of the contract, as is obvious.

Section 5/3 - The general form preferred by the jurists for offer and acceptance to indicate decisive intent is the past tense, such as: 'I sold' and 'I bought' and 'I pawned' and 'I took in pawn' and 'I married' and 'I got married,' etc.
The word 'I accepted' or 'I agreed' or similar after the mention of the offer suffices and dispenses with repeating what preceded in the offer.
However, the past tense is not a condition; rather it is better than others because it indicates decisive creation (insha') in contracts.
So if both parties express in the present tense intending the present moment, the contract is concluded; as if he says 'I am selling to you now,' and the other says 'I am buying now' (Ref: Majalla 170).
Similarly in all transactions, every expression that clearly shows the decisive intent of the two parties in creating contracts according to the subject of each contract.
In the contract of sale, if the buyer offers by saying 'This is upon me for a thousand,' and the seller says 'I accept,' or the seller offers by saying 'It is yours for a thousand if it pleases you or if you like it or if you want,' and the buyer says 'It pleases me' or 'I like it' or 'I wanted it,' the sale is concluded with all these expressions (Ref: Fath al-Qadir by Kamal ibn al-Humam 5/458-459). Even the jurists have considered the imperative form and other expressions sufficient if they indicate an implicit offer or acceptance that the meaning necessitates and entails, as when one says 'I bought this from you for such-and-such' and the owner says 'Take it' or 'May Allah bless you.'

So the intent of the parties in a suspended, future, or optional contract is decisive, not like the intent of one who promises to sell or says 'I will sell.' Rather, the suspended contract is definitely intended by the party not at present, but when the external condition upon which it is suspended exists; the future contract is decisively intended for the present, except that the beginning of its effect is delayed to a specific time; and the contract with option is also decisive in its original conclusion, and the option is only a right granted to one of the parties to rescind the existing contract. So all this does not contradict the decisiveness meant here in the form of the contract.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the requirements for a valid contract in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on the form (sighah) of the contract that expresses the parties' intent. The passage begins by stating the fundamental principle: if the form of the contract does not indicate a real connection and mutual commitment, then no contract exists. On this basis, a mere promise (wa‘d) to sell does not constitute a sale and is not enforceable in court. This is contrasted with a binding contract, which requires a decisive expression of intent. One example of a non-binding promise is when the offer or acceptance includes a future tense marker like 'sin' or 'sawfa' (e.g., 'I will sell' or 'I will buy'). Even if the other party immediately accepts, no contract is formed.

The author then notes that modern Syrian law has modified this classical fiqhi ruling for certain contracts, making promises to sell real estate binding under specific conditions. However, marriage contracts are excluded from this exception. This shows how positive law can interact with fiqh, but the underlying fiqhi principle remains.

Next, the passage distinguishes between a promise and a contract that is suspended, future, or subject to an option. These types of contracts still involve a decisive intent (jazm) at the time of the contract, even though their effects are delayed or conditional. For example, a sale with a condition (khiyar al-shart) gives the buyer the right to rescind within a specified period, but the contract itself is concluded with decisive intent. Similarly, a contract added to a future date (e.g., a lease starting next month) is decisively intended now, even though its legal effects begin later. This is different from a mere promise, which lacks any present intent to be bound.

The passage then explains the preferred form for offer and acceptance: the past tense (e.g., 'I sold', 'I bought') is recommended because it clearly indicates present intent to create a legal obligation. However, the past tense is not a condition; the present tense is acceptable if it is meant for the immediate present (e.g., 'I am selling now'). Even imperative forms like 'Take it' can suffice if they imply an offer and acceptance. The key is that the expression must clearly show the decisive intent of both parties to create the contract.

Finally, the author reiterates that in contracts with conditions, future commencement, or options, the intent is decisive—unlike a simple promise. The decisive intent is present at the moment of the contract, even if some effects are postponed. This clarifies that such contracts are valid and distinguishable from non-binding promises.

Key Terms
صيغة العقد (sīghat al-‘aqd): The verbal expression that indicates the intention of the contracting parties to create a legal obligation.
الوعد (al-wa‘d): A mere promise that does not create a binding contract and is not enforceable in court (unless modified by law).
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The first declaration of intent to contract, made by the offeror.
القبول (al-qabūl): The acceptance of the offer, made by the offeree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a promise (wa‘d) to sell a property?
A) It creates a binding contract immediately.
B) It is not binding and no sale is formed.
C) It is binding if the price is specified.
D) It becomes a sale if accepted within three days.

Key Takeaway
A contract requires decisive intent expressed in the form; a promise, even if accepted, does not create a binding obligation in classical Hanafi fiqh.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Suspended, Future, and Optional Contracts: Preserving Decisive Intent
Clarifies that contracts with conditions, future commencement, or options are valid and contain decisive intent, unlike promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a contract with a condition (khiyar al-shart), future commencement, or suspension still meets the requirement of decisive intent, and distinguish such contracts from a mere promise.

Core Idea
A suspended or option contract is valid because the intent is decisive at the moment—only its effect is delayed or conditional.

This passage clarifies a common point of confusion: contracts that include conditions, future start dates, or cancellation options are still valid because the parties' intent is decisive at the time of the contract. This distinguishes them from mere promises, where no present commitment exists. A mufti must understand this distinction to correctly advise on the validity of such agreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three special contract types
The passage mentions suspended (mu'allaq), future (mudaf), and option (khiyar al-shart) contracts. Understand each: suspended contract's existence depends on a future condition; future contract's effect begins at a later date; option contract gives one party the right to cancel within a period.
Examples: 'If he travels, I have guaranteed him' (suspended); 'I lease my house starting next month' (future); 'You have the option for three days' (option).
2
Understand the potential concern
Why might someone think these lack decisive intent? Because the full legal effect is not immediate. For example, a future lease does not give possession now; an option contract can be undone by the buyer. This might seem like the parties are not fully committed.
3
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author states unequivocally that the intent in these contracts is 'decisive' (مبتوتة) and not like that of a promise. The suspended contract is definitely intended to be binding when the condition occurs; the future contract is intended now, only the start of effect is delayed; the option contract is concluded with decisive intent, with an additional right to rescind.
The option is a separate right, not a lack of commitment.
4
Contrast with a mere promise
In a promise, there is no present intent to be bound; the promisor only intends to do something in the future. In these contracts, the parties intend to be bound now, even if some elements are conditional or future.
Example: 'I will sell you this next month' (promise) vs 'I sell you this now, but possession is next month' (future contract with decisive intent).
5
Apply to real scenarios
Practice: Given a scenario where a buyer says 'I buy this house, but I have the right to cancel within one week,' analyze whether this is a valid contract. (Answer: Yes, it is a valid sale with khiyar al-shart.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٠) صيغة العقد وما إليها
ومن الواضح أنه إذا انتفت دلالة الصيغة على وقوع الارتباط والتعاقد، فلا عقد ولا التزام. وعلى هذا يقرر الفقهاء أن الوعد بالبيع لا ينعقد به البيع ولا يلزم صاحبه قضاء(١)
ومن صورة الوعد أن تقترن عبارة الإيجاب أو القبول بأداة تأخير وتسويف، كحرف السين أو سوف. فلو قال البائع لمن يستام الشيء: سأبيعكه بكذا، أو قال المستام سوف أشتريه فإنَّ البيع لا ينعقد بذلك ولو قبل الطرف الآخر قبولاً باتا (٢) (ر: المجلة / ١٧١) .

(١) يلحظ هنا أن المواد / ٢٢٠ - ٢٢٧ من قانون الملكية العقارية السابق لدينا ذي الرقم /٣٣٣٩/ قد قضت بأن الوعد ببيع العقار ملزم للواعد إذ حصل بشرائط ومراسيم عينها القانون المذكور وهو موجب للتسجيل الجبري في العقار على اسم الشخص الموعود إذا ادعى هذا على المالك الناكل عن وعده.
فالحكم الفقهي في أن الوعد بالعقد غير ملزم أصبح في بلادنا معدلاً بهذا القانون في حق العقار.
والواقع [؟] ببيع العقار كما رسمه القانون المذكور هو في الحقيقة تعهد من المالك بالبيع متى طلب راغب الشراء خلال مدة معينة وهذا التعهد يتفق عليه الطرفان، وليس مجرد وعد.
ثم جاء القانون المدني السوري الذي صَدّر خلال الطبعة الثالثة من هذا المدخل، فعمم هذا الحكم على كل وعد بعقد فاعتبره ملزماً للواعد إذا استوفى شرائطه التي قررها القانون المذكور مهما كان نوع العقد الموعود.
ومن الواضح أن عقد الزواج لا يدخل تحت هذا التعميم، لأنه خاضع لقانون الأحوال الشخصية الذي لا يجعل الوعد بالزواج ملزماً.

(٢) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن العقد قد يقع معلقاً وجوده على وجود شرط، كما لو علق الكفيل كفالته على سفر المدين بأن قال : إذا سافر فقد كفلته . وقد يعقد العقد مضافاً بدء حكمه إلى المستقبل، كما لو آجر إنسان داره سنة تبتدىء من أول الشهر القادم. وقد يعقد العقد بخيار لأحد العاقدين كما لو تبايعا على أن يكون المشتري مخيراً إلى ثلاثة أيام فيكون له خلالها حق في نقض البيع ورد المبيع، وهو المسمى: خيار الشرط (ر: ف ٧/٣ تاسعاً).
فالتعليق أو الإضافة في العقود التي تقبلهما، وكذا خيار الشرط في العقود التي يجري فيها، لا ينافي الجزم الواجب وجوده في صيغة العقد، كما لا يخفى

Chapter (30): The Form of the Contract and What Pertains to It

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa discusses the requirements for a valid contract in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on the form (sighah) of the contract that expresses the parties' intent. The passage begins by stating the fundamental principle: if the form of the contract does not indicate a real connection and mutual commitment, then no contract exists. On this basis, a mere promise (wa‘d) to sell does not constitute a sale and is not enforceable in court. This is contrasted with a binding contract, which requires a decisive expression of intent. One example of a non-binding promise is when the offer or acceptance includes a future tense marker like 'sin' or 'sawfa' (e.g., 'I will sell' or 'I will buy'). Even if the other party immediately accepts, no contract is formed.

The author then notes that modern Syrian law has modified this classical fiqhi ruling for certain contracts, making promises to sell real estate binding under specific conditions. However, marriage contracts are excluded from this exception. This shows how positive law can interact with fiqh, but the underlying fiqhi principle remains.

Next, the passage distinguishes between a promise and a contract that is suspended, future, or subject to an option. These types of contracts still involve a decisive intent (jazm) at the time of the contract, even though their effects are delayed or conditional. For example, a sale with a condition (khiyar al-shart) gives the buyer the right to rescind within a specified period, but the contract itself is concluded with decisive intent. Similarly, a contract added to a future date (e.g., a lease starting next month) is decisively intended now, even though its legal effects begin later. This is different from a mere promise, which lacks any present intent to be bound.

The passage then explains the preferred form for offer and acceptance: the past tense (e.g., 'I sold', 'I bought') is recommended because it clearly indicates present intent to create a legal obligation. However, the past tense is not a condition; the present tense is acceptable if it is meant for the immediate present (e.g., 'I am selling now'). Even imperative forms like 'Take it' can suffice if they imply an offer and acceptance. The key is that the expression must clearly show the decisive intent of both parties to create the contract.

Finally, the author reiterates that in contracts with conditions, future commencement, or options, the intent is decisive—unlike a simple promise. The decisive intent is present at the moment of the contract, even if some effects are postponed. This clarifies that such contracts are valid and distinguishable from non-binding promises.

Key Terms
صيغة العقد (sīghat al-‘aqd): The verbal expression that indicates the intention of the contracting parties to create a legal obligation.
الوعد (al-wa‘d): A mere promise that does not create a binding contract and is not enforceable in court (unless modified by law).
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The first declaration of intent to contract, made by the offeror.
القبول (al-qabūl): The acceptance of the offer, made by the offeree.
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Suspension of a contract's existence upon the occurrence of a future condition.
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa): Adding the start of the contract's effect to a future date.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): An option to cancel a contract within a specified period, agreed by the parties.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the status of a contract that begins next month?
A) It is invalid because the future tense indicates lack of decisiveness.
B) It is valid because the parties intended the contract now, only the effect is delayed.
C) It is a promise, not a contract.
D) It is valid only if the rent is paid in advance.

Key Takeaway
Contracts with conditions, future commencement, or options are valid and contain decisive intent; they are not mere promises.

The Maxim of Ibaha and Badhl: Why Mu'ātāt Works for Sales but Not Marriage
Legal analysis of mu'ātāt in contracts and the maxim limiting ibaha to wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ibaha wa al-badhl innama yajriyan fi al-amwal la fi al-mut'ah al-jinsiyyah' to evaluate the validity of mu'ātāt in any contract, distinguishing financial from personal contracts.

Core Idea
Mu'ātāt works for money, not marriage—property can be made lawful by consent, but sexual pleasure requires a verbal contract.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work explains why the Hanafi school accepts informal exchange (mu'ātāt) in sales and other financial contracts but unanimously rejects it in marriage. The key lies in a legal maxim: permission and dispensation apply to property, not to sexual pleasure. In this lesson, you will learn how this maxim governs the validity of contracts and why marriage requires explicit verbal offer and acceptance.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the maxim: 'al-ibaha wa al-badhl innama yajriyan fi al-amwal la fi al-mut'ah al-jinsiyyah'. Note that it applies to all contracts, distinguishing between those involving property (wealth) and those involving personal rights (like sexual pleasure).
The maxim is a 'dabt' (restrictive sub-maxim) of the broader principle that custom is authoritative in financial matters.
2
Identify the two furu' cases
The passage contrasts two cases: sale (and similar financial exchanges) vs. marriage. Recognize that each is a branch (far') of the maxim.
Other financial contracts like lease, currency exchange, loan, and gift are also covered.
3
Apply the maxim to each case to derive the ruling
For sale: since property is involved, consent (taradi) is sufficient, and mu'ātāt is valid. For marriage: since sexual pleasure is involved, mere consent is insufficient to distinguish marriage from fornication; verbal offer/acceptance is required.
The Hanafi proof for sales is based on Qur'anic permission, custom, and absence of transmitted practice of verbal utterance.
4
Understand the rationale behind the distinction
In financial transactions, consent is the sole distinguisher between lawful taking and theft/Theft. In intimate relationships, both marriage and illicit relationships involve consent, so an additional formal element—verbal contract—is needed to clarify intent and prevent doubt.
This rationale is the 'talil' (legal reasoning) behind the maxim.
5
Note the cross-madhhab consensus
All Islamic schools agree that mu'ātāt is invalid in marriage. The disagreement is only about its validity in sales, with Hanafis allowing it based on custom.
The passage cites al-Qarafi (Maliki) and Ibn Qudamah (Hanbali) who also affirm the marriage exception.

أن الإباحة والبذل إنما يجريان في الأموال لا في المتعة الجنسية.
ر كتاب الدعوى في رد المحتار ٣٣٤/٢ والبدائع ٢٢٧/٦). وذلك لأن الشريطة الشرعية في استحلال مال الغير هي رضى صاحبه ببذله إما مجاناً أو بعوض. فلو أباح الإنسان ماله لأحد حل هذا المال للمباح له.
بخلاف ما لو أباحت المرأة نفسها لرجل، فإن المتعة لا تحل بينهما بهذه الإباحة، بل هي السفاح الممنوع.

الشرعي.
أما التعاشر الاستمتاعي بين الجنسين الرجل والمرأة فإنّ له حالتين إحداهما مشروعة وهي النكاح والأخرى جريمة ممنوعة وهي المخادنة والسفاح. وجميع نتائج النكاح الخطيرة في الأموال والأنساب وسائر حقوق الأسرة تثبت كلها الحالة المشروعة وتنتفي في الحالة الممنوعة. ومن الواضح أن الحالتين أي الزواج والمخادنة تشبه إحداهما الأخرى في مظهرها ؛ وليس التراضي هو المميز بين الحالتين، لأنه موجود في كل منهما، وهو غير كاف في هذه المعاشرة بين الجنسين، فإن التراضي إنما كان كافياً في المعاوضة المالية لأن الأموال يجري فيها البذل والإباحة كما تقدم.
فلو جاز التعاطي في عقد الزواج - بأن يدفع رجل إلى امرأة مبلغاً من المال كمهر بقصد الزواج، فتتابعه دون أن يتلفظا بعقد وتعيش معه ويتعاشرا كأزواج - لما كان في هذا التعاطي دلالة ظاهرة على أكثر من التراضي على المعاشرة، أما قصد الزواج فلا يظهر للناظر بهذا التعاطي وإن مجرد التراضي على المعاشرة لا يكفي في تمييز النكاح عن السفاح كما سَلَف بيانه، بل لا بد من ظهور التراضي على غَرَض الزواج -
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. Mu'ātāt (mutual giving) is applied by our jurists in the contract of sale and what is similar to it from commutative contracts such as lease, currency exchange, rescission, and likewise the contract of compromise for a consideration, and partition; all are in the meaning of commutative contracts. It also applies in non-commutative contracts such as loan and gift (Majallah articles 804, 839). However, all Islamic schools of jurisprudence are agreed upon the non-application of mu'ātāt in the contract of marriage, because marriage has a religious consideration connected to creed in the manner of making marital pleasure lawful; nothing suffices in it in place of utterance. Also, the Law has regard in distinguishing legitimate marriage between a man and a woman from a licentious relationship—a distinction that is not manifested and from whose path doubt is removed except by verbal contract, because if mu'ātāt were to occur in marriage without utterance, the type of intended relationship would not be evident: whether it is marriage or illicit friendship, as previously indicated. (F 6/27 vol.). And writing enters into utterance, so it is considered in a contract between absent parties like face-to-face speech. And: But the utterance in the offer and acceptance of the marriage contract may be literal or metaphorical: thus marriage is contracted, for example, with the word 'gift', and with every word that conveys the meaning of permanent transfer of ownership, as if a woman says to a man, 'I gift myself to you for a dower of such-and-such,' and the man accepts in the presence of witnesses, then the marriage is contracted as previously stated (Radd al-Muhtar 2/271). = Our proof: Verily Allah permitted sale and did not specify its modality. So it is obligatory to refer in it to custom, just as reference was made to it in taking possession and acquisition. The Muslims in their markets and sales are upon that. And it has not been transmitted from the Prophet nor his Companions, despite the frequency of sales among them, that they used offer and acceptance; had they used them it would have been widely transmitted, and had it been a condition, it would have been obligatory to transmit it. (end of quote). Ibn Qudamah has come with valuable words and understanding in this context; see al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir, vol. 4, beginning of sales. (1) The eminent Shihab al-Din al-Qarafi al-Maliki has composed in his book al-Furuq a comprehensive discussion on the difference between sale and marriage in terms of conclusion by mu'ātāt in the former but not the latter, and between the juristic schools and their proofs in that, and he said: 416 Chapter (30) The Form of Contract and Related Matters. So these texts of the scholars, despite their differences, none has said anything for mu'ātāt in marriage as they said it in sale. And al-Qarafi has elaborated in his Furuq, as did the author of Tahdhib al-Furuq printed in its margin in explaining this difference (see al-Furuq p. 157 and from its Tahdhib 3/143-145, and 180-182). I say: The essence of the legal wisdom in the non-application of mu'ātāt in the marriage contract appears from referring to a rule that the jurists establish, which is: Permissibility and dispensing (ibaha and badhl) only apply to wealth, not to sexual pleasure. (See Kitab al-Da'wa in Radd al-Muhtar 2/334 and al-Bada'i' 6/227). That is because the legal condition for making lawful the property of another is the owner's consent to dispense it either gratuitously or for consideration. So if a person makes his property permissible for someone, this property becomes lawful for the one to whom it is made permissible. Contrary to if a woman makes herself permissible for a man, then pleasure does not become lawful between them by this permission; rather it is prohibited fornication. On this basis, mu'ātāt is sufficient in a financial commutative contract because mutual consent to the dispensation therein is the distinguisher between the two states of taking property rightfully or wrongfully, and mu'ātāt conveys this mutual consent in a manner no less indicative than the utterance of offer and acceptance, thus achieving the legal purpose. As for intimate association between the two sexes, man and woman, it has two states: one legitimate, which is marriage, and the other a prohibited crime, which is illicit friendship and fornication. And all the serious consequences of marriage in property, lineage, and all family rights are established only in the legitimate state and negated in the prohibited state. It is obvious that the two states—marriage and illicit friendship—resemble each other in their outward appearance; and mutual consent is not the distinguisher between the two states because it exists in both, and it is not sufficient in this association between the sexes. For mutual consent was sufficient in financial transactions only because property involves dispensation and permission as previously stated. If mu'ātāt were allowed in the marriage contract—such that a man pays a woman an amount of money as a dower intending marriage, and she follows him without uttering a contract, and she lives with him and they cohabit as spouses—then this mu'ātāt would have no apparent indication beyond mutual consent to cohabitation. As for the intention of marriage, it does not appear to the onlooker through this mu'ātāt, and mere mutual consent to cohabitation is not sufficient to distinguish marriage from fornication, as previously explained. Rather, the appearance of mutual consent to the purpose of marriage is indispensable.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of the theory of contracts, specifically the concept of 'mu'ātāt' (informal mutual exchange without verbal offer and acceptance). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the Hanafi position that mu'ātāt is valid in contracts of sale and other commutative contracts such as lease, currency exchange, rescission, compromise for consideration, and partition. It also applies in non-commutative contracts like loan and gift, as per the Majallah. However, all Islamic schools of jurisprudence (including Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) unanimously agree that mu'ātāt is not valid in the marriage contract (nikah). The reason given is that marriage has a religious dimension tied to the creed in making marital intimacy lawful; utterance is essential. Moreover, the Law aims to clearly distinguish legitimate marriage from illicit relationships, and only a verbal contract can remove doubt. Writing (the marriage document) is considered equivalent to speech for absent parties. The author then notes that even the utterance in marriage can be literal or metaphorical—for example, the word 'gift' can be used if it implies permanent transfer of ownership. He cites Radd al-Muhtar and earlier discussions. Then he presents the Hanafi proof for allowing mu'ātāt in sales: Allah permitted sale but did not specify its form; thus custom ('urf) is the reference, just as in taking possession (qabd). Muslims in markets have always practiced mu'ātāt; if offer and acceptance were obligatory, it would have been widely transmitted from the Prophet and Companions, but it was not. He references Ibn Qudamah (Hanbali) and al-Qarafi (Maliki) who both agree that no one allowed mu'ātāt in marriage. The author then distills the legal wisdom into a rule: 'Permissibility and dispensing (ibaha and badhl) only apply to wealth, not to sexual pleasure.' This is because property can be made lawful by the owner's consent, but sexual pleasure requires a proper legal contract (marriage) to avoid fornication. Mutual consent (taradi) is the distinguisher in financial contracts, but in intimate relationships, both marriage and fornication involve consent, so additional formalities are needed to distinguish them. Therefore, mu'ātāt cannot reveal the intention of marriage; it only shows consent to cohabitation, which is ambiguous. Hence, verbal contract is necessary for marriage.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-mu'ātāt): Mutual giving and taking without express verbal offer and acceptance, based on custom
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage contract requiring verbal offer and acceptance for validity
الإباحة والبذل (al-ibaha wa al-badhl): Legal concept of making something permissible and dispensing it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is mu'ātāt (informal exchange) not valid in the marriage contract according to all Islamic schools?
A) Because marriage requires a written document.
B) Because mutual consent alone cannot distinguish marriage from fornication, and utterance is needed to clearly indicate the intention of permanent marital relationship.
C) Because the Prophet explicitly forbade mu'ātāt in marriage.
D) Because the dower must be specified verbally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that 'ibaha and badhl apply only to property' explains why mu'ātāt suffices for sales but not for marriage, which requires verbal contract.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Contract Formation: Verbal, Mutual Exchange, and Implied Situation
Explaining the methods of contract formation and the different rules for marriage and financial contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on ibahah to distinguish when mutual exchange suffices for contract formation and when verbal expression is required, and to identify contracts concluded by lisan al-hal.

Core Idea
In property, consent justifies transfer; in marriage, only verbal expression distinguishes lawful wedlock from illicit union.

Contracts in Islamic law can be formed in multiple ways: by clear words (the norm), by mutual exchange of goods without words (ta'ati), or by the implied language of the situation (lisan al-hal). This lesson unpacks the crucial distinction between financial contracts and the marriage contract: why ta'ati works for sales but not for marriage, and how lisan al-hal creates valid obligations. You will explore the governing maxim and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes of contract formation
From the passage, list the three ways a contract can be concluded: verbal expression (the original method), mutual exchange (ta'ati – action without words), and the language of the situation (lisan al-hal – circumstances indicating consent).
The author adds his own classification: lisan al-hal covers both ta'ati and other contextual indications.
2
Understand the key maxim on ibahah and badhl
Locate the maxim: 'الإباحة والبذل إنما يجريان في الأموال لا في المتعة الجنسية' (Permissibility and granting apply only to property, not to sexual enjoyment). This is the foundation for the different rules.
The maxim explains why mere consent suffices for property but not for sexual relations.
3
Apply the maxim to sale and marriage
Analyze: in a sale, the owner's consent (ibahah) to transfer property is the main condition. Ta'ati clearly indicates this consent. In marriage, sexual enjoyment is not made permissible by mere consent; the specific intention of marriage must be expressed verbally to distinguish it from fornication.
The passage emphasizes that consent is present in both lawful and illicit relationships, so it cannot be the differentiator.
4
Recognize lisan al-hal in deposit (wadi'ah)
Study the example: leaving goods before someone who remains silent creates a deposit contract by implication. The situation (silence + acceptance of goods) imposes obligations of safekeeping.
This is distinct from ta'ati because there is no exchange; the whole contract arises from the circumstances.
5
Summarize the hierarchy of contract formation methods
Conclude: the primary method is verbal expression. Ta'ati is permissible only in financial dealings (due to the maxit). Lisan al-hal operates in specific contexts where actions clearly indicate consent and obligations.
The mufti must identify which method applies based on the nature of the transaction and the parties' intentions.

الفصل (۳۰) صيغة العقد وما إليها
فهذه نصوص العلماء على اختلافها لم يقل أحد فيها بالمعاطاة في النكاح كما قالوه في البيع . وقد أسهب القرافي في فروقه»، وكذا صاحب تهذيب الفروق المطبوع على هامشه في تعليل هذا الفرق (ر : الفرق / ١٥٧ من كتاب «الفروق ومن تهذيبه ١٤٣/٣ - ١٤٥ ، و/ ۱۸۰ - ۱۸۲).
أقول : إِنَّ جوهر الحكمة الشرعية في عدم جريان التعاطي في عقد النكاح يظهر من الرجوع إلى قاعدة يقررها الفقهاء هي: أن الإباحة والبذل إنما يجريان في الأموال لا في المتعة الجنسية. ر كتاب الدعوى في رد المحتار ٣٣٤/٢ والبدائع ٢٢٧/٦). وذلك لأن الشريطة الشرعية في استحلال مال الغير هي رضى صاحبه ببذله إما مجاناً أو بعوض. فلو أباح الإنسان ماله لأحد حل هذا المال للمباح له. بخلاف ما لو أباحت المرأة نفسها لرجل، فإن المتعة لا تحل بينهما بهذه الإباحة، بل هي السفاح الممنوع. وعلى هذا الأساس كان التعاطي كافياً في عقد المعاوضة المالية؛ لأن التراضي على البذل فيها هو المميز بين حالتي أخذ المال بالحق أو بالباطل، وإن التعاطي يفيد هذا التراضي بصورة لا تقل دلالة عن لفظ الإيجاب والقبول فيحصل به المقصود الشرعي. أما التعاشر الاستمتاعي بين الجنسين الرجل والمرأة فإنّ له حالتين إحداهما مشروعة وهي النكاح والأخرى جريمة ممنوعة وهي المخادنة والسفاح. وجميع نتائج النكاح الخطيرة في الأموال والأنساب وسائر حقوق الأسرة تثبت كلها الحالة المشروعة وتنتفي في الحالة الممنوعة. ومن الواضح أن الحالتين أي الزواج والمخادنة تشبه إحداهما الأخرى في مظهرها ؛ وليس التراضي هو المميز بين الحالتين، لأنه موجود في كل منهما، وهو غير كاف في هذه المعاشرة بين الجنسين، فإن التراضي إنما كان كافياً في المعاوضة المالية لأن الأموال يجري فيها البذل والإباحة كما تقدم.
فلو جاز التعاطي في عقد الزواج - بأن يدفع رجل إلى امرأة مبلغاً من المال كمهر بقصد الزواج، فتتابعه دون أن يتلفظا بعقد وتعيش معه ويتعاشرا كأزواج - لما كان في هذا التعاطي دلالة ظاهرة على أكثر من التراضي على المعاشرة، أما قصد الزواج فلا يظهر للناظر بهذا التعاطي وإن مجرد التراضي على المعاشرة لا يكفي في تمييز النكاح عن السفاح كما سَلَف بيانه، بل لا بد من ظهور التراضي على غَرَض الزواج - ويظهر هذا الغرض بصورة لا اشتباه فيها إلا بالتعبير اللفظي . فلذلك أجمعت الاجتهادات الإسلامية على عدم انعقاد النكاح بالتعاطي دون الإيجاب والقبول اللفظيين كما كان عقد النكاح هو العقد الوحيد الذي أوجب فيه الشرع الإسلامي شيئاً من المراسيم الشكلية لصحته بأن اشترط فيه الإشهاد عليه، واستحسن شهره وإذاعته كي يشيع خبره وتنتفي الريبة من اجتماع الرجل والمرأة على حياة مشتركة لا يعرف أساسها ومبناها كما تقدم.
وأما الانعقاد بلسان الحال فكما لو ترك إنسان متاعه بين يدي شخص آخر وذهب، وذلك الآخر ساكت لم ينهه ولم يتنصل من حفظه، فإنه ينعقد عقد الإيداع بينهما بدلالة الحال فيصير الشخص الموضوع أمامه مودَعاً مكلفاً بالحفظ ومسؤولاً عنه إذا قصر فيه . ولو وضع ماله أمام جماعة كذلك أصبحوا جميعاً مستودعين. فلو قاموا واحداً بعد آخر تعيَّن حفظ الوديعة على الأخير. فإذا قام وترك المال حتى ضاع فإنه يضمنه الصاحبه . وكذا لو دخل إنسان خاناً مثلاً فسأل صاحبه أين أربط دابتي، فأراه محلاً فربطها فيه وذهب انعقد الإيداع دلالة، وأصبح صاحب الخان مكلفاً بحفظ الدابة، ومسؤولاً عن التقصير فيه (ر المجلة، ومرآتها، م/ (۱)(۷۷۳).
والخلاصة أنَّ العقد في الفقه الإسلامي ارتباط إرادي، أداته الطبيعية وطريقه الأصلية اللفظ المبين.
وقد سمينا نحن هذا النوع من الدلالة السان الحال أخذاً من فحوى كلامهم وقسمنا عندئذ الانعقاد بالدلالة إلى قسمين: الانعقاد بالتعاطي، وبلسان الحال، كما رأيت لأن التعاطي الذي يذكره الفقهاء هو أيضاً نوع من الدلالة بطريق المبادلة الفعلية (ر: المجلة / ١٧٥).
Chapter 30: The Form of the Contract and What Pertains to It. These are the texts of the scholars, despite their differences, none of them said that mu'atah (mutual exchange without words) is valid in marriage as they said it is in sale. Al-Qarafi elaborated in his 'Furuq', as did the author of 'Tahdhib al-Furuq' printed in its margin, in explaining this difference. I say: The essence of the legal wisdom in not allowing mutual exchange in the marriage contract appears by referring to a maxim established by jurists: that permissibility (ibahah) and granting (badhl) apply only to property, not to sexual enjoyment. Because the legal condition for making permissible the property of another is the owner's consent to give it, either freely or for compensation. So if a person permits his property to someone, that property becomes permissible for the one permitted. Contrary to if a woman permits herself to a man, sexual enjoyment does not become permissible between them by this permission; rather it is forbidden fornication. On this basis, mutual exchange is sufficient in a financial exchange contract, because mutual consent to give is what distinguishes between taking property rightfully or wrongfully, and mutual exchange indicates this consent in a manner no less indicative than the words of offer and acceptance, so the legal objective is achieved. As for sexual association between the sexes, man and woman, it has two states: one lawful, which is marriage, and the other a prohibited crime, which is illicit intimacy and fornication. All the serious consequences of marriage in property, lineage, and other family rights are established only in the lawful state and are negated in the prohibited state. It is clear that the two states, marriage and illicit intimacy, resemble each other in appearance; and mutual consent is not what distinguishes them, because it exists in both, and it is not sufficient in this intersex association. For mutual consent was sufficient in financial exchange only because property admits of granting and permissibility as mentioned. So if mutual exchange were allowed in the marriage contract – such as a man giving a woman some money as dower with the intention of marriage, and she follows him without uttering a contract, and lives with him as spouses – then this mutual exchange would indicate no more than mutual consent to association, but the intention of marriage would not be apparent to an observer. Merely agreeing to associate is not sufficient to distinguish marriage from fornication, as previously explained. Rather, the consent for the purpose of marriage must appear, and this purpose does not appear unmistakably except through verbal expression. Therefore, Islamic ijtihads are unanimous that marriage is not concluded by mutual exchange without verbal offer and acceptance, just as the marriage contract is the only contract in which Islamic law required some formal procedures for its validity, by stipulating witnesses for it, and recommended its publicizing and announcement so that its news spreads and doubt is removed about a man and woman living together without knowing its basis and foundation. As for conclusion by the language of the situation (lisan al-hal), it is like when a person leaves his goods in front of another person and goes away, and that other is silent, not forbidding him nor disclaiming custody, then the contract of deposit is concluded between them by indication of the situation, so the person in front of whom the goods are placed becomes a depositary, obligated to safeguard and responsible if he is negligent. If he places his goods in front of a group similarly, they all become depositaries. If they leave one after another, the obligation of custody is determined to be on the last one. If he leaves and abandons the goods until they are lost, he is liable for them. Similarly, if a person enters a caravanserai for example and asks its owner 'Where do I tie my animal?' and he shows him a place, so he ties it and goes, the deposit contract is concluded by indication, and the owner of the caravanserai becomes obligated to safeguard the animal and responsible for negligence in it. The summary is that the contract in Islamic jurisprudence is a volitional connection, whose natural instrument and original method is clear verbal expression. We have called this type of indication 'lisan al-hal' taken from the gist of their statements, and we have thus divided conclusion by indication into two types: conclusion by mutual exchange (ta'ati) and by the language of the situation (lisan al-hal), as you have seen, because the mutual exchange that jurists mention is also a type of indication through the method of actual exchange.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage explains the different methods of contract formation in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, discusses three ways: verbal expression (the default), mutual exchange (ta'ati, meaning actual transfer without words), and the language of the situation (lisan al-hal). He emphasizes that financial exchange contracts can be valid by simple mutual exchange because the underlying principle is that property can be made permissible by the owner's consent, which is sufficiently indicated by action. However, the marriage contract is an exception: it requires explicit verbal offer and acceptance. The reason is that the distinction between lawful marriage and unlawful fornication is not merely consent – both involve consent – but the specific intention of marriage must be clearly manifested. Hence, Islamic consensus ('ijma') holds that marriage cannot be concluded by mere mutual exchange. Additionally, the passage gives examples of contracts concluded by 'lisan al-hal', such as deposit (wadi'ah) when goods are left before someone who silently accepts the responsibility. In such cases, the situation itself creates a binding contract and imposes duties of safekeeping. The author concludes that the primary and natural method of contract is verbal expression, but other methods are recognized as valid within their proper spheres.

Key Terms
التعاطي (at-ta'āṭī): Mutual exchange of goods or money without verbal offer and acceptance, relying on actions to indicate consent.
لسان الحال (lisān al-ḥāl): The language of the situation; circumstances that indicate consent to a contract without words.
الإباحة (al-ibāḥah): The legal permissibility that makes something lawful to use or possess.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is mutual exchange (ta'ati) sufficient for a sale but not for a marriage contract?
A) Because sale involves property which can be made permissible by the owner's consent, while marriage involves sexual enjoyment which requires explicit verbal expression to distinguish it from fornication.
B) Because sale is a lesser contract and marriage is more important; oral expression is always required for important contracts.
C) Because mutual exchange is only allowed in gifts, not in sales.
D) Because marriage requires witnesses, and witnesses cannot be present if only actions are involved.

Key Takeaway
The core legal principle: permissibility and granting apply to property, not to sexual enjoyment, so marriage requires explicit verbal expression whereas financial contracts can be formed by mutual exchange.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Islamic Contract Law Rejects Verbal Formalism
Refutes the misconception of magical word influence and explains flexible contract formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Islamic contract law is not formalistic and identify the various means of forming a contract (speech, writing, gesture, ta'ati, lisan al-hal) according to Hanafi jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Contracts are valid when decisive will is clearly manifested—by words, actions, or context—not by specific verbal formulas.

Some scholars, viewing Islamic law from a distance, have wrongly accused it of believing in the 'magical influence of words.' In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa dismantles that misconception by showcasing the flexibility of Islamic contract theory: mutual conduct (ta'ati), contextual clues (lisan al-hal), and any clear expression suffice. Your task is to parse his argument and map the spectrum of valid contract formation methods in the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation of verbal formalism
Find where al-Zarqa describes the 'magical influence of words' belief and note his rejection of it as a foreign-imposed idea.
Look at the opening lines where he criticizes Dr. Shafiq Shahata.
2
Examine the evidence for mutual conduct (ta'ati)
Al-Zarqa cites that Islamic jurisprudence accepts contracts by mutual conduct except for marriage. Note the reason for the marriage exception and the Shafi'i school's initial opposition.
Marriage requires speech to distinguish lawful union from illicit relations.
3
Analyze the role of speech as original but not exclusive
Speech is the natural way to express decisive will, but substitutes (writing, gesture, ta'ati) are valid out of necessity or choice.
Speech is the asl (original), but substitutes are khalaf (substitute).
4
Recognize the concept of lisan al-hal (indications of the state)
Contracts can even be formed by contextual evidence, which is less than mutual conduct. This shows the breadth of means to manifest intent.
Lisan al-hal is a supplementary method.
5
Conclude the focus on manifestation of will
The jurists do not require specific words or formulas; any expression that conveys the intended meaning suffices. The past tense is preferred but not mandatory.
The criterion is clear expression of decisive will, not lifeless words.

العقد
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
The magical influence of words in the early centuries is most like fantasies!! We, who have known in Professor Dr. Shafiq Shahata the quality of research, precision of consideration and analysis, and appreciate in this treatise of his a new effort worthy of appreciation in any case, are extremely astonished at this idea that he likely slipped into from a foreign source. He did not study Islamic law at its sources and did not know the foundations of its rulings, but rather looks at it from afar as one looks at the ghost of a distant edifice in a desert, imagining it to be a tree or a cow before recognizing it!:
So where is the verbal formalism in the Islamic theory of contract, when we have seen that Islamic jurisprudence has recognized the formation of contracts by mutual conduct except in the contract of marriage, for which verbal expression is required for a legislative purpose that only explicit speech can achieve, distinguishing the legitimate goal of the union of the two sexes from the prohibited, as previously explained in vol. 9/30? And the juristic disagreement concerning mutual conduct was not severe — as Professor Shafiq Shahata thinks — but was among the easiest of juristic disagreements. The Shafi'is initially opposed it due to their doubt about the clear manifestation of will therein, but later their late jurists ended up approving it out of necessity.
Moreover, when we judge Islamic jurisprudence, we must look to its broadest schools on the subject, not the narrowest, as long as the broader also correctly represents the theory of Sharia from one of its established perspectives. A single juristic school is only an understanding of its proponent, not a judgment on the Sharia.
Rather, our jurists have explicitly stated the formation of a contract by even less than mutual conduct, through indications from the context of the situation, as previously explained in vol. 11/30.
So where is this generous contractual theory from the belief in the magic of words in the early ages?!
:
Appendix to Chapter (30): Is There Literalism in Jurisprudence?
As for the fact that Islamic jurisprudence considers the spoken word in contracts as the original, and considers anything else such as writing, gesture, or mutual conduct as a substitute for speech, it is because speech is the natural way of manifesting decisive will, and it remains the natural way in that. This does not prevent accepting other methods as substitutes for speech, either out of necessity, such as the gesture of a mute, or by choice, such as mutual conduct.
Indeed, the jurists have explicitly stated regarding sale that it is formed by one of two methods: either by speech, which is offer and acceptance, or by action without any speech, which is mutual conduct. (See al-Durr al-Mukhtar, beginning of Sales).
And we have seen that Islamic jurisprudence did not require for any contract a specific word or formula, but rather made every expression, whether literal or figurative, valid in every contract whenever it expresses its intended specific meaning. The jurists only preferred the use of the past tense in forming the contract as a preference, not as a requirement, because it is more indicative of decisiveness and realization. All this most clearly indicates that the focus in the contractual process is the manifestation of decisive will and clarity of the intended type of contract without ambiguity, so that its effects and obligations are established between the contracting parties. The word is not the focus in itself.
And it is not hidden that mere internal intention does not produce an action; it remains in the realm of hidden intentions even if it is a decisive resolve. So if someone intends divorce or sale or being a seller, [it is not enough]; rather, a tangible means is necessary to manifest the will in a form that the judiciary can grasp and build upon. This is among the self-evident matters of jurisprudence and law (see Radd al-Muhtar, 4/13).
Thus, speech in all legislation is that original means of bringing will into the realm of tangible existence. However, some legal systems add to it formal ceremonial rites of specific movements, gestures, or traditional actions, without which the contract is not considered concluded, as was the case with the Romans.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy argument defending the flexibility of Islamic contract theory against the charge of verbal formalism. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by criticizing a scholar (Dr. Shafiq Shahata) who, he claims, viewed early Islamic jurisprudence as believing in the 'magical influence of words'—a notion the author dismisses as fantastical. Al-Zarqa argues that Islamic contract law is not formalistic: it recognizes the formation of contracts by mutual conduct (ta'ati) except for marriage, where speech is required for a specific legislative purpose (to distinguish lawful union from illicit relations). He notes that the Shafi'i school initially opposed conduct-based contracts due to doubts about clear manifestation of intent, but later approved it out of necessity. The author emphasizes that when judging Islamic law, one must look to its broadest schools, not the narrowest. He further states that contracts can be formed even without verbal exchange, through 'indications of the state' (lisan al-hal). In the appendix, he explains that while speech is the original and natural method of expressing decisive will, other methods—writing, gesture, and conduct—are valid substitutes, either out of necessity (e.g., a mute's gesture) or choice (e.g., mutual conduct). He cites that in sale, the contract is formed either by speech (offer and acceptance) or by action without words (ta'ati), referring to al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Critically, Islamic jurisprudence does not require any specific word or formula; any expression, literal or figurative, suffices if it conveys the intended meaning. The use of past tense is preferred but not obligatory. The true focus is the manifestation of decisive will and clarity of the contract type. Internal intention alone, however resolute, does not create a contract; a tangible means is essential for the judiciary to enforce it. The author contrasts this with Roman law, which added formalistic rituals without which a contract was invalid.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): Mutual conduct; formation of a contract by the exchange of actions (e.g., handing over money and goods) without spoken offer and acceptance.
الإرادة الجازمة (al-irada al-jazima): Decisive will; the firm intention that must be manifested externally to form a valid contract.
لسان الحال (lisan al-hal): Indications of the state; context or circumstances that reveal a party's intent even without explicit words or actions.
الشكلية اللفظية (al-shakliyya al-lafziyya): Verbal formalism; the view that specific words or formulas are necessary for a valid contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which contract requires a spoken word for a legislative purpose?
A) Sale
B) Marriage
C) Gift
D) Agency

Key Takeaway
Islamic contract law focuses on the manifestation of decisive will, not on lifeless verbal formulas, accepting speech, actions, writing, and contextual indications as valid means of contract formation.

The Originality of Verbal Expression in Contracts and Its Implications for Fatwa
Rational argument for the primacy of verbal expression in contract formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that verbal expression is the original method of contract formation and apply it to evaluate contract validity in fatwa, distinguishing between formation and proof.

Core Idea
Contracts are formed by manifesting decisive will; speech is the natural original, but any clear expression suffices.

Understanding why Islamic law prioritizes verbal expression in contracts is essential for a mufti. This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work provides a rational foundation: speech is the natural and original means of manifesting intent, but it is not the only means. Writing, gesture, and mutual action (ta'ati) are valid substitutes. As a mufti, you must apply this principle to avoid invalidating contracts based on empty formalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Position
State clearly that the Hanafi school considers verbal expression (lafz) as the original (asl) method for contract formation. Other means are substitutes (khalaf). This is the starting point for any fatwa on contract formation.
The passage uses the term 'أصلاً' (original) to emphasize the foundational status of speech.
2
Understand the Rationale
The rationale is natural: speech is the natural way humans manifest decisive will (irada jazima). The passage argues that no other means is as natural for everyday transactions. This is a teleological argument (talil) based on practicality.
The author contrasts with Roman law that required formal rituals, showing Islamic law's flexibility.
3
Recognize Flexibility in Expression
Islamic law does not require specific words or forms. Any expression—literal or figurative—that clearly indicates the intended contract is valid. The preference for the past tense is only for clarity, not obligation.
This is a key point to avoid the common misconception that Arabic or specific formulas are required.
4
Distinguish Formation from Proof
Writing is valid for formation, but it is exceptional. The passage highlights that writing may be required for proof in some legal systems, but that is separate from formation. A contract can be formed without writing.
This distinction is crucial when dealing with modern documentation requirements.
5
Apply to Fatwa Practice
When issuing a fatwa on contract validity, focus on whether the parties manifested their decisive will through any sensory means. Do not impose additional formalities. The default is that speech suffices; other means are acceptable substitutes depending on context.
This principle aligns with the Hanafi maxim that contracts are based on intent and customary practice.
