ملحق بالفصل (٣٠) هل من لفظية في الفقه
وأما أنَّ الفقه الإسلامي يعتبر اللفظ في عقد العقود أصلاً؛ وما سواه من كتابة أو إشارة أو تعاط يعتبره خلفاً اللفظ، فلان أنَّ اللفظ الواقع عن هو الطريق الطبيعية في إظهار الإرادة الجازمة، ولا يزال هو الطريق الطبيعية في ذلك. وهذا لا أن تقبل طرق أخرى خلفاً عن النطق إما اضطراراً يمنع كإشارة الأخرس أو اختياراً كالتعاطي.
حتى إن الفقهاء قد بينوا صراحة في البيع أنه ينعقد بإحدى طريقتين :
إما باللفظ وهو الإيجاب والقبول، وإما بالفعل دون ما لفظ، وهو التعاطي. ر الدر المختار، أول البيوع).
وقد رأينا أنَّ الفقه الإسلامي لم يشترط في عقد من العقود لفظاً ولا صيغة مُعَينين بل جعل كل عبارة من حقيقة أو مجاز، صالحة في كل عقد متى أعربت عن معناه النوعي المقصود وإنما رجح الفقهاء استعمال صيغة الماضي في بناء العقد ترجيحاً لا إلزاماً، لأنه أدل على الجزم والتحقق. وكل ذلك يفيد أوضح إفادة أنَّ المنظور إليه فقهاً في عملية العقد إنما هو إظهار الإرادة الجازمة ووضوح نوع العقد المراد بلا اشتباه ليحكم بثبوت أحكامه والتزاماته بين المتعاقدين، وليس اللفظ هو المنظور إليه لذاته .
ولا يخفى أنَّ النيَّة المجردة الباطنة لا ينشأ بها فعل، وهي في حيز البطون ولو كانت عزيمة جازمة. فمن عَزَمَ الطلاق أو البيع أو بائعاً، بل لا بد من وسيلة حسية تبرز الإرادة بصورة يستطيع القضاء أن يمسك بها ويبني عليها. وهذا من بديهيات الفقه والقانون (ر: رد المحتار .(١٣/٤
فاللفظ في كل تشريع هو تلك الوسيلة الأصلية في إخراج الإرادة إلى حيز الوجود الحسي. ولكن بعض الشرائع قد تضيف إليه مراسم شكلية صورية من حركات أو إشارات أو أفعال تقليدية مخصوصة لا يعتبر بمجرد اللفظ ،دونها كما كان عند الرومانيين.
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
عنه في
والكتابة أخت اللفظ تمتاز عليه بالثبات والضبط، وتنحط الدلالة على جزم الإرادة أحياناً، لأنَّ الإنسان قد يكتب للتجربة أو للاحتياط والتهيؤ وهو متردّد بعد في الإبرام والتنفيذ. (ر: الزيلعي (٢١٨/٦). ولكن إيجاب الكتابة في العقود بوجه عام حرج عظيم لا سيما في. الحاجات التافهة اليومية بل قد تتعذر في محيط أو في زمن تغلب فيه الأمية. فليس من شأنها أن تكون هي الطريقة العقدية الأصلية في تشريع عام دائم يجب أن يبني على الممكنات الثابتة فلذا بقيت الكتابة طريقة استثنائية في نظر الفقه الإسلامي تنوب عن النطق نيابة ويترك أمرها للأزمنة والأمكنة في شرائط اعتبارها .
ولكن قد تعتبر الكتابة في بعض الشرائع هي الأصل في إثبات بعض العقود، ولا يخفى أنَّ الإثبات غير الانعقاد.
وقد توجب النظريات الحقوقية وبعض القوانين المدنية اليوم كتابة بعض أنواع العقود في صك لتأخذ أحكامها وتوجب التزاماتها، ولكن هذا أيضاً إيجاب شكلي استثنائي على أن الصك ليس في الحقيقة هو العقد، وإنما هو إقرار خطي بوقوعه.
على أنه إذا لم يكن النطق هو الوسيلة الأصلية في عقد العقود، وما سواه من الوسائل البيانية خلف فأي وسيلة سواه من إشارة أو تعاط أو غيرهما أحسن إفصاحاً عن الإرادة الجازمة لتكون أولى بهذه الأصالة من النطق ؟
وأي تشريع قديم أو حديث إلى اليوم يرى أنَّ الكتابة أو الإشارة أو أي وسيلة أخرى غير النطق هي الطريق الطبيعية الأصيلة في عقد العقود بوجه عام، وأن النطق فيها خلف وفرع فلا يقبل من الإنسان شراء خبزه ولحمه وثوبه وسائر حاجاته اليومية من أسواقها بلسانه إلا استثناء؟!
فطبيعة الحال في الإعراب عن الإرادة الجازمة هي الداعية إلى اعتبار النطق أصلاً في العقود، وليست هي عقيدة التأثير السحري للألفاظ !!
Appendix to Section (30): On the Verbal Nature in Fiqh. As for the fact that Islamic jurisprudence considers verbal expression in contracts as the original; and what other than it from writing, gesture, or mutual action (ta'ati) it considers as a substitute for the verbal expression, because the verbal expression is the natural means for manifesting decisive will, and it remains the natural means for that. This does not mean that other means are not accepted as substitutes for speech, either out of necessity, such as the gesture of a mute, or by choice, such as ta'ati. Thus the jurists have stated explicitly in sale that it is concluded by one of two methods: either by verbal expression, which is the offer and acceptance, or by action without any verbal expression, which is ta'ati. (See al-Durr al-Mukhtar, beginning of Sales). And we have seen that Islamic jurisprudence did not require for any contract a specific verbal expression or a specific form, but rather made every expression, whether literal or figurative, valid for every contract whenever it expresses its intended essential meaning. The jurists only preferred the use of the past tense in contracting as a preference, not a requirement, because it is more indicative of decisiveness and realization. All of this indicates most clearly that the legally considered perspective in the contracting process is merely the manifestation of decisive will and clarity of the intended type of contract without confusion, so that its rulings and obligations may be established between the contracting parties, and the verbal expression is not considered for its own sake. And it is not hidden that the mere inner intention does not create an act, and it is within the realm of internals, even if it is a decisive resolve. So whoever resolves on divorce or sale or as a seller, rather, there must be a sensory means that brings forth the will in a manner that the judiciary can grasp and rely upon. And this is one of the self-evident truths of fiqh and law (see Radd al-Muhtar, 13/4). So verbal expression in every legislation is that original means of bringing the will into the realm of sensory existence. However, some legislations may add to it formal ritualistic ceremonies of specific movements, gestures, or customary actions; the contract is not considered valid by mere verbal expression without them, as was the case with the Romans. Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. And writing is the sister of verbal expression; it surpasses it in stability and precision, but its indication of decisiveness sometimes diminishes, because a person may write for trial or precaution and preparation while still hesitant about finalization and execution. (See al-Zayla'i, 6/218). But requiring writing in contracts in general is a great hardship, especially in trivial daily needs; indeed it may be impossible in an environment or time where illiteracy prevails. So it is not suitable to be the original method of contracting in a permanent general legislation that must be built on established possibilities. Therefore, writing remained an exceptional method in the view of Islamic jurisprudence, deputizing for speech, and its consideration is left to times and places regarding conditions of its validity. But writing may be considered in some legal systems as the original for proving some contracts, and it is not hidden that proof is not the same as formation. And legal theories and some civil laws today require writing of some types of contracts in a document for them to take effect and create obligations, but this is also an exceptional formal requirement, while the document is not really the contract; rather it is a written acknowledgment of its occurrence. Furthermore, if speech is not the original means in contracting, and other expressive means are substitutes, then what other means – gesture, mutual action, or others – is better at expressing decisive will so as to be more deserving of this originality than speech? And which legislation, ancient or modern to this day, considers that writing, gesture, or any other means besides speech is the natural original way in contracting in general, and that speech therein is a substitute and a secondary means, so that a person would only exceptionally be allowed to buy his bread, meat, clothing, and all his daily needs from the markets with his tongue?! Thus the nature of the situation in expressing decisive will is what calls for considering speech as the original in contracts, and it is not a belief in the magical effect of words!!
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument (talil) in legal philosophy, establishing why verbal expression (lafz) is the original means of contract formation in Islamic law. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: verbal expression is the primary method; other means (writing, gesture, mutual action) are substitutes. He justifies this by appealing to nature—speech is the natural way humans manifest decisive will. This is not a rigid requirement; Islamic law does not mandate specific words or forms; any expression that clearly indicates the intended contract suffices. The preference for the past tense is only for clarity, not obligation. The core is the manifestation of intent (irada jazima). The author then contrasts with other legal systems that impose formalities. He discusses writing: while it has advantages (stability, precision), it is not the original method due to practical hardship and the fact that it can be done without final commitment. Writing remains exceptional, deputizing for speech, and its validity depends on context. The author concludes rhetorically, arguing that if speech were not the original, then which alternative better expresses decisive will? No legislation, past or present, makes writing or gesture the primary method for everyday transactions. Thus, the principle is grounded in practical necessity, not superstitious reverence for words. For a mufti, this reinforces that fatwas on contract formation should focus on the reality of intent and expression, not empty formalism.

Key Terms
لفظ (lafz): Verbal expression used in contracts; considered the original method for manifesting intent.
تعاط (ta'ati): Mutual action without speech, such as handing over goods and paying, constituting a valid contract formation.
إرادة جازمة (irada jazima): Decisive will; the firm intention that must be externally manifested for a contract to be valid.
كتابة (kitaba): Writing; considered a substitute for verbal expression in contract formation, but its indication of decisiveness may be weaker.
إشارة (ishara): Gesture; a substitute for speech, especially for those unable to speak (e.g., a mute).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which statement best captures the Hanafi view on contract formation?
A) Only spoken words in Arabic and past tense form valid contracts.
B) Any manifestation of decisive will, including speech, writing, gesture, or mutual action, can form a valid contract.
C) Contracts must be formed by spoken words only; writing is only for proof.
D) Writing is the original method because it provides stability and precision.

Key Takeaway
Verbal expression is the original method of contract formation because it is the natural means of manifesting decisive will, but Islamic law accepts any sensory expression that clearly conveys intent.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Primacy of Speech in Contract Formation
Argues that speech is the original method of contract formation, writing is exceptional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the original method of contract formation in Islamic law and distinguish formation (in'aqad) from proof (ithbat).

Core Idea
Speech is the natural, original method; writing is exceptional and only for proof.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Islamic contract law: speech (nutq) is the primary means of forming a contract. While writing offers stability, it is not the original method because it can be ambiguous regarding firm intent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Islamic legal reasoning to modern contractual practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis
The author argues that speech is the original method of contract formation, while writing is exceptional and deputizes for speech. Start by locating the central claim in the first few lines.
The thesis is stated clearly: 'writing remained an exceptional method in the view of Islamic jurisprudence, deputizing for speech.'
2
Analyze the evidence for speech's primacy
The author gives several rational arguments: writing can be done without firm intent, requiring writing for all contracts is a hardship, and in many times/places illiteracy makes writing impossible.
These arguments are based on practicality and human nature, not on religious texts.
3
Distinguish formation from proof
The author emphasizes that writing may be required for proof (ithbat) but not for formation (in'aqad). Contract formation occurs through speech; proof is a separate matter.
This distinction is critical: modern laws requiring written documents are about proof, not formation.
4
Refute the alternative view
The author challenges the idea that writing or any other means could be the original method. He asks: what ancient or modern legislation treats writing or gestures as the natural way to buy daily necessities?
The rhetorical question shows that no legal system adopts writing as the original method for everyday transactions.
5
Conclude with the basis for speech's primacy
The conclusion is that the natural state of expressing firm intent (irada jazima) drives the choice of speech as the original method, not any mystical belief in the power of words.
The author rejects the idea that this is superstition; it is based on practical reality.

٤٢٤
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and what pertains to it in Fiqh. From it: Writing is the sister of speech, distinguished by stability and precision, but its indication of firm intent is sometimes inferior, because a person may write for experimentation or precaution or preparation while still hesitant about finalization and execution. (See: al-Zayla'i 6/218). But making writing obligatory in contracts generally is a great hardship, especially in trivial daily needs, and may be impossible in an environment or time where illiteracy prevails. So it is not proper for it to be the original contractual method in a permanent general legislation that must be built on stable possibilities. Hence writing remained an exceptional method in the view of Islamic jurisprudence, deputizing for speech, and its consideration is left to times and places. But writing may be considered the original method for proving some contracts in some legal systems, and it is well-known that proof is different from formation. Modern legal theories and some civil codes today require writing for some types of contracts in a document to give them legal effect and impose obligations, but this too is an exceptional formal requirement, for the document is not in reality the contract, but rather a written acknowledgment of its occurrence. Nevertheless, if speech is not the original means of forming contracts, and all other expressive means are substitutes, then what means other than speech—gesture, exchange-of-commodities (ta'ati), or others—is better at expressing firm intent to deserve this originality? And what ancient or modern legislation to date considers writing, gesture, or any other means besides speech as the natural, original way of forming contracts in general, and considers speech therein a substitute and secondary, so that a person cannot buy his bread, meat, garment, and all his daily needs from their markets by his tongue except exceptionally?! Rather, the natural state of expressing firm intent is what prompts considering speech as the origin in contracts, not the belief in the magical effect of words!!
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument establishing the primacy of speech (nutq) over writing (kitabah) in contract formation. The author begins by acknowledging that writing has advantages (stability, precision) but notes it is inferior in reliably indicating firm intent because one may write provisionally. He emphasizes that requiring writing for all contracts would be excessively burdensome, especially in illiterate societies. He then distinguishes between proving a contract (ithbat) and its formation (in'aqad)—writing may be required for proof, but formation occurs through speech. He refutes modern legal trends that mandate writing for validity, calling them exceptional formal requirements. The core argument is practical and based on custom: no legal system treats writing as the natural method for everyday transactions. The passage concludes that speech is the original means because it best expresses firm intent, not because of any mystical power of words. This is a foundational point in Islamic contract law.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitabah): Writing as a means of expressing contractual intent, considered exceptional and deputizing for speech.
نطق (nutq): Spoken expression, the original and primary method of contract formation in Islamic law.
إرادة جازمة (irada jazima): Firm and decisive intent to enter into a contract, essential for its validity.
انعقاد (in'aqad): The formation of a contract through the conjunction of offer and acceptance.
إثبات (ithbat): Proof or evidence of a contract, distinct from its formation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Speech is the original method of contract formation in Islamic law; writing is exceptional and serves primarily for proof, not formation.

The Seven General Conditions for Contract Formation
Defining and enumerating the general conditions for contract validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and special conditions and list the seven general conditions that must be present in every contract for its formation.

Core Idea
Every valid contract requires seven foundational conditions; missing any one prevents formation.

In this passage, we move from the constituents of a contract to the conditions required for its formation. The author distinguishes between general conditions that apply universally and special conditions that are contract-specific. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any mufti analyzing a contractual issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of conditions
The passage divides conditions into general (sharā’iṭ ‘āmmah) and special (sharā’iṭ khāṣṣah). General conditions must be present in every contract; special conditions are only required for certain contract types.
2
List the seven general conditions
The author enumerates: 1) capacity of the parties, 2) suitability of the subject matter, 3) no prohibition under penalty of nullity, 4) fulfillment of its own special conditions, 5) that the contract be beneficial, 6) offer remains valid until acceptance, 7) unity of the contract session.
Memorize this list; it forms the foundation of Hanafi contract theory.
3
Understand the consequence of missing a general condition
If any general condition is absent, the contract does not form (lā yan‘aqid). This is a clear, binary result – either the contract exists or not.
4
Note that special conditions are covered elsewhere
Special conditions (e.g., witnesses for marriage, delivery in real contracts) are discussed in their respective chapters. This lesson focuses on the universal prerequisites.
This parsing exercise sharpens your ability to sort conditions by scope.

الفصل الحادي والثلاثون
الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود
التميز بين الشرائط العامة والخاصة - الشرائط العامة السبع وإيضاحها - نتيجة فقدان شيء من شرائط الانعقاد.

١/٣١ - رأينا في الفصلين السابقين مقومات العقد، وهي والعاقدان، والمحل، والموضوع والأركان؛ وألممنا بما يتصل بهذه المقومات من مباحث عامة.
وكل من تلك المقومات تُشترط له شرائط شرعية يجب أن تتوافر فيه ليتم تكوين العقد وانعقاده.
وشرائط الانعقاد هذه في العقود نوعان:
- شرائط عامة، وهي التي يجب أن يتوافر وجودها في كل عقد.
- وشرائط خاصة وهي التي يشترط وجودها في بعض العقود دون سواها، فإن بعض العقود قد اختصها الشرع بشرائط إضافية علاوة على الشرائط العامة كاشتراط الشهود لانعقاد النكاح دون غيره من العقود، وكالتسليم في العقود العينية كما تقدّم وكعدم تعليق العقد على شرط في المعاوضات والتمليكات كالبيع والهبة فإنَّ التعليق يبطلها كما سنرى في بحث الشروط العقدية (ر) : ف ٢٦/٤٦ - ٢٧.
فتلك الشرائط الخاصة ببعض العقود تذكر معها في أبوابها.
أما الشرائط العامة التي هي موضع بحثنا هنا فسبع شرائط، هي:
١ - أهلية العاقدين.
٢ - قابلية محل العقد لحكمه.
٣ - أن لا يكون العقد ممنوعاً بمقتضى نص شرعي تحت طائلة البطلان.
٤ - أن يستوفي العقد شرائط انعقاده الخاصة به.
٥ - أن يكون العقد مفيداً.
٦ - بقاء الإيجاب صحيحاً إلى وقوع القبول.
٧ - اتحاد مجلس العقد.
وإليك إيضاح هذه الشرائط فيما يلي:
٢/٣١ - أولاً : أهلية العاقدين :
والمراد بها أن يكون العاقدان متمتعين بالصفات التي تسوّغ لهما شرعاً مباشرة العقد.
فمن ليس بأهل أصلاً لممارسة العقود مطلقاً، كالمجنون والصغير غير المميز، لا ينعقد عقده.
وقد تنقص أهلية الممارسة نقصاناً ولا تكون معدومة، فحينئذ تختلف آثار نقصانها في أنواع العقود.
وسنرى تفصيل ذلك في نظرية الأهليات التي سنفرد لها باباً خاصاً.
٣/٣١ - ثانياً : قابلية محل العقد لحكمه:
فلا ينعقد ما ليس بمال شرعاً كالجيفة مثلاً، ولا بيع الموقوف، لأن نتيجة البيع تمليك المال المبيع للمشتري، وإن المال الموقوف محجور عن التملك والتمليك إلا في حالات خاصة على سبيل الاستبدال فلا ينعقد بيعه في غيرها لعدم قابلية المحل، بخلاف الإجارة فإنَّ المال الموقوف يقبلها فتنعقد عليه.
Chapter Thirty-One: The General Conditions for Contract Formation. Distinction between General and Specific Conditions – The Seven General Conditions and their Explanation – The Consequence of Missing Any Condition of Formation. 1/31: We saw in the previous two chapters the constituents of the contract: the two contracting parties, the subject matter, the object, and the pillars; and we touched on general discussions related to these constituents. Each of these constituents has legal conditions that must be fulfilled for the contract to be formed and concluded. These conditions of formation in contracts are of two types: – General conditions, which must be present in every contract. – Special conditions, which are required in some contracts but not others, for some contracts are singled out by the Law with additional conditions beyond the general ones, such as requiring witnesses for the validity of marriage to the exclusion of other contracts, delivery in real contracts as previously mentioned, and non-suspension of the contract on a condition in commutative contracts and transfers like sale and gift, because suspension invalidates them as we will see in the discussion of contractual conditions (refer to section 26/46-27). So those special conditions for some contracts are mentioned in their respective chapters. As for the general conditions that are our subject here, they are seven: 1. Capacity of the contracting parties. 2. Suitability of the subject matter for its ruling. 3. That the contract not be prohibited by a textual provision under penalty of nullity. 4. That the contract fulfill its own special conditions of formation. 5. That the contract be beneficial. 6. That the offer remain valid until acceptance occurs. 7. Unity of the contract session. And here is the clarification of these conditions in the following: 2/31: First: Capacity of the contracting parties: This means that the two contracting parties possess the qualities that legally authorize them to directly undertake the contract. So one who is not eligible at all to engage in contracts absolutely, such as a madman or a non-discerning minor, his contract is not formed. The capacity to engage may be deficient but not non-existent, and then the effects of its deficiency vary across types of contracts. We will see the details of that in the theory of capacities, for which we will dedicate a separate chapter. 3/31: Second: Suitability of the subject matter for its ruling: So that which is not legally considered property, such as a carcass, does not give rise to a valid contract, nor does the sale of an endowment (waqf), because the result of a sale is to transfer ownership of the sold item to the buyer, and the endowed property is prevented from being owned or transferred except in special cases by way of substitution (istibdal), so its sale does not form in other cases due to lack of suitability of the subject matter, unlike lease (ijarah), because the endowed property admits lease, so it is validly formed.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a foundational text on contract theory in Hanafi fiqh. It distinguishes between general conditions (شروط عامة) that apply to all contracts and special conditions (شروط خاصة) that apply only to certain types. The author lists seven general conditions. The first condition is capacity (أهلية): the parties must have legal capacity to transact. Those completely lacking capacity (e.g., insane, non-discerning minor) cannot form a contract. Partial deficiency in capacity affects contracts differently, to be detailed later. The second condition is suitability of the subject matter: the object must be legally recognized as property and must be capable of being transferred. For example, a carcass (جيفة) is not property, and a waqf (endowment) generally cannot be sold because it is inalienable; but it can be leased because lease does not transfer ownership. This illustrates that the same subject matter may be suitable for one type of contract but not another.

Key Terms
شرائط عامة (sharā’iṭ ‘āmmah): Conditions that must be present in every contract for its formation
شرائط خاصة (sharā’iṭ khāṣṣah): Conditions required only for specific contract types (e.g., witnesses for marriage)
اتحاد مجلس العقد (ittiḥād majlis al-‘aqd): Unity of the contract session – offer and acceptance must occur in one continuous session

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many general conditions for contract formation does the author list?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The seven general conditions for contract formation are the universal prerequisites; missing any one prevents the contract from coming into existence.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

General Conditions for Contract Formation: The First Three
Explains the first three general conditions for contract formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three general conditions for contract formation and apply them to determine if a contract is void (bāṭil) or valid.

Core Idea
Three conditions must exist at formation: capacity, suitable subject matter, and no prohibition under penalty of nullity.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm lays the groundwork for a mufti's analysis of any contract. The first three conditions—capacity of the parties, suitability of the subject, and absence of a textual prohibition—are gatekeepers: if any is missing, the contract is void (bāṭil) from the start and cannot be ratified later. Understanding these conditions is essential for issuing correct fatwas on contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Assess Capacity of the Contracting Parties
Identify whether the parties are legally eligible to conclude contracts. Completely incompetent persons (insane, non-discerning minor) cannot form a valid contract. Partial deficiency in capacity produces varying effects.
2
Examine the Subject Matter for Suitability
The subject must be legally considered property (māl) and must be able to bear the contract's ruling. For example, a carcass is not māl, waqf property cannot be sold (but can be leased), and perishable goods cannot be pledged.
The suitability depends on the contract type; e.g., leasing waqf is valid because it doesn't transfer ownership.
3
Check for Prohibition Under Penalty of Nullity
Determine if the contract is explicitly prohibited by a textual ruling such that it is deemed illegitimate from the start. Example: a minor's donation or sale with gross fraud (ghabn fāḥish) is void because no one has legal authority (wilāyah) to conclude it.
4
Apply the Legal Maxim on Authority to Ratify
If at the time of formation there is no one with the right to ratify and execute the contract, it is void (bāṭil). Such a contract cannot be later ratified; it must be re-contracted if desired.
The maxim: 'Every contract issued without anyone having the right to ratify it at its issuance is void.'
5
Conclude Whether the Contract is Void
If any of the first three conditions is missing, the contract is void and has no legal effect. Ratification is impossible; the contract must be made anew.

وكذلك لا ينعقد رهن الأموال التي يتسارع إليها الفساد كالخضروات الطازجة، والجمد (۱) (أي الجليد، لأنَّ حكم الرهن احتباس المرهون لأجل استيفاء الدين منه عند عدم الأداء في ميعاده، وهذه الأموال لا تقبل الحفظ والاحتباس مع بقاء منافعها .

(1) الجَمْد (بفتح فسكون الماء المتجمد من البرودة.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. 1 - Capacity of the contracting parties. 2 - Suitability of the subject matter of the contract for its ruling. 3 - That the contract not be prohibited by a textual provision under penalty of nullity. 4 - That the contract fulfill its specific conditions of formation. 5 - That the contract be beneficial. 6 - That the offer remains valid until acceptance occurs. 7 - Unity of the contract session. And here is an explanation of these conditions as follows: 2/31 - First: Capacity of the contracting parties: By this is meant that the two contracting parties possess the qualities that legally permit them to directly undertake the contract. So whoever is not eligible at all for practicing contracts absolutely, like the insane and the non-discerning minor, his contract does not become valid. And the capacity for practice may be deficient without being non-existent; then the effects of its deficiency vary in the types of contracts. And we will see the details of that in the theory of capacities which we will devote a special chapter to. 3/31 - Second: Suitability of the subject matter of the contract for its ruling: So what is not legally considered wealth, like a carcass for example, does not become valid, nor does the sale of a waqf (endowment) because the result of a sale is to transfer ownership of the sold property to the buyer, and waqf property is prevented from being owned and from making others own it except in special cases by way of substitution, so its sale does not become valid in other cases due to the lack of suitability of the subject matter, unlike leasing (ijara), for waqf property accepts it, so it becomes valid on it. Chapter (31) General Conditions for the Formation of Contracts. Likewise, the pledge of assets that quickly spoil, like fresh vegetables and ice (1) (i.e., frozen water), does not become valid because the ruling of pledge is the retention of the pledged item in order to recover the debt from it when payment is not made on time, and these assets do not accept preservation and retention while preserving their benefits. 4/31 - Third: That the contract not be prohibited by a textual provision under penalty of nullity: Because a contract prohibited by law under penalty of nullity is considered by the jurists to be illegitimate from its very origin, contrary to the general legal system. So the gift (hiba) from the property of a minor under guardianship does not become valid, nor does his sale with gross fraud, whether the donor or seller is the minor himself, his guardian, his executor, or even the judge himself by virtue of his general authority in the absence of a specific guardian. And that is because donating from the property of a minor is not allowed for anyone, and selling with gross fraud is like donating. So this contract is one that no one has the authority to conclude according to its subject matter, so it is issued void; even if the minor reaches puberty and ratifies it, his ratification is not considered because a void contract cannot be ratified (as we will see in the discussion of nullity in the theory of causes of invalidity). Rather, he must renew his contract after reaching puberty if he wishes. The legal maxim in this regard is: Every contract that is issued and there is no one who has the right to ratify and execute it at the time of its issuance is void. (Al-Bada'i' 5/149-150). The jurists express the reasoning for the nullity of this type of contracts by saying that legal authority (wilaya shar'iyya) is absent from it. They mean by legal authority in this context that there is someone who legally possesses the power to conclude this contract, even if it is other than the person who actually undertook the contract. So if this authority in this sense is not available, the contract is void and not formed. (1) Al-Jamad: water that freezes due to cold.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents the first three of seven general conditions for the formation of contracts (shurut al-in'iqad). The author is systematically building a theory of contract validity. The first condition is capacity (ahliyya) of the contracting parties: only those with legal capacity can conclude contracts. The insane and minors without discretion have no capacity at all; their contracts are void. For those with partial capacity, the effects vary, and details are deferred. The second condition is that the subject matter must be suitable for the contract's ruling. A sale requires the subject to be legally considered property (mal); carcasses are not property. Waqf property cannot be sold because it is inalienable except by substitution, but it can be leased because leasing does not transfer ownership. The subject must also be durable enough for the contract; perishable goods like fresh vegetables cannot be pledged because pledge requires retention. The third condition is that the contract must not be prohibited by a textual ruling under penalty of nullity. Contracts that are fundamentally prohibited, like a minor's donation or sale with gross fraud (ghabn fahish), are void ab initio. Even if the minor later ratifies it after puberty, the ratification is invalid because a void contract (batil) cannot be ratified; it must be re-contracted. The underlying maxim is that a contract issued without anyone having the authority to ratify it at its time is void. This is because legal authority (wilaya shar'iyya) is absent. The author emphasises that the relevant authority does not have to be the actual contracting party; rather, there must exist someone who has the legal power to conclude such a contract. If no such authority exists, the contract is void.

Key Terms
الولاية الشرعية (al-wilāyah al-sharʿiyyah): Legal authority that must exist for a contract to be valid; the power to conclude a contract on behalf of another, even if not the actual contracting party.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāḥish): Gross fraud or exploitation where the price is significantly below market value; treated like a donation (tabarru') from a minor's property.
الباطل (al-bāṭil): Void contract; legally nonexistent from inception, cannot be ratified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of a minor's property with gross fraud (ghabn fāḥish) void?
A) Because the minor lacks capacity and the sale is treated as a donation, which no one has authority to make.
B) Because the subject matter is not suitable for sale.
C) Because the contract is prohibited by a textual provision under penalty of nullity only if the guardian sells.
D) Because the minor did not give valid consent.

Key Takeaway
A contract issued without anyone having the legal authority (wilāyah) to conclude or ratify it at the time of formation is void (bāṭil) and cannot be ratified later.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Causes of Invalidity in Contracts
Classification of reasons for contract invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify causes of invalidity in Hanafi contracts and apply the condition that prohibition must be 'under penalty of invalidity'.

Core Idea
A contract is void only if prohibition explicitly or implicitly threatens invalidity.

Not every forbidden contract is automatically invalid. Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between prohibitions that render a contract void (bāṭil) and those that merely make it disliked or unlawful. This lesson equips you with the classification framework from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal so that you can identify the precise cause of invalidity and apply the critical condition: the prohibition must be 'under penalty of invalidity' (taḥta ṭā’ilat al-buṭlān).

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Begin by determining whether a given contract is prohibited by Sharī‘ah or by a valid state law. The prohibition may be explicit (e.g., selling intoxicants) or derived by analogy (e.g., selling narcotics).
Prohibition alone is not enough; it must be linked to invalidity.
2
Check the condition: 'under penalty of invalidity'
Examine whether the prohibition is stated or understood to render the contract void (bāṭil). Hanafi usul requires that the prohibition be 'taḥta ṭā’ilat al-buṭlān' – that is, the legal consequence is nullity, not mere sinfulness.
If the prohibition is merely tanzīhī (disliked), the contract remains valid.
3
Classify the cause of invalidity
Once invalidity is established, classify it into one of five categories: method (e.g., mulāmasa), subject matter (e.g., intoxicants), object (e.g., minor's donation), external factor (e.g., Friday prayer time sale), or relation to party (e.g., sale of game by muḥrim).
The same contract may fall under multiple causes.
4
Distinguish personal authority from formation
Note that lack of personal capacity (wilāya shakhṣiyya) does not prevent formation; a fudūlī's contract is formed but suspended. Invalidity is distinct from suspension.
This is a key Hanafi nuance.
5
Apply the ruling and consequences
If the contract is bāṭil, it produces no legal effects from the start. The subject matter should be returned, and any consideration is forfeit unless returned in kind.
For deposit contracts (wadī‘a), the invalidity of a gold transaction does not affect the deposit itself.

Source Text

الفصل (۳۱) الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
This is in contrast to personal guardianship, which is that the contracting party has legal authority, such as being an owner or a deputy of the owner like an agent or executor. This personal guardianship is not a condition for the contract's formation, for the contract of an unauthorized agent (al-fuduli) on behalf of another is formed, but its effectiveness depends on the ratification of the one who has the right of lawful disposition.
Or
It is observed here that the contract prohibited by law under penalty of invalidity may be due to the method of the contract, such as the sale of touch (bay' al-mulamasah), the sale of throwing (al-munabadhah), and throwing a stone (ilqa' al-hajar), which the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade in the past; or due to the subject matter of the contract, such as selling narcotics for prohibited use, or hiring for sinful acts or committing crimes; or due to the object of the contract, such as donation from a minor's property. All these contracts are invalid regardless of the legal reason for prohibition, provided the prohibition is under penalty of invalidity explicitly in the text, by inference, or by analogy (1). Invalidity may also be due to a reason related to the subject matter or the contracting party, such as selling game from the Haram, and selling game caught by a person in ihram for Hajj or Umrah, even if hunted outside the Haram, for its sale is invalid just as eating it is prohibited because it is considered carrion by virtue of the legal text that prohibits that.
Similarly, in Syria today, any contract made in gold currency is invalid after it was prohibited by a special law.
(1) Such was the case in our legal rulings since the Ottoman era: contracts creating real rights over immovable property, such as sale, mortgage, etc. The Ottoman law required all these real estate contracts to be made at the Land Registry office and recorded in its official register under penalty of invalidity; thus real estate contracts made outside the land registry are not considered valid even if a deed is written, and the party who refuses to execute and register them is not obliged because of their invalidity... And on this the judicial doctrine in our country proceeded in understanding the texts of the Real Property Law No. 3339 issued during the French Mandate in 1930. However, the new body of the Syrian Court of Cassation (Cour de Cassation) has recently departed from the previous doctrine in understanding the texts of this latter law and decided that all these real estate contracts are considered valid in themselves even if made secretly, and may be claimed upon, and the contracting party is compelled by the court to register, but ownership does not transfer by this contract between the parties, nor does the effect of the contract extend to others except from the date of registration. See the appellate decision issued on 18 Rabi' al-Awwal 1368 AH = 17 January 1949, under number // Fundamental Rights / 46. And this was adopted and also decided by the Syrian Civil Code issued in our country in 1949 during the third edition of this jurisprudential introduction.

Similarly, in Syria today, any contract made in gold currency is invalid after it was prohibited by a special law.
(1) This law was issued by the French Delegate on 26 January 1940 under No. 18 L/R during the French Mandate over Syria and Lebanon, and is still in effect today. It considered dealing in gold null and void, and required in previous contracts that the contracting party pay against the gold undertaken Syrian paper money at the official rate announced by the French Delegation.
The expression and translation used the word 'engagements' contracted in gold. Our now-defunct foreign judiciary and the present national judiciary have been greatly confused in applying this law, such that some judges ruled that gold deposits do not require the depositary to return the same gold, but only to return its equivalent in paper money according to the official rate, not the market rate. Thus the depositor loses more than half the value of the deposit because the official rate of gold value in Syrian paper money is less than half the market price. This is wrong; the correct interpretation is that the word 'engagements' in the said law refers only to engagements whose execution transfers ownership, such as sale, lease, settlement, even marriage if contracted with a dowry in gold currency, and all transactions that involve an undertaking to pay wealth that exits one's ownership and enters another's ownership.
Thus it does not include a contract of deposit; rather, the gold deposit must be returned in kind, because the undertaking to return it does not involve transfer of ownership; it is required to be preserved on the ownership of its owner the depositor, as evidence that the said law did not consider gold a prohibited substance subject to confiscation, but rather prohibited dealing in it. So whoever has the right to own and keep gold himself has the right to keep it by deposit through another, and the depositary is obliged to return the same item. By this, some of the injustices of this political law and its evils are removed.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm is a classification of the causes of invalidity (bāṭil) in contracts. The author first distinguishes personal capacity (wilāya shakhṣiyya) from authority over the subject matter; personal capacity is not required for formation (in‘iqād), as the contract of an unauthorized agent (al-fuḍūlī) is formed but suspended on ratification. He then identifies four categories of prohibition that render a contract bāṭil: (1) the method (ṭarīqa), such as the pre-Islamic sales of mulāmasa (touch) and munābādha (throw), prohibited by hadith; (2) the subject matter (maḥall), like selling intoxicants or hiring for sin; (3) the object (mawḍū‘), like a minor's donation; and (4) an external reason, like a sale during Friday prayer time (mentioned later). The author also notes that invalidity may stem from a relation to the subject matter or the contracting party, as with game from the Haram or caught by a muḥrim. He provides a contemporary example: Syrian law prohibiting contracts in gold currency, which he argues should not apply to deposit contracts (wadī‘a) because the return of the deposited gold is not a transfer of ownership. The passage is a practical classification for understanding when a contract is void ab initio.

Key Terms
ولاية شخصية (wilāya shakhṣiyya): السلطة الشرعية لشخص العاقد بأن يكون مالكاً أو نائباً عن المالك
فضولي (fuḍūlī): من يتصرف في مال غيره أو في عقد دون إذن
إجازة (ijāza): إمضاء العقد من صاحب الحق الشرعي بعد وقوعه
تحت طائلة البطلان (taḥta ṭā’ilat al-buṭlān): المنع الذي يُفقد العقد صحته، صراحةً أو استنباطاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The method of contract, such as mulāmasa
B) The subject matter, such as selling narcotics
C) The lack of personal capacity of the contracting party (wilāya shakhṣiyya)
D) An external factor, such as selling game from the Haram

Key Takeaway
Invalidity requires the prohibition to be 'under penalty of invalidity'; causes are classified by method, subject matter, object, or relation to party, and this framework helps a mufti correctly distinguish void contracts from those that are merely prohibited.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Conditions of Formation vs. Validity in Hanafi Contracts
Summarizing conditions of formation and validity, distinguishing void from voidable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditions of formation (shurūṭ al-inʿiqād) and conditions of validity (shurūṭ al-ṣiḥḥa) and apply the Hanafi classification of contracts as either void (bāṭil) or voidable (fāsid).

Core Idea
A missing condition of formation voids a contract; a missing condition of validity makes it voidable but rectifiable.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational Hanafi taxonomy of contracts. The passage distinguishes two sets of conditions: those necessary for a contract to exist at all (shurūṭ al-inʿiqād) and those required for its legal perfection (shurūṭ al-ṣiḥḥa). Understanding this distinction is critical for any mufti when analyzing the validity of a transaction. We will also see how the Hanafi school uniquely recognizes an intermediate 'voidable' (fāsid) category, unlike other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of conditions
Distinguish between conditions of formation (necessary for existence) and conditions of validity (necessary for legal soundness).
Conditions of formation are general; conditions of validity are often contract-specific.
2
Apply the classification to a scenario
Given a contract missing a condition of formation (e.g., offer and acceptance not properly exchanged), label it as bāṭil (void). Given a contract with a condition of validity missing (e.g., unknown price), label it as fāsid (voidable).
The consequence of fāsid is liability to rescission.
3
Contrast with the majority view
Note that non-Hanafi schools do not recognize fāsid; they treat such contracts as either valid or void. Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
Under Hanafi law, a fāsid contract can be rectified if the defect is removed before rescission.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٩/٣١ - خلاصة عن شرائط الانعقاد وشرائط الصحة:
بينا فيما سلف أنَّ هذه الشرائط السبع التي أوضحناها يتوقف على تحققها جميعاً تكوين كل عقد فهي شرائط انعقاد عامة.
فإذا فقدت واحدة من هذه الشرائط في عقد من العقود كانت النتيجة عدم انعقاد العقد شرعاً وإن وجدت صورته حساً؛ فيكون وجود العقد كعدمه في النظر الشرعي فلا تترتب عليه النتائج والالتزامات التي رتبها الشرع على انعقاده.
وعندئذ يسمى العقد في اصطلاح الفقهاء : عقداً باطلا.
وهناك شرائط شرعية سوى هذه تسمى شرائط الصحة إذا فقد شيء منها لا يبطل العقد، بل يكون منعقداً، لكنه يعتبر مختلاً في بعض النواحي الفرعية منه غير الأساسية. وذلك كجهالة أحد البدلين في البيع أو جهالة الأجل المشروط في بعض الالتزامات في العقد كما لو تبايع اثنان دون تحديد الثمن أو تبايعا بثمن مؤجل دون تحديد الأجل، إذ يوقع تنفيذ مثل هذا العقد في نزاع مشكل بين الطرفين يتعذر على القضاء حله، لأن الجهالة حجة لكل من الطرفين في التمسك بالأكثر أو بالأقل، على حسب مصلحته.
وعندئذ يكون العقد بنحو هذا الاختلال مستوجباً للفسخ، ويسمى في نظر الاجتهاد الحنفي عقداً فاسداً.
وشرائط الصحة معظمها شرائط خاصة يشترط منها في كل عقد ما لا يشترط في غيره. وإن فهم مبانيها إجمالاً يتوقف على إيضاح فكرة الفساد ونتائجه مما سنراه في نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية فلذا لم نتعرض هنا لبحث شيء من شرائط الصحة.
على أن معظم الاجتهادات الإسلامية، سوى الاجتهاد الحنفي لم تفرق بين البطلان والفساد؛ فتعتبر العقد بين حالتين فقط : إما منعقداً صحيحاً، وإما باطلاً غير منعقد.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
9/31 - Summary of Conditions of Formation and Conditions of Validity:
We have explained previously that these seven conditions which we clarified, the formation of every contract depends on all of them being fulfilled; thus they are general conditions of formation.
If one of these conditions is missing in any contract, the result is that the contract is not formed legally even if its form exists sensorially; so the existence of the contract is like its non-existence in the view of the Law, and the results and obligations that the Law has attached to its formation are not produced.
Then the contract is called in the terminology of jurists: a void (bāṭil) contract.
And there are other legal conditions besides these called conditions of validity; if something of these is missing, the contract does not become void, but rather it is formed, yet it is considered defective in some subsidiary aspects not fundamental. For example, ignorance (jahāla) of one of the two counter-values in a sale, or ignorance of the term when a term is stipulated in some obligations in the contract, as when two persons make a sale without specifying the price, or they make a sale with a deferred price without specifying the term, because executing such a contract leads to a problematic dispute between the parties that is difficult for the judiciary to resolve, since ignorance is a plea for each party to insist on the higher or lower amount according to his interest.
Then a contract with this kind of defect is liable to recission, and is called in the view of Hanafi ijtihad: a voidable (fāsid) contract.
And conditions of validity mostly are specific conditions, some of which are required in one contract but not in another. Understanding their foundations in general depends on clarifying the idea of corruption (fasād) and its consequences, which we will see in the theory of legal sanctions, so we did not discuss here any of the conditions of validity.
However, most Islamic schools of law, apart from the Hanafi school, did not distinguish between void (buṭlān) and voidable (fasād); they consider contracts to be only two states: either validly formed and sound, or void and not formed.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the conditions of contract formation and validity, presenting the key Hanafi distinction between void (bāṭil) and voidable (fāsid) contracts. The author begins by reiterating that the seven general conditions—previously explained—are necessary for any contract to be legally formed. If any one of them is absent, the contract is not considered to have come into existence in the eyes of the Law, even if its outward form appears. Such a contract is termed bāṭil (void).

Next, the author introduces a separate category of conditions: conditions of validity (sharā’iṭ al-ṣiḥḥa). These are subsidiary; if missing, the contract does form but is defective in a non-essential aspect. Examples given include ignorance (jahāla) concerning the price or the term in a sale. Such defects make the contract liable to rescission (faskh), and in Hanafi terminology this is called a fāsid (voidable) contract.

The author notes that most conditions of validity are specific to particular contracts. The detailed discussion of fasād and its effects is postponed to the theory of legal sanctions (naẓariyyat al-mu’ayyidāt al-shar‘iyya).

Finally, the passage highlights that the majority of Islamic legal schools do not differentiate between buṭlān and fasād; they recognize only valid or void contracts. The Hanafi school is unique in maintaining the intermediate category of fāsid, which allows for a contract that is formed but flawed and can be rectified or rescinded.

For the student: Memorize the two categories and the examples. The distinction is fundamental in Hanafi mu‘āmalāt.

Key Terms
باطل (bāṭil): A contract that fails to form due to missing a condition of formation; legally non-existent.
فاسد (fāsid): A contract that is formed but defective in a subsidiary condition, liable to rescission.
شرائط الانعقاد (shurūṭ al-inʿiqād): Conditions that must be present for a contract to come into legal existence.
شرائط الصحة (shurūṭ al-ṣiḥḥa): Conditions that must be present for a contract to be legally sound; missing them makes the contract voidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the result if a condition of validity (such as specifying the price) is missing in a sale contract?
A) The contract is void (bāṭil).
B) The contract is voidable (fāsid) and subject to rescission.
C) The contract is valid as long as both parties agree.
D) The contract is suspended until the condition is fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes between void (bāṭil) and voidable (fāsid) contracts based on whether a condition of formation or a condition of validity is missing.

The Two Categories of Will: Real vs. Apparent
Defines two types of will and the role of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and apparent will in Hanafi law and explain why mere intention does not create legal effects, while still qualifying acts.

Core Idea
Inner intention alone does not form contracts; outward expression is required, but intention shapes legal outcomes.

This passage introduces the foundational distinction between the inner will (irāda ḥaqīqiyya) and the apparent will (irāda ẓāhira). Understanding this division is essential for the mufti because it clarifies when a legal act is formed and how intention influences the description of an act. The author first defines the two types, then explains the legal effect of intention.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of will
Identify the two categories: real will (inner, hidden) and apparent will (expressed through speech or conduct). Write definitions based on the text.
Real will is not known externally; apparent will is necessary for legal acts.
2
State the legal principle regarding inner will
The text asserts that inner will alone does not create legal effects—no contract, divorce, or waqf is formed by mere intention, even if both parties confirm it. Confirm with the examples given.
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi muʿāmalāt.
3
Explain the role of intention (niyya)
Intention does not form acts but qualifies them. It gives a 'color' or quality to accompanying acts. Use the examples of appropriating mubāḥāt (intention to own) and the depositary using the deposit (intention to use).
Niyya transposes the legal description of an act.
4
Summarize the mufti's takeaway
For fatwa purposes: outward expression is essential for validity of legal acts; intention determines the legal nature of the act (e.g., ownership, liability).
This distinction helps resolve cases of ambiguous expressions.

Chapter Thirty-Two
Contractual Will
First Section: Divisions of Will
Apparent Will - Real Inner Will.
132 - Will is divided into two types: real and apparent. As for the real will, it is the inner will that is not known. As for the apparent will, it is that which emerges through expression or what serves in its stead, such as mutual handing over (ta‘āṭī).
2/32
22 - The Inner Real Will:
The inner will alone does not take the place of actions and legal declarations. A contract is not formed by mere intention, even if both parties confirm the existence of their intention. Whoever intends divorce or a waqf, for example, does not become divorced or a waqf-founder merely by his intention and resolve. So every action is not completed by mere intention.
But intention has a guiding effect that is legally considered in describing what it accompanies. Thus if it accompanies an act or an omission, it colors it with a hue and gives it a quality upon which a specific civil ruling follows in the view of the Law. (1) (Refer to: al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā’ir, first section, p. 43).
(1) An example is that the intention to own in appropriating (iḥrāz) permissible things makes the appropriation an acquisition of ownership. And in rules of liability (ḍamān), they said: If the depositary (wadī‘) uses the deposited item which he was entrusted to preserve, he is considered a transgressor and becomes liable for it if it perishes, like a usurper. So if he leaves it and returns it to safekeeping with the intention of returning to -
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of contractual will (al-irāda al-‘aqdiyya). The author divides will into two categories: real will (al-irāda al-ḥaqīqiyya), which is the inner intention not outwardly known, and apparent will (al-irāda al-ẓāhira), which is manifested through expression or conduct such as ta‘āṭī (mutual delivery without formal offer-acceptance).

The key legal principle is that mere inner intention is insufficient to create legal effects. For a contract to be formed, there must be an outward expression. Even if both parties inwardly intend the same thing, no contract arises unless it is expressed. Similarly, a person who inwardly intends to divorce his wife or create a waqf does not actually effectuate those acts merely by his resolve.

However, intention (niyya) does have a legally significant role: it qualifies the act it accompanies. The text quotes al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā’ir to illustrate that intention can affect the legal description of an act. For example, when one takes possession of ownerless property (mubāḥāt), the intention to own it makes such appropriation a means of acquiring ownership. In the law of deposit, if a depositary uses the deposited item with intent to benefit himself, he becomes a transgressor (mut‘addin) and is liable for any loss, just like a usurper. But if he returns it to safekeeping with the intention to later resume use? The text cuts off, but the point is clear: intention transforms the legal character of an act.

For the mufti: This passage establishes that legal declarations require outward expression; mere inward thought is non-binding. But once expressed, intention can influence the legal outcome, especially in areas like appropriation, liability, and the validity of acts.

Key Terms
إرادة حقيقية (irāda ḥaqīqiyya): The inner, real will that is not known externally.
إرادة ظاهرة (irāda ẓāhira): The external will expressed through speech or conduct.
نية (niyya): Intention; does not by itself create legal effects but qualifies accompanying acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the role of intention (niyya) in contracts according to the passage?
A) Intention alone can create a contract if both parties agree.
B) Intention has no legal effect whatsoever.
C) Intention can affect the legal description of an act but cannot form a contract by itself.
D) Intention only matters in criminal law.

Key Takeaway
Outward expression is essential for legal acts; intention qualifies but does not create.

The Hanafi Rule on Silence in Contracts: Maxim and Applications
Explains the two types of will and the maxim on silence with furuat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'silence is not acceptance' to concrete cases, identify exceptions, and state the mufta bihi ruling for a given scenario.

Core Idea
Silence does not bind: the maxim 'السكوت لا يعتبر قبولاً' is the default, but custom and context create exceptions.

In Hanafi contract law, the inner will (al-irāda al-ḥaqīqiyya) is distinct from expressed will (al-irāda al-ẓāhira). Only expressed will creates legal effects. A key corollary: silence is not acceptance. This lesson unpacks that maxim, its scope, and its applied rulings, equipping the mufti with a precise tool for evaluating contractual consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Source
State the maxim in Arabic: 'السكوت لا يعتبر في الأصل قبولاً' and its English translation. Note its codification in al-Majallah (art. 67).
This is a sughra (minor) maxim, a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) within the broader Hanafi framework.
2
Classify the Maxim
Recognize its rank (sughra) and type (dabt). Understand its scope: contracts and legal acts, especially acceptance.
Related maxims: 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (complements intention) and 'العادة محكمة' (custom governs exceptions).
3
Apply the Maxim to the Furuat
Go through the four cases from the passage: sale by mutual delivery, renewal of monthly lease, tenant's silence after rent increase, virgin girl's silence in marriage. Explain why each is considered acceptance despite silence.
In all cases, an action or prior relationship supplies the expression.
4
Recognize the Exceptions (Istithna'at)
Identify where silence does produce legal effects: waiving rights (e.g., shuf'a, khiyar al-bulugh) and modern tenancy laws that restrict the effect of silence.
These exceptions are based on custom (urf) and positive law, not on a change of the maxim itself.
5
Evaluate the Mufta Bihi and Modern Restrictions
The mufta bihi is that silence is not acceptance in principle, with exceptions. However, modern legislation may override the classical ruling on rent increases. The mufti must consider both.
The passage notes that today, silence regarding rent increases is not binding acceptance due to protective laws.

أ الإرادة الباطنة الحقيقية :

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وعلى هذا فإنّ السكوت لا يعتبر في الأصل قبولاً، وكان من القواعد
أنه لا ينسب إلى ساكت قول (المجلة / ٦٧) .
وإذا كان الفقهاء قد انتهوا إلى تصحيح التعاقد في بعض الأحوال دون ما لفظ فما ذلك منهم إلا بعد الاستيثاق من وجود عمل ظاهري أو قرينة تقوم مقام اللفظ؛ كما في بيع التعاطي الآنف الشرح، فإن فيه فعلاً تنفيذياً قام مقام اللفظ ؛ وكما في تجدد عقد الإجارة بالسكوت إذا عقد الطرفان الإيجار كل شهر بأجرة معينة، حيث ينعقد على شهر واحد ؛ ثم كلما دخل شهر جديد وهما ساكتان دون ،فسخ، تجددت الإجارة بينهما. ولو أنذر المؤجر المستأجر بزيادة الأجر اعتباراً من الشهر القادم فاستمر في السكنى حتى دخل الشهر وهو ساكت اعتبر قبولاً، ولزمته الأجرة الزائدة (م/ ٤٣٨ و ٤٩٤)(١) .
وكذا سكوت الفتاة البكر إذا استأمرها - أي استأذنها - وليها في عقد تكاحها، إذ يعتبر سكوتها إذناً وتوكيلاً بالعقد استناداً إلى دلالة العادة والعرف، لأن المعتاد من أمثالها الاستحياء عن إظهار الرغبة لا عن الرفض. والقاعدة في السكوت أنه لا أثر له إلا في أنواع من الإسقاطات، كسقوط حق الشفعة بالسكوت عن الطلب، وسقوط خيار البلوغ بسكوت الفتاة عن فسخ النكاح، كما يعرف في مواضعه.

ب : الإرادة الظاهرة :
وأما الإرادة الظاهرة، وهي التعبير عن الإرادة الحقيقية بكلام أو بفعل صادر
(١) هذا مقيد لدينا اليوم بقوانين تمديد عقود الإيجار التي تمنع المؤجر من حق التخلية أو زيادة الأجرة عن النسبة التي عينها القانون، فلا يُعتبر اليوم هذا السكوت قبولاً ملزماً .
Chapter Thirty-Two: Contractual Will. First Section: Types of Will. Apparent Will - Real Hidden Will. 132 - Will is divided into two types: real and apparent. As for real will, it is the inner will that is not disclosed. As for apparent will, it is that which emerges through expression or what stands in its place, such as mutual delivery. A. The Real Inner Will: The inner will alone does not stand in the place of actions and legal acts. A contract is not concluded by mere intention, even if both parties agree on the existence of their intention. Whoever intends divorce or endowment, for example, does not become divorced or a donor by his mere intention and resolve. For everything that is an action is not completed by mere intention. However, intention has a guiding effect recognized by Sharia in describing what it accompanies. So if it accompanies an act or omission, it dyes it with a dye and gives it a quality upon which a specific civil ruling is based in the view of Sharia (1) (see: al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, first art, p. 43). (1) Example of that is the intention of ownership in acquiring permissible things makes the acquisition a cause of ownership. In the rulings of guarantee, they said: if the depositee uses the deposit entrusted to him for safekeeping, he is considered a transgressor against it and is liable for it if it perishes, like a usurper. If he leaves it and returns it to safekeeping intending to return to its use, he remains a usurper liable for it if it perishes, even if its perishing was without his doing or negligence. As for if he leaves it and preserves it with the intention of refraining from using it, then the description of transgression leaves him and he becomes trustworthy, so he is not liable for it if it perishes. In crimes, intention is considered through the arbitration of circumstantial evidence (see al-Majallah, art. 2 and its commentaries). The legal basis for distinguishing intentional from error runs through it. Book Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Based on this, silence is not considered acceptance in principle, and it was among the rules that silence is not attributed to speech (al-Majallah, 67). If the jurists have reached the conclusion to validate contracting in some cases without words, that is only after verifying the existence of an apparent action or indication that stands in place of words; as in the sale by mutual delivery just explained, for it contains an executive action that stands in place of words; and as in the renewal of a lease contract by silence when the two parties contract the lease each month for a specified rent, where it is concluded for one month; then each time a new month enters while they are silent without rescission, the lease is renewed between them. If the landlord notifies the tenant of an increase in rent from the coming month and he continues living until the month enters while silent, it is considered acceptance and the increased rent is due (m/ 438 and 494) (1). Similarly, the silence of a virgin girl when her guardian asks her permission for her marriage contract is considered permission and appointment for the contract based on the indication of custom and tradition, because it is customary for her likes to be shy about showing desire, not about rejection. And the rule regarding silence is that it has no effect except in types of relinquishments, such as the waiver of the right of pre-emption by silence in demanding, and the waiver of the option of puberty by the girl's silence regarding rescission of marriage, as known in its places. B. Apparent Will: As for apparent will, it is the expression of true will by speech or by an action issuing (1) This is restricted today by laws extending lease contracts that prevent the landlord from the right of eviction or increasing the rent above the percentage determined by law, so this silence is not today considered binding acceptance.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses the two types of will in contracts: real (inner) will and apparent (expressed) will. It is a lesson in legal philosophy and maxims. The author begins by defining the two types: real will is the hidden intention, while apparent will is the expression of that intention through words or actions. He then emphasizes a fundamental principle: inner intention alone, even if both parties agree on it, does not constitute a contract. For example, intending divorce or endowment does not effect them without expression. However, intention does have legal significance when it accompanies an act—it can transform the nature of the act, e.g., acquiring permissible things with intent to own creates ownership, or a depositee's intent to use the deposit changes his status to guarantor. The author then turns to a key legal maxim: silence is not considered acceptance in principle (al-Majallah, art. 67). But there are exceptions where silence is treated as acceptance due to custom or a prior contractual context. Examples include: sale by mutual delivery (buyu' al-ta'ati) where action replaces words; renewal of a monthly lease by silence when both parties remain silent after the first month; a tenant's silence after notice of rent increase; and the silence of a virgin girl upon being asked by her guardian for marriage, which is consent due to custom. The rule also notes that silence is effective in waiving rights, such as the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) and the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh). The footnote adds a contemporary restriction: in modern tenancy laws, silence may not constitute acceptance due to protective legislation. This passage provides the mufti with essential principles for evaluating contractual intent and the role of expression.

Key Terms
الإرادة الحقيقية (al-irada al-haqiqiyya): The inner, hidden will that is not disclosed.
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irada al-zahira): The will that emerges through speech, action, or equivalent signs.
السكوت (al-sukut): Refraining from speech; in contracts, usually not considered acceptance unless custom or context indicates otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the default ruling regarding silence as acceptance in contracts?
A) Silence is always considered acceptance.
B) Silence is never considered acceptance under any circumstances.
C) Silence is not considered acceptance in principle, but custom or context may make it so.
D) Silence is considered acceptance only in sales.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'silence is not acceptance' requires expression for contracts, but custom and prior dealings can turn silence into acceptance, with exceptions for waivers and under modern law.

Apparent Will and Custom in Contract Formation
Explaining reliance on apparent will and the integrating role of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that apparent will determines contract validity and use the maxim 'al-ma'ruf urfan' to interpret contract terms.

Core Idea
Apparent will suffices for contract; custom fills unstated terms as if explicitly stipulated.

In Hanafi contract law, the expressed will (offer and acceptance) is the foundation of a binding agreement. However, not every detail is explicitly stated. This lesson explains how apparent will is supplemented by custom and circumstantial evidence, and introduces the key maxim that what is customary has the force of a stipulation.

Chapter 32: Contractual Management. The apparent will is the operative factor in the contract without needing to search for the true inner will, as long as that true will is hidden and there is no evidence negating it. Thus the apparent will remains alone in the field of expression; it becomes sufficient evidence for the existence of the true will, and the rulings of the contract are established by this apparent will, which is then considered the operative factor in the origination of the contract and in defining its limits and binding restrictions. Included in the apparent will regarding the limits and restrictions of the contract is what is indicated by circumstantial evidence or the customs and habits of people, because circumstantial evidence and customs have additional significant indications that the contracting parties rely upon and with which they dispense with explicit expression; so their indication must be like expression, and therefore in the view of the jurists of the Shariah they have a far-reaching effect in interpreting the contract and defining its limits and obligations. Among the established legal maxims is: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition' (see: F 5/6). Considered its inclusion. Thus a sale contract in which the accessories of the sold item are not specified includes what the situation indicates should be included. For example, in the sale of a milch cow, its calf is included without mention in the contract, so it [the calf] is brought with it to the place of sale. And a lease contract if the commencement of its term is not specified, the term is considered from the date of the contract. And an agency to purchase restricts the agent to the market price. And authorizing a porter to purchase an animal applies to pack animals, not riding animals. And a sale or lease contract and the like, if the method of payment is not specified and the people's custom is to pay in installments, the payment is considered to be in installments according to their custom, and so on. That is because the true will in all such examples, by indication of circumstantial evidence or custom, is considered apparent, no longer residing in the realm of concealment and hiddenness; and since encompassing all limits and restrictions is difficult, it is presumed that the contracting party relies on the circumstantial evidence or custom. Book Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Second Section: Concealment of the True Will, its Absence, and Doubt Therein. The difference between concealment of the true will, its absence, and doubt therein, and the results thereof: First: The theory of fictitious contracts (mudawada or talji'a) - manipulation in consideration - manipulation in person - the issue of 'borrowed name' - jest. Second: The theory of defects of will or defects of consent. 432 - The operation of the apparent will in the formation of the contract, although it does not require investigation into the existence of the true inner will, its effect is suspended if the absence of this true will appears alongside it, because the true will is no longer hidden in the realm of concealment, and the apparent will is not alone in expressing it; rather, other evidence emerges that contradicts the indication of the apparent will and proves the absence of the true will or doubt and uncertainty about it. Therefore, the difference must be noted in this context between three cases: 1) concealment of the true will, 2) its absence, and 3) doubt therein. As for its concealment, it is disregarded, because the apparent will through offer and acceptance has taken its place. As for its absence, it indicates that the offer and acceptance did not truthfully express the intention to contract. As for doubt, it is an intermediate between concealment and absence, when the true will is presumed to exist and there is no evidence of its absence, but it is marred by a defect.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' deals with the foundational legal philosophy of contract formation in Hanafi fiqh. The author opens by establishing the principle of reliance on the apparent will (al-irada al-zahira) — the expressed offer and acceptance — as sufficient for contract validity, without probing the inner hidden will, as long as there is no evidence contradicting it. This principle ensures legal stability. He then expands this by noting that circumstantial evidence and custom are part of the apparent will's expression, citing the maxim 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً' (What is known by custom is like a stipulated condition). He provides concrete Hanafi examples: in a sale of a milch cow, the calf is automatically included due to custom; in a lease, the term starts from the contract date if unspecified; an agent is bound to the market price; authorizing a porter to buy an animal implies a pack animal, not a riding one; and if payment method is unspecified, custom (e.g., installments) governs. The author then introduces the critical distinction among three states regarding the true will: concealment (خفاء) — ignored, apparent will suffices; absence (انتفاء) — contract invalid because offer and acceptance do not sincerely reflect intent; doubt (اشتباه) — intermediate, when the true will is presumed but tainted by a defect (explored in later sections on sham contracts and defects of consent). This passage sets the stage for understanding contract formation and invalidity in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irada al-zahira): The expressed will of the contracting party through offer and acceptance, supplemented by circumstantial evidence and custom, which suffices for contract formation in the absence of contrary evidence.
الإرادة الحقيقية (al-irada al-haqiqiyya): The true inner will or intention of the contracting party, which may be hidden, absent, or doubtful, affecting contract validity.
المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً (al-ma'ruf urfan ka al-mashrut shartan): A legal maxim meaning that customary practice has the same effect as an explicit stipulation in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi position on the role of apparent will in contracts?
A) The inner true will must always be investigated for validity.
B) The apparent will (offer and acceptance) is sufficient unless evidence contradicts the true will.
C) Custom cannot supplement any missing terms.
D) The contract is invalid if any term is unspecified.

Key Takeaway
Apparent will and custom form the basis of contract validity in Hanafi law; custom fills silent terms.

Three States of Actual Will: Concealment, Absence, and Doubt
Distinguishing three states of true will in contract law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between concealment, absence, and doubt of the true will and identify their effects on contract validity.

Core Idea
Concealed true will is ignored; absent true will invalidates; doubtful true will may require further investigation.

The apparent will usually suffices for contract validity, but when evidence of the true inner will contradicts it, the contract's fate changes. This lesson explains the three states of the true will—hidden, absent, or doubtful—and the legal consequences of each.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default: apparent will prevails when true will is concealed
When the true will is hidden but not contradicted, the apparent will determines validity.
Concealment is the normal state.
2
Identify absence: when offer and acceptance do not truly reflect intent
If evidence proves the true will is absent (e.g., sham contract), the contract is void.
Examples: talji'a (fictitious sale), jest.
3
Recognize doubt: intermediate state where true will is presumed but tainted
Doubt arises when there is some defect (e.g., duress, fraud) that calls the true will into question.
Contract may be voidable or invalid.
4
Apply to the examples from the text
The text mentions sham contracts and defects of consent as examples of absence and doubt.
