Chapter 32: Contractual Management. The apparent will is the operative factor in the contract without needing to search for the true inner will, as long as that true will is hidden and there is no evidence negating it. Thus the apparent will remains alone in the field of expression; it becomes sufficient evidence for the existence of the true will, and the rulings of the contract are established by this apparent will, which is then considered the operative factor in the origination of the contract and in defining its limits and binding restrictions. Included in the apparent will regarding the limits and restrictions of the contract is what is indicated by circumstantial evidence or the customs and habits of people, because circumstantial evidence and customs have additional significant indications that the contracting parties rely upon and with which they dispense with explicit expression; so their indication must be like expression, and therefore in the view of the jurists of the Shariah they have a far-reaching effect in interpreting the contract and defining its limits and obligations. Among the established legal maxims is: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated by condition' (see: F 5/6). Considered its inclusion. Thus a sale contract in which the accessories of the sold item are not specified includes what the situation indicates should be included. For example, in the sale of a milch cow, its calf is included without mention in the contract, so it [the calf] is brought with it to the place of sale. And a lease contract if the commencement of its term is not specified, the term is considered from the date of the contract. And an agency to purchase restricts the agent to the market price. And authorizing a porter to purchase an animal applies to pack animals, not riding animals. And a sale or lease contract and the like, if the method of payment is not specified and the people's custom is to pay in installments, the payment is considered to be in installments according to their custom, and so on. That is because the true will in all such examples, by indication of circumstantial evidence or custom, is considered apparent, no longer residing in the realm of concealment and hiddenness; and since encompassing all limits and restrictions is difficult, it is presumed that the contracting party relies on the circumstantial evidence or custom. Book Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Second Section: Concealment of the True Will, its Absence, and Doubt Therein. The difference between concealment of the true will, its absence, and doubt therein, and the results thereof: First: The theory of fictitious contracts (mudawada or talji'a) - manipulation in consideration - manipulation in person - the issue of 'borrowed name' - jest. Second: The theory of defects of will or defects of consent. 432 - The operation of the apparent will in the formation of the contract, although it does not require investigation into the existence of the true inner will, its effect is suspended if the absence of this true will appears alongside it, because the true will is no longer hidden in the realm of concealment, and the apparent will is not alone in expressing it; rather, other evidence emerges that contradicts the indication of the apparent will and proves the absence of the true will or doubt and uncertainty about it. Therefore, the difference must be noted in this context between three cases: 1) concealment of the true will, 2) its absence, and 3) doubt therein. As for its concealment, it is disregarded, because the apparent will through offer and acceptance has taken its place. As for its absence, it indicates that the offer and acceptance did not truthfully express the intention to contract. As for doubt, it is an intermediate between concealment and absence, when the true will is presumed to exist and there is no evidence of its absence, but it is marred by a defect.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' deals with the foundational legal philosophy of contract formation in Hanafi fiqh. The author opens by establishing the principle of reliance on the apparent will (al-irada al-zahira) — the expressed offer and acceptance — as sufficient for contract validity, without probing the inner hidden will, as long as there is no evidence contradicting it. This principle ensures legal stability. He then expands this by noting that circumstantial evidence and custom are part of the apparent will's expression, citing the maxim 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً' (What is known by custom is like a stipulated condition). He provides concrete Hanafi examples: in a sale of a milch cow, the calf is automatically included due to custom; in a lease, the term starts from the contract date if unspecified; an agent is bound to the market price; authorizing a porter to buy an animal implies a pack animal, not a riding one; and if payment method is unspecified, custom (e.g., installments) governs. The author then introduces the critical distinction among three states regarding the true will: concealment (خفاء) — ignored, apparent will suffices; absence (انتفاء) — contract invalid because offer and acceptance do not sincerely reflect intent; doubt (اشتباه) — intermediate, when the true will is presumed but tainted by a defect (explored in later sections on sham contracts and defects of consent). This passage sets the stage for understanding contract formation and invalidity in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
خفاء الإرادة الحقيقية (khafa' al-irada al-haqiqiyya): Concealment of the true will; the default state where the inner intent is hidden but not contradicted.
انتفاء الإرادة الحقيقية (intifa' al-irada al-haqiqiyya): Absence of the true will; proven lack of genuine intent to contract.
الاشتباه في الإرادة الحقيقية (al-ishtibah fi al-irada al-haqiqiyya): Doubt about the true will; when there is some defect (e.g., duress, fraud) but no proof of complete absence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal consequence of a proven absence (intifa') of the true will?
A) The contract is valid.
B) The contract is void.
C) The contract is voidable.
D) The contract requires additional investigation.

Key Takeaway
The true will can be concealed (no effect), absent (void), or doubtful (voidable); the apparent will governs unless contradicted.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Three States of True Intention and Their Legal Consequences
Explaining the three states of true intention and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between concealment, absence, and doubt of true intention in contracts and identify the resulting theories of simulated contracts and defects of consent.

Core Idea
True intention matters only when absent or doubtful; mere concealment is legally irrelevant.

In Islamic contract law, the apparent intention expressed through offer and acceptance is prima facie binding. However, when evidence emerges that the inner true intention is absent or clouded by doubt, the law intervenes. This lesson explores the three possible states of true intention—concealment, absence, and doubt—and explains how each affects the validity of a contract. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when assessing the validity of contracts and the existence of genuine consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states of true intention
Locate in the passage the three states: concealment (khafā'), absence (intifā'), and doubt (ishtibāh). Note that concealment is disregarded, absence invalidates, and doubt creates a defect.
The three states are explicitly listed in the Arabic text.
2
Understand why concealment is disregarded
The passage states that if true intention is merely hidden, the apparent intention suffices; no inquiry into the inner intention is required. This aligns with the Hanafi principle of relying on external manifestations.
Concealment is a default state; it does not affect the contract's existence.
3
Differentiate absence from doubt
Absence means the parties never truly intended the contract; the expression is insincere. Doubt means there is a taint (e.g., coercion, deception) that casts uncertainty on the true intention but does not prove its absence.
Absence leads to simulation (ṣūriyya); doubt leads to defects of consent (ʿuyūb al-riḍā).
4
Connect the states to the two legal theories
The passage tells us that from absence arises the theory of simulated contracts (void contracts lacking substance); from doubt arises the theory of defects of consent (valid but defective contracts with rights of rescission).
This framework is foundational for analyzing contractual validity.

The Difference Between Concealment of True Intention, Its Absence, and Doubt Concerning It, and the Consequences Thereof. First: The Theory of Simulated Contracts – Collusion or Evasion – Collusion in the Countervalue – Collusion in the Person – or the Issue of 'Pseudonym' – Jest. Second: The Theory of Impurities of Intention or Defects of Consent. 432 – The effect of apparent intention in the formation of a contract, even if it does not require investigation into the existence of the inner true intention, its effect ceases if the absence of this true intention appears along with it; because the true intention is no longer hidden in the realm of concealment, and the apparent intention is the sole expression of it; rather, other evidence emerges that contradicts the indication of the apparent intention and proves the absence of the true intention or doubt and uncertainty about it. [Page 414] Based on the latter two states (absence of contractual intention and doubt concerning it), two theories arose: - The theory of simulated contracts. - And the theory of impurities of intention or defects of consent. a) If the agreement of the two parties in the contract is only apparent, and the absence of true intention in the foundation of the contract is established, then the contract is simulated – i.e., it has only the appearance and form of a contract, not its reality and essence. b) If the true intention is not proven to be absent, but something exists that places us in doubt and uncertainty about it, the contract is valid, but there is a defect in its pillar that weakens the contractual bond, called in legal terminology defects of consent. We will present the cases of simulated contracts in Chapter 33; as for defects of consent or impurities of intention and their rulings, we will present them successively in the following chapters: - Coercion in Chapter 34. - Deception (Khalaba) in Chapter 35. - Error in Chapter 36. - Supervening defects of consent due to imbalance in performance in Chapter 37. 439
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic contract theory: the treatment of the true inner intention (al-irāda al-ḥaqīqiyya) versus the apparent intention (al-irāda al-ẓāhira). The author, Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, classifies three possible states of the true intention: concealment (khafā'), absence (intifā'), and doubt (ishtibāh). He argues that mere concealment is legally irrelevant—the apparent intention expressed through offer and acceptance suffices to form a contract, and one does not inquire into hidden motives. However, when there is evidence of the actual absence of true intention (e.g., the parties did not genuinely intend the contract) or when there is doubt about it (e.g., due to coercion, deception, or error), the law intervenes. This leads to two distinct theories: (1) simulated contracts (ṣūriyyat al-ʿuqūd), where the contract is a mere form without substance, rendering it invalid; and (2) defects of consent (ʿuyūb al-riḍā), where the contract is valid but has a defect that may give one party the right to rescind or annul the contract. The passage sets the stage for subsequent chapters that detail specific defects such as coercion (ikrāh), deception (khalāba), error (ghalaṭ), and supervening defects. This analysis is consistent with the Hanafi school's emphasis on the objective manifestation of intent, tempered by safeguards against exploitation and genuine lack of consent.

Key Terms
إرادة حقيقية (al-irāda al-ḥaqīqiyya): The true inner intention of a party to a contract, which may be concealed, absent, or subject to doubt.
إرادة ظاهرة (al-irāda al-ẓāhira): The expressed intention through offer and acceptance, which forms the basis of contract formation regardless of hidden motives.
صورية العقود (ṣūriyyat al-ʿuqūd): Simulated contracts where the external form of a contract exists but the parties do not actually intend its legal effects (e.g., collusive contracts or evasion).
عيوب الرضا (ʿuyūb al-riḍā): Defects of consent that affect the validity of a contract, such as coercion, deception, error, or other factors that vitiate free will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Concealment of true intention
B) Absence of true intention
C) Doubt concerning true intention
D) Both absence and doubt

Key Takeaway
True intention in contracts matters only when proven absent or doubtful; mere concealment does not affect validity.

The Two Theories: Simulation and Defects of Consent
Introducing conceptual distinction between two types of contractual invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a simulated contract and a contract with a defect of consent, and explain the legal consequences of each in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A contract either lacks real will (simulation) or has a defective will (defect of consent); the legal treatment differs fundamentally.

This passage sets the foundation for understanding contractual invalidities in Islamic law. A mufti must first determine whether the contract lacks real will entirely or whether the will exists but is tainted. The distinction between simulation and defect of consent is critical because the first renders the contract void ab initio, while the second makes it voidable. The upcoming chapters will detail each defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two situations: (A) absence of real will in the contract (simulation) and (B) suspicion or doubt about the will (defects of consent). Note that these are mutually exclusive categories.
2
Define simulation
Simulation occurs when the agreement is only outward; there is no real intention to contract. The contract has the form but not the essence.
Hanafi: simulation renders the contract void (batil) as the essential element of consent is missing.
3
Define defect of consent
A defect of consent means the real will is present but something (e.g., coercion, fraud) creates uncertainty about it. The contract is valid in form but has a defect in its pillar.
Hanafi: such contracts are voidable (qabil li al-ibtal), not automatically void.
4
Contrast legal consequences
Simulation: the contract is void ab initio; it never had legal effect. Defect of consent: the contract is valid until annulled by the affected party.
This determines the mufti's ruling: void vs voidable.
5
Preview the defects
The passage lists four types of defects to be covered: coercion, deception, error, and supervening defects due to impaired performance.
Each will be studied in later chapters.

الإكراه في الفصل ٣٤.
الخلابة، في الفصل ٣٥.
الغلط، في الفصل ٣٦.
- عيوب الرضا الطارئة بسبب اختلال التنفيذ، في الفصل ٣٧.
٤٣٩
Chapter (32) Contractual Volition: An influence that makes us doubt the position of the contracting party in entering the contract, were it not for the effect on his will. Based on the latter two cases (absence of contractual will, and suspicion thereof), two theories arise: - Theory of simulation of contracts. - Theory of defects of will, or defects of consent. (A) If the agreement of the two parties is only apparent, and the absence of real will in the very contract is verified, the contract is simulated, i.e., it has only the appearance and form of a contract, not its reality and essence. (B) If the real will is not verified to be absent, but rather there exists something that puts us in uncertainty and doubt about it, the contract is valid, but in its pillar there is a defect that weakens the contractual bond, called in legal terminology defects of consent. We will present the cases of simulation in Chapter 33. As for defects of consent or defects of will and their rulings, we will present them in sequence in the following chapters: - Coercion in Chapter 34. - Deception in Chapter 35. - Error in Chapter 36. - Supervening defects of consent due to impaired performance in Chapter 37.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational conceptual distinction in Islamic contract law: the difference between a contract where the will is entirely absent (simulation) and a contract where the will exists but is affected by a defect (defects of consent). The author explains that from these two scenarios, two legal theories emerge: the theory of simulated contracts and the theory of defects of consent. In simulation, the parties' agreement is only outward; there is no real intention to contract, so the contract is invalid in essence. In defects of consent, the will is present but tainted by factors like coercion or deception, rendering the contract valid but defective. This distinction is crucial for a mufti because the legal consequences differ: a simulated contract is void ab initio, while a contract with a defect may be voidable. The author then outlines the upcoming chapters where each type of defect will be discussed in detail.

Key Terms
الإرادة العقدية (al-irādat al-'aqdiyyah): The intention and will of a party to enter into a contract, which is essential for the contract's validity.
صورية العقود (ṣūriyyat al-‘uqūd): A contract that outwardly appears valid but lacks real intent from the parties, i.e., it is a sham.
عيوب الرضا (‘uyūb al-riḍā): Defects that affect the consent of a party, such as coercion, fraud, or error, making the contract voidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A simulated contract lacks real will entirely, while a defect of consent leaves the will present but uncertain.
B) A simulated contract is valid, while a defect of consent makes it invalid.
C) Both are the same in legal effect.
D) A simulated contract involves coercion, while a defect involves error.

Key Takeaway
Simulation voids a contract entirely; a defect of consent makes it voidable.

Simulation in Contracts: Collusive Simulation (Al-Muwada'a)
Defining simulation and detailing the first type: collusive simulation (al-muwada'a)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main types of simulation in contracts (collusion and jest) and apply the Hanafi ruling that collusive simulation renders a contract void due to lack of genuine consent.

Core Idea
Simulated contracts are null because real consent is absent; Hanafi law voids them as fraudulent.

Simulation in contracts undermines the essential element of mutual consent. The Hanafi school distinguishes between collusion (al-muwāḍa‘ah) and jest (al-hazl). In this lesson, we examine the first type—collusive simulation—where parties secretly agree that the outward contract is a sham, often to defraud others. Such contracts are void ab initio.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two categories of simulation
The passage states that simulation appears in two cases: collusion (al-muwāḍa‘ah) and jest (al-hazl). Distinguish between them: collusion involves a secret prior agreement, while jest lacks serious intent but not necessarily secrecy.
Both categories involve a discrepancy between appearance and reality, but the ruling differs.
2
Define collusive simulation (al-muwāḍa‘ah)
The passage defines it as the parties secretly agreeing to the contrary of what they will declare. Identify its essence: a secret pact that the outward contract will have no real legal effect.
Also called 'al-talji'ah' (recourse).
3
Identify the three forms under collusion
Collusion can occur in the contract itself, in the consideration (price), or in the person (identity of a party). Focus on the first form for this lesson.
The passage elaborates only on collusion in the contract itself.
4
Recognize the motivations and fraudulent purposes
Common examples: debtors hiding assets from creditors, or creating fake loans to allow relatives to share in bankruptcy distribution. The motive may also be fear of an unjust ruler.
These are all fraudulent acts that harm the rights of third parties.
5
Apply the Hanafi ruling
Because the essential element of genuine consent (rīḍā) is missing, the contract is void (bāṭil). The passage implies this through the description of simulation as 'only outward form' without actual conclusion.
Hanafi: lack of true consent invalidates the contract from its inception.

Chapter Thirty-Three: Simulation of Contracts. 1/33 - Simulation appears in two cases: 1 - The case of collusion (al-muwāḍa‘ah) or recourse (al-talji’ah), and 2 - The case of jest (al-hazl). Section One: Collusion (al-Muwāḍa‘ah). As for collusion, it is that the two contracting parties secretly agree to the contrary of what they will declare. Under it there are three forms: collusion in the very contract, in the consideration, and in the person. 2/33 - 233 - A. Collusion in the very contract: It is that the two contracting parties collude and secretly agree before the contract that they will conclude the contract only in outward form, as a pretense to others, without it being actually concluded between them, for some purpose of theirs or one of them in the false contract they declare. This often occurs from debtors when they sell their goods collusively to smuggle them away from creditors, or they make simulated debts and loans on themselves to some of their special associates or relatives to jostle with real creditors in dividing the bankrupt debtor's assets, then secretly return it to the debtor with whom they colluded in the false debt. The motive for collusion may be fear of an unjust ruler or...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines simulation in contracts and then focuses on the first type: collusive simulation (al-muwāḍa‘ah). The author explains that simulation takes two forms: collusion (where parties secretly agree to contradict their public declaration) and jest (where the contract is made in humor without serious intent). Under collusion, there are three subcategories: in the contract itself, in the consideration (price), and in the person. The passage elaborates on collusion in the contract itself: the parties secretly agree that the contract is purely outward, with no real legal effect, often to deceive third parties. The author provides concrete examples from practice: debtors selling their property collusively to hide assets from creditors, or creating fake loans to relatives so that they can participate in the distribution of the bankrupt's estate, then secretly returning the money. This is a prohibited form of simulation because it involves fraud and harm to creditors. The passage also mentions that collusion may be motivated by fear of an unjust ruler. Hanafi law would treat such simulated contracts as void, because the essential element of real consent is missing.

Key Terms
صورية العقود (ṣūriyyat al-‘uqūd): A contract that is only outward form without real intention, classified into collusion and jest.
المواضعة (al-muwāḍa‘ah): A secret agreement between parties to make a contract appear different from their true intention, often to deceive others.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or humor; making a contract in jest without serious intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The contract is made in jest.
D) The contract involves a mistake about the subject matter.

Key Takeaway
A contract that is only outward form without genuine consent is void in Hanafi law, whether the simulation is through collusion or jest, but the ruling differs: collusion is always prohibited, while jest may be valid.

Muwada‘ah: Simulated Contracts in Hanafi Law
Definition and legal analysis of collusive simulation in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define muwada‘ah, identify its three forms, and apply the Hanafi rule that the contract is void in reality but presumed valid until collusion is proven.

Core Idea
A collusive contract is void for lack of real intent, but its external validity is presumed until proven otherwise.

The Hanafi school recognizes that contracts may be simulated (ṣūriyyah) when the parties secretly agree to a different reality than what they publicly declare. This simulation, called muwāḍa‘ah or taljī‘ah, is a critical concept for the mufti to identify and adjudicate. This lesson parses the definition, forms, motives, and legal rulings of simulated contracts as laid out by al-Zarqa.

Guided Path
1
Define muwada‘ah and distinguish from hazl
Read the opening section carefully. Muwada‘ah is a secret prior agreement contrary to the announced contract. Compare it to hazl (jest) which also lacks real intent but without pre-agreed collusion.
The source states simulation appears in two cases: muwada‘ah and hazl.
2
Identify the three forms of collusion
Al-Zarqa lists: (a) in the essence of the contract (e.g., a fake sale), (b) in the consideration (e.g., fake price), (c) in the person (e.g., a dummy buyer). Find examples in the text.
The three forms are explicitly stated in paragraph 2/33.
3
Extract the legal effect: void in reality, valid in appearance
The author says the contract is 'bāṭil fī al-wāqi‘' (void in reality) because the essential element of true intention is missing. However, it is treated as valid in the external world until someone proves collusion.
This dual effect is unique: internal voidness vs. external presumption of validity.
4
Analyze the burden of proof in disputes
When a dispute arises over simulation, the contract is presumed genuine. The one claiming muwada‘ah must prove it. Both parties and harmed third parties (e.g., creditors, heirs) may challenge.
The burden lies on the one alleging simulation, as per the maxim 'al-aṣl fi al-‘uqūd al-ṣiḥḥah'.

العقد أو الإقرار الصوريين.
Chapter Thirty-Three: Fictitiousness of Contracts. 1/33 - The fictitiousness of contracts appears in two situations: 1 - The case of collusion (muwada‘ah) or refuge-giving (talji‘ah), and 2 - The case of jest (hazl). Section One: Collusion (al-muwada‘ah). As for collusion, it is that the two contracting parties secretly agree contrary to what they will announce. Under it are three forms: collusion in the contract itself, in the consideration, and in the person. 2/33 - 233: a - Collusion in the contract itself: It is that the two contracting parties reach a secret understanding and agreement before the contract that they will conclude the contract only in apparent form, to delude others, while in reality it is not concluded between them, for some purpose of theirs or one of them in what they announce of the false contract. This often happens from debtors when they sell their properties in collusion to smuggle them from their creditors, or they conclude fictitious loans and debts upon themselves in favor of some of their relatives or close associates to crowd out real creditors in dividing the bankrupt debtor's property, then secretly return it to the debtor with whom they conspired on the false debt. The motive for collusion may be fear of the oppression of a tyrannical ruler or an overpowering usurper, especially in places or times where despotism prevails and the authority of the judiciary is weak, so the owner of property takes refuge with another and agrees with him to sell it to him in an apparent, non-genuine sale. Also, collusion may occur with respect to an acknowledgement of a sum of money or a contract, not to the creation of the contract, such as when a bequeather acknowledges a debt or contract in favor of some of his heirs to make it a means and pretext for preferring him in the inheritance share over other heirs. Because collusion involves a secret conspiracy between the two parties, it is called 'muwata‘ah' (agreement) or 'tawatu‘' (collusion). And because it involves a person taking refuge with another through this contract to take the fictitious contract as an apparent cover for fear of a usurper or oppressor, it is also called in this case 'talji‘ah' (refuge-giving). Thus, this type of collusion, whether related to the creation of the contract or the acknowledgement thereof, renders the contract and the acknowledgement void in reality, even if they stand in appearance, because of the absence of true intention from the very basis of the contract or acknowledgement. And when there is a dispute as to whether the contract or acknowledgement is collusive or genuine, it is considered genuine, and judicially the word of the one who upholds it is taken until the other party proves the collusion. Each of the two parties may claim collusion and prove it, or may request his opponent to take an oath denying it. And the third party harmed by the collusion, such as the creditor or the heir, may also claim it and prove it, or request an oath denying it in order to establish the nullity of the fictitious contract or acknowledgement.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work defines and analyzes the concept of 'مَواضعة' (muwada‘ah), which is a form of simulated contract (صورية العقود). The author begins by stating that contract simulation appears in two main situations: collusion (muwada‘ah) and jest (hazl). He then focuses on muwada‘ah, explaining that it involves a secret prior agreement between the parties contrary to what they publicly declare. There are three types: collusion in the essence of the contract (i.e., the contract itself is fake), in the consideration (price or object), or in the person (e.g., a dummy party). The passage then describes the common scenarios where debtors simulate sales to hide assets from creditors or create fictitious loans to inflate the pool of creditors, ultimately to defraud real creditors. Another motive is fear of oppression from a tyrannical ruler or usurper, driving a person to make a simulated sale as a protective measure. The author also notes that muwada‘ah can apply not only to the creation of a contract but also to acknowledgements (إقرار), e.g., a testator acknowledging a debt in favor of one heir to give him an advantage over others in inheritance. Because of the secret nature, it is also called 'مواطأة' (mutual agreement) or 'تلجئة' (refuge contract). The legal effect is that such a contract or acknowledgement is void in reality (باطل في الواقع) because the essential element of true intention (الإرادة الحقيقية) is missing. However, in the external world, the contract appears valid and is treated as such until the collusion is proven. If a dispute arises over whether a contract is simulated or real, it is presumed genuine, and the burden of proof falls on the one claiming simulation. Both parties to the contract, as well as any third party harmed (like a creditor or heir), have the right to allege and prove collusion or demand an oath from the other party denying it.

Key Terms
المواضعة (al-muwāḍa‘ah): A secret agreement between contracting parties to conclude a contract that appears genuine but is not intended to be real; used for fraudulent or protective purposes.
التلجئة (al-talji‘ah): A type of muwada‘ah where a person takes refuge by making a simulated contract to protect property from oppression or usurpation.
صورية العقود (ṣūriyyat al-‘uqūd): The state where a contract is apparent but not real; simulation of contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only a judge or qadi.

D) Only the party that did not participate in the contract.

Key Takeaway
A collusive contract is void in reality due to missing intention, but it is presumed valid in the external world until the one claiming collusion proves it.

Jest Contracts and the Three Exceptions in Hanafi Law
Explains the rule and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' to determine whether a contract made in jest is valid, and identify the three exceptions (nikah, talaq, itaq) where jest is treated as serious.

Core Idea
Jest contracts are void except for marriage, divorce, and manumission.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule that contracts made in jest (hazl) are void (batil) due to the absence of real intention. However, Islamic law makes an exception for three critically important actions—marriage, divorce, and manumission—based on a prophetic hadith. The governing legal maxim is 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' (giving effect to speech is better than neglecting it).

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify that a contract made in jest (hazl) is considered void (batil) and produces no legal effects because the real intention (irada haqiqiyya) is absent. Note that this applies to all contracts and verbal dispositions.
The jester does not intend the legal consequences.
2
Identify the governing qawida
Recognize that the maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' supports the presumption that speech is meant seriously. In a dispute, the party claiming seriousness is believed; the other must prove jest.
This complements the principle 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة'.
3
Recognize the scope of the rule
Understand that fictitiousness (surahiyya) can render any contract void, but the rule has a boundary: there are three exceptions where the law disregards actual intent.
The general rule is subject to istithna'.
4
List the three exceptions and their dalil
Name the exceptions: marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itaq). Cite the hadith: 'Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and manumission.' Explain that even if uttered in jest, these actions are legally effective.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah; scholars differ on its strength but the ruling is accepted.
5
Apply the rule to a furu' case
Given a scenario (e.g., a gift contract made in jest), state that the gift is void (batil) and ownership does not transfer. Then apply the exception: if a marriage contract is made in jest, it is valid and binding.
The exception covers only the three specific actions.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
سيعقدانه هو هَزْل أو لعب لا يراد حكمه، ثم يعقدا متفاهمين على هذا الأساس.
- وإما بقرائن الحال التي تدل على أنَّ المتكلم هازل أو مستهزىء. فالهازل، وإن كان يتكلم بصيغة العقد بإرادته ورضاه، هو عابث لا يريد الحكم، بل يأباه .
ولذا يعتبر العقد بالهزل عقداً صورياً باطلاً غير منعقد، فلا يترتب عليه حكم أصلاً فلو كان بيعاً لا يفيد انتقال ملكية المبيع ولو قبضه المشتري لانتفاء الإرادة الحقيقية منه (١) .
(۱) والظاهر من عبارات الفقهاء، أنه عند الاختلاف بين العاقدين في كون العقد هزلاً أو جداً فالقول لمن يتمسك بالجد لأنه الأصل في الكلام؛ ومن القواعد أنَّ إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله»؛ وللطرف الآخر إثبات الهزل كما تقدم بيانه في المواضعة، لأنَّ الهزل والمواضعة أخوان .
هذا، وإنَّ الصورية لباس يمكن أن تلبسه العقود جميع والتصرفات القولية فتفقد به اعتبارها وأحكامها إلا في ثلاثة مواطن لم يقبل التشريع الإسلامي فيها صورية، بل أعطى فيها التصرف القولي الظاهر نتائجه كاملة دون نظر إلى الإرادة الحقيقية وهذه المستثنيات الثلاثة النكاح والطلاق والعتاق، لقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: ثلاث جدهنَّ جِد، وهَزْلُهنَّ جِد النكاح والطلاق والعتاق» (۱) . فعقد النكاح الجاري بالهزل أو بالمواضعة على صوريته يصح وينت نتائجه بين المتعاقدين فيحل المتعة بينهما ويلزمهما بوجائبه .
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (٢١٩٤)، والترمذي (١١٨٤) وابن ماجه (۲۰۳۹) من حديث أبي هريرة. وقال الترمذي حديث حسن غريب. وقال الحاكم ۱۹۸/۲ : صحيح الإسناد، ورده الذهبي بقوله : فيه لين. وذكر الزيلعي في نصب الراية ٢٩٤/٣ في معناه أحاديث أخرى وآثاراً عن الصحابة تدل على ثبوته وقوته .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
... they intend it as jest or play not intending its legal effect, then they contract understanding each other on this basis.
- Or by contextual evidence indicating that the speaker is jesting or mocking. The jester, even if he utters the contract form with his will and consent, is playing, not intending the legal effect, rather rejecting it.
Hence the contract made in jest is considered a fictitious contract, void and not concluded, so no legal effect follows from it at all. For example, if it were a sale, it would not effect transfer of ownership of the sold item even if the buyer took possession, due to absence of real intention (1).
(1) It appears from the statements of the jurists that when there is disagreement between the contracting parties as to whether the contract was jest or serious, the word is given to the one who insists on seriousness because that is the origin in speech; and from the maxims: 'Giving effect to speech is better than neglecting it'; and for the other party to prove jest, as previously explained in the case of prearranged simulation (mawada'a), because jest and prearranged simulation are brothers.
Moreover, fictitiousness is a garment that all contracts and verbal disposals can wear, thereby losing their validity and effects, except in three instances where Islamic law did not accept fictitiousness in them; rather it gave the apparent verbal disposal its full effects without regard to the real intention. These three exceptions are: marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itaq), due to the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and manumission.' (1) Thus a marriage contract performed in jest or with prearranged simulation on its fictitiousness is valid and produces its effects between the contracting parties, making sexual relations lawful between them and binding them with its obligations.
(1) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2194), al-Tirmidhi (1184), and Ibn Majah (2039) from the hadith of Abu Hurayra. Al-Tirmidhi said: hasan gharib. Al-Hakim said (2/198): sahih al-isnad, but al-Dhahabi rejected it saying: there is weakness in it. Al-Zayla'i in Nasb al-Raya (3/294) mentioned other hadiths and reports from the Companions indicating its strength and authenticity.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effect of contracts made in jest (hazl) and the concept of fictitious contracts (surah). The author explains that a jest contract is considered void (batil) and produces no legal effects because real intention (irada) is absent. The Hanafi position, supported by the muhaqqiqun, is that such contracts are void, not merely voidable. In case of dispute over whether a contract was jest or serious, the presumption is in favor of seriousness because the default in speech is that it is given effect (إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله). The party alleging jest must prove it. The passage then states that all contracts and verbal dispositions can be rendered fictitious (surah) except in three matters: marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itaq). These exceptions are based on the hadith: 'Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and manumission.' This means that even if uttered in jest, these three actions are legally effective. The author provides the hadith citation and notes its authentication by various scholars. The underlying rationale is that these matters involve serious consequences affecting lineage, family, and personal freedom, so the law disregards the actual intent to protect social order. The passage concludes that a marriage contract performed in jest or with prearranged simulation (mawada'a) is valid and binding.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest; a statement made in play or humor without intending its legal effect.
الصورية (al-surahiyya): Fictitiousness; a situation where the outward form of a contract is present but the real intention is absent.
المواضعة (al-mawada'a): Prearranged simulation; parties agree beforehand that a contract is pro forma without real effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which three contracts are valid even if made in jest?
A) Marriage, sale, and gift
B) Marriage, divorce, and manumission
C) Sale, lease, and pledge
D) Marriage, will, and endowment

Key Takeaway
Contracts made in jest are void except for marriage, divorce, and manumission, which are fully effective regardless of jest.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Defects of Consent: Definition and Twofold Classification
Defining and classifying defects of consent in Hanafi legal theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a defect of consent in Hanafi legal theory and classify it into two categories based on the timing of the cause.

Core Idea
A defect of consent arises either at the will's inception or later, undermining its soundness and binding effect.

This passage introduces the concept of defects of consent (ʿuyūb al-riḍā), explaining that they are not complete absence of will but rather afflictions that taint the will. The author then classifies these defects into two types: those accompanying the birth of the will (e.g., coercion) and those supervening after contract formation (e.g., partial destruction of the subject matter before delivery). Understanding this classification is crucial for a mufti to determine when a contract may be voidable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the sentence that defines a defect of consent. It says the will is neither absent nor completely sound, but afflicted.
Focus on the phrase 'آفة أصابت إرادة العاقد' (an affliction that has affected the will).
2
Extract the two classification bases
Find the two categories: causes that accompany the birth of the will (internal/initial) and causes that supervene after contract formation (external/subsequent).
The passage uses the analogy of a child born deformed vs. later injury.
3
Apply the analogy
Explain how each type corresponds to the analogy: coercion is like a congenital defect; supervening destruction is like a later accident.
4
Determine the legal effect
Both types render the consent defective, so the contract is not sound, complete, and binding (i.e., voidable).
The passage explicitly states the consent cannot be considered 'ṣaḥīḥan kāmilan mulziman'.
5
Relate to iftaa practice
When a mufti receives a contract dispute, he must identify whether the alleged defect arose at formation (e.g., coercion, mistake) or after formation (e.g., partial destruction, change in circumstances).
This classification guides the mufti to the correct legal rule (e.g., option for defect, option for tafarruq al-ṣafqah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١/٣٤ - تمهيد:
الفصل الرابع والثلاثون
عيوب الرضا - الإكراه
نرید بشوائب الإرادة أو عيوب الرضا، كما سلفت الإشارة إليه، حالات لا يحكم معها بانتفاء الإرادة العقدية الحقيقية، كما لا يحكم معها بسلامة هذه الإرادة من كل شائبة؛ بل يوجد آفة أصابت إرادة العاقد، ولا يستطاع معها اعتبار رضاه صحيحاً كاملاً ملزماً.
وهذه الشوائب في نظر الفقه الإسلامي:
- إما أن تنشأ عن سبب قد رافق ولادة الإرادة، وأثر في تكوينها أو في توجيهها منذ البداية تأثيراً فاسداً كالوليد الذي يولد مشوَّه الخلقة، كما يرى في حالة الإكراه على التعاقد.
- وإما أن تنشأ أحياناً عن سبب طارىء غير ملحوظ عند التعاقد يؤدي إلى اختلال في تنفيذ العقد، فتعتبر الإرادة معه معيبة مؤوفة لأنه لم يتحقق التراضي على أساسه كما في حالة تلف بعض المبيع قبل التسليم فيختل رضا المشتري بالباقي وحده بحصته من الثمن، وهي الحالة التي سنراها تحت عنوان : تفرق الصفقة.
فالسبب الطارىء على الإرادة التي ولدت سليمة هو في مثل هذه الحال يرجع على الإرادة ويعيبها كالسبب المرافق لولادتها.
1/34 - Prelude: Chapter Thirty-Four: Defects of Consent – Coercion. By flaws of will or defects of consent, as previously indicated, we mean cases where neither the absence of true contractual will is ruled, nor is the will considered free from any flaw; rather, there is an affliction that has affected the will of the contracting party, and it is not possible to consider his consent as sound, complete, and binding. These flaws in the view of Islamic jurisprudence: - Either they arise from a cause that accompanied the birth of the will, and affected its formation or direction from the beginning in a corrupt manner, like a child born deformed, as seen in the case of coercion to contract. - Or they sometimes arise from a supervening cause not noticed at the time of contracting, leading to a disturbance in the execution of the contract, so the will is considered defective and impaired because mutual consent was not realized on that basis, as in the case of partial destruction of the sold item before delivery, causing the buyer's consent to the remainder alone for its portion of the price to be disturbed – a situation we will see under the title 'Division of the Transaction'. So the supervening cause on a will that was born sound, in such a case, goes back to the will and defects it like the cause that accompanied its birth.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is the preamble to the chapter on defects of consent (عيوب الرضا) and specifically coercion (الإكراه). The author defines what is meant by flaws of consent: they are situations where the will is not completely absent, nor completely sound. There is an 'affliction' (آفة) that taints the will, making it defective. The passage then classifies such defects into two types based on the timing of the cause: (1) Causes that accompany the birth of the will, influencing its formation from the outset—like coercion, which forces a person to contract against their genuine inclination. This is analogous to a child born with a deformity. (2) Causes that arise later, after the contract is made, but which undermine the basis of mutual consent, such as when part of the sold item is destroyed before delivery, making the buyer unwilling to accept the remainder for the full price. This later-occurring defect retroactively affects the validity of the consent. The author notes that this second type will be discussed under the topic 'Disruption of the Transaction' (تفرق الصفقة). In Hanafi law, both types render the contract voidable or invalid under certain conditions. This classification is essential for a mufti to understand when a contract may be challenged.

Key Terms
عيوب الرضا (ʿuyūb al-riḍā): Defects in consent that prevent the consent from being considered sound and complete.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion; forcing someone to contract against their will.
تفرق الصفقة (tafarruq al-ṣafqah): Division of the transaction; a situation where part of the subject matter is destroyed, affecting consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a defect of consent caused by a supervening (later) factor?
A) Coercion at the time of contracting
B) Mistake about the price
C) Partial destruction of the sold item before delivery
D) Mutual mistake about the subject matter

Key Takeaway
Defects of consent in Hanafi law are classified by the timing of the cause: formation defects (e.g., coercion) and supervening defects (e.g., partial destruction before delivery); both render the consent defective and the contract voidable.
